# Likkutei Sichos


## שמות

## Sicha 1

## 1

A. "And he said to the wicked one, 'Why would you strike your fellow?'" Rashi explains: "'Why would you strike' — even though he did not strike him, he is called wicked for raising his hand." The source for this is in Maseches Sanhedrin: Reish Lakish said, "Whoever raises his hand against his fellow, even if he did not strike him, is called wicked, as it is said, 'And he said to the wicked one, "Why would you strike your fellow?"' It does not say 'Why did you strike,' but rather 'Why would you strike' — even though he did not strike him, he is called wicked."

This ruling is codified in the Rambam, who states: "It is forbidden for a person to inflict injury, whether upon himself or upon his fellow... Anyone who strikes a kosher person of Israel... transgresses a negative commandment... It is forbidden even to raise one's hand against one's fellow, and anyone who raises his hand against his fellow, even if he did not strike him, is wicked."

The Rambam deviates from the language of the Gemara in two respects: (1) He adds, "It is forbidden even to raise one's hand against one's fellow." (2) Instead of "he is called wicked," he writes, "he is wicked."

The intent of the Rambam can be explained as follows: The statement of Reish Lakish in the Gemara could be interpreted as not being a practical halachic ruling, but rather an *aggadic* statement regarding the severity of the sin of striking one's fellow [as is seemingly implied by the statements brought in the Gemara there in continuation — "he is called a sinner... his hand shall be cut off... he has no atonement but burial"]. The Rambam emphasizes that this is not the case; rather, (1) raising one's hand against a fellow is a complete prohibition, and (2) the one who raises his hand "is wicked"—meaning, this is not merely a label, but a legal status of *rasha* applies to him, as stated in the Hagahos Maimonios here (in the name of the Ra'avan) that he is disqualified from testifying or taking an oath.

Based on this, we can explain why the Tur deviates from the Rambam's language, stating: "It is forbidden for a person to strike his fellow, and if he strikes him, he transgresses a negative commandment... And one who raises his hand against his fellow to strike him, even if he did not strike him, is called wicked." That is to say: (1) He omits the Rambam's emphasis that this is a prohibition, and (2) he uses the Gemara's language, "is called wicked" (rather than the Rambam's "he is wicked"), because the Tur holds that this is not a practical halachic ruling, and he brings it in the style of *aggadah* to convey the severity of the sin (just as he brings *aggadic* statements in many places regarding character traits, conduct, and various mitzvos).

## 2

[Seemingly, this is also the opinion of the Mechaber in the Shulchan Aruch, who copied (in Hilchos Chovel) the language of the Tur mentioned above (regarding the severity of raising a hand against one's fellow), yet he omitted in the Shulchan Aruch, Hilchos Eidus, the words of the Mordechai that one who raises his hand against his fellow is disqualified from testifying or taking an oath (even though in the Beis Yosef in Hilchos Eidus he copied the opinion of the Mordechai, as will be discussed in section B). This implies, seemingly, that for practical halacha, he did not adopt the view of the Mordechai].

B. From the simple reading of the Rambam, "It is forbidden even to raise one's hand against one's fellow"—unlike what he wrote in the previous halacha regarding one who strikes a person, "he transgresses a negative commandment"—it is implied that this is not a Torah-level prohibition, but only a Rabbinic prohibition (which they supported with the verse "And he said to the wicked one, 'Why would you strike your fellow?'"). This is also explicitly stated in the Sefer HaChinuch: "Our Sages, of blessed memory, prevented us even from gesturing to strike, and they said, 'Whoever raises his hand against his fellow to strike him is called wicked, as it is said, "And he said to the wicked one, 'Why would you strike your fellow?'"'" Thus, it is explicitly only a Rabbinic prohibition.

However, the Beis Yosef on the Tur there writes in explanation of the Mordechai's opinion that one who raises his hand against his fellow is disqualified from testifying, stating: "And if one who raises his hand against his fellow is not liable for lashes, we must say that one who transgresses a negative commandment for which there are no lashes is disqualified by Rabbinic decree. And even though from the words of the Rambam and our teacher it appears that one is not disqualified by Rabbinic decree except for one who transgresses the words of the Sages, it is implied that it is all the more so for one who transgresses Torah-level commandments for which there are no lashes."

Thus, the Beis Yosef interprets the opinion of the Mordechai as

## 3

being a Torah-level negative commandment (and this is also implied by the simple reading of his language, as he is in doubt whether he is liable for lashes or not—as he wrote, "And if one who raises... is not liable for lashes, we must say...").

It turns out there are three opinions regarding the interpretation of Reish Lakish's statement: (1) It was not said as a halachic ruling, but as *aggadah*. (2) It is a complete Rabbinic prohibition. (3) One transgresses a Torah-level negative commandment (to the point that one might think he is liable for lashes).

C. It seems we can explain the basis of this dispute as depending on the definition of the prohibition of "raising one's hand against one's fellow," which can be interpreted in two ways:

(1) Raising one's hand against a fellow is the beginning of the act of striking one's fellow. This is the novelty in the ruling that one who raises his hand is called wicked: that at the beginning of such an act of striking, he is already "called wicked."

(2) The prohibition is not due to the pain and damage caused to the fellow who is struck, but rather due to the raiser of the hand; the very act of raising one's hand against a fellow is a forbidden action.

That is to say: The prohibition of striking one's fellow, or even raising a hand against him (like all prohibitions between man and his fellow), has two components: (a) From the perspective of the recipient—the damage and pain to the fellow. (b) From the perspective of the person (the actor)—the bad conduct (character trait) of the raiser and the striker.

This is the distinction in the definition of the "wickedness" of one who "raises his hand against his fellow": According to the first approach, it is only because this action (raising the hand) is preparation and the beginning of the act of striking one's fellow; whereas according to the second approach, where the definition of "wickedness" is the base conduct (trait) of the raiser and the like, the raising of the hand itself is an evil act, and therefore, because of this conduct itself, he is called wicked.

It can be said that this is the reason for the dispute: whether the fact that one who raises his hand against his fellow is called wicked is merely a severe *aggadic* matter, or whether it is a halachic prohibition and the status of *rasha* (and disqualification from testimony and oaths) applies to him. By way of introduction:

It is a rule in the laws of the Torah (except for *avodah zarah*, etc.) that one is not punished for thoughts. Based on this, it is difficult to understand in our case: if the prohibition is "striking one's fellow," why is "one who raises his hand against his fellow, even if he did not strike him, called wicked"?

And even though here there was not only a thought, but also the act of raising the hand—still, there is no act of striking one's fellow.

It can be said that this is the reason for the opinion that being "called wicked" is not a halachic status of *rasha* (to disqualify him from testimony and oaths)—because a person is not disqualified from testimony and oaths by raising a hand.

But according to the Rambam, "he is wicked"—because he holds like the second approach mentioned above, that the very raising of the hand is a forbidden act, and therefore he has committed a forbidden deed.

However, based on this, the Beis Yosef's reasoning that one who raises his hand

## 5

the doubling of the language, that at the end of his statement he repeats, "even if he did not strike him (he is called wicked)"—is to emphasize that this prohibition is not related to the striking of one's fellow, but that raising a hand (to strike) in and of itself is the prohibition, "even though he did not (there is no continuation, he did not) strike him."

This is unlike the Midrashim, where the content of the matter of raising a hand against one's fellow is that this is (the beginning of the act of) striking one's fellow; therefore, they add, "raising his hand to strike his fellow," "one who stretches out his hand to his fellow to strike him."

[Based on this, it is well-understood why the Tur also adds this word—"and one who raises his hand against his fellow to strike him" (which is not in the Gemara as mentioned above)—for according to the Tur, the severity of raising one's hand against one's fellow is because it is the beginning of the act of striking one's fellow (even though, because it is only a beginning, the status of *rasha* does not apply to him, as mentioned above)].

However, even between the two Midrashim mentioned above, there is a fundamental difference:

In *Shemos Rabbah*, the emphasis is: "From here, that from the moment a person raises his hand to strike his fellow... he is called wicked." This refers to the same definition of the label of "wicked" that is caused by the act of striking one's fellow itself, and the novelty in the Midrash is that the beginning of this label is "from the moment a person raises his hand to strike his fellow," as if by raising his hand, he is already beginning to strike his fellow. This is like the opinion of the Beis Yosef (in his commentary on the Tur), that one who raises his hand against his fellow is part of the negative commandment of *chovel* (striking) one's fellow, as mentioned above.

Whereas in *Tanchuma* and *Bamidbar Rabbah*, which state simply, "one who stretches out his hand to his fellow to strike him, even if he did not strike him, is called wicked," and brings this detail together with three others who "are called wicked"—the intention is not for a halachic ruling of *rasha*, but as an *aggadic* matter, like the other three listed in the Midrash there who are called wicked, which were not brought as halacha to disqualify them from testimony and oaths. This is like the opinion of the Tur, as mentioned above.

E. One can add an explanation of the Rambam's opinion, that the very raising of the hand against one's fellow contains a prohibition:

The purpose of man's creation is "to serve his Creator" through the fulfillment of Torah and mitzvos, with every single limb according to its nature (as explained in detail in *Sefer Chareidim*—the division of mitzvos according to the limbs of man).

Now, "hand" denotes the concept of giving. It can be said that this is the primary purpose of the hand's creation—to use it for giving (for influence, *chesed*—just as He is involved in *gemilus chasadim*, so too you...); but

## 6

when a person "raises his hand against his fellow"—that instead of using the hand for the purpose of its creation, to benefit his fellow, he uses it in an opposite manner, to strike his fellow—and it should be noted that this is wickedness without any benefit or gain to the striker (unlike the benefit of a thief from his theft and the like, and in the language of the verse: "Men do not despise a thief if he steals to satisfy his soul when he is hungry")—then there is a severe sin between man and his Creator, for he is reversing the role of the hand's creation from one extreme to the other, as mentioned above.

[It should be added: Since most of the Torah's mitzvos are practical mitzvos, to be performed with a person's hand, it follows that the primary limb with which the (majority of) "service" to his Creator is fulfilled is specifically the human hand (unlike other limbs, which, although he must also serve his Creator with them, their primary use is in matters of preparing for a mitzvah, such as running to perform a mitzvah and the like). Thus, when he uses his hand in an opposite manner, there is a contradiction and opposition to the entire purpose of man's creation, "to serve his Creator."]

And this sin toward Hashem begins immediately upon raising the hand against one's fellow (even before the harm to the fellow), for with this raising, he is using his hand contrary to the intent of its creation.

It can be said that this matter is also hinted at in the source of the teaching—"And he said to the wicked one, 'Why would you strike your fellow?'":

The striker and the struck in that instance were Dathan and Abiram, who were "quarreling" with one another. Now, later at the end of our parsha, it is recounted that after the officers of the Children of Israel claimed to Pharaoh, "Why do you do this to your servants?" and Pharaoh answered, "Go, work, and straw will not be given to you, and you shall give the quota of bricks," Dathan and Abiram encountered Moses and Aaron, "And they said to them, 'May Hashem look upon you and judge, for you have made our scent abhorrent in the eyes of Pharaoh and in the eyes of his servants, to place a sword in their hand to kill us.'" It turns out that Dathan and Abiram were striving for the benefit of Israel (and especially according to the interpretation that they were among the officers of the Children of Israel, who cried out to Pharaoh (as mentioned above), "Why do you do this to your servants, etc.")—and nevertheless,

## 7

in this instance, instead of their striving for the benefit of Israel, they themselves, between one another, behaved in an opposite manner (contrary to their usual custom) and were quarreling with one another!

[Similar to the above regarding one who raises his hand against his fellow—that this movement of his hand is contrary to the intent of the creation of the hand itself.]

F. And more deeply, according to Chassidus, it can be said:

It has been explained elsewhere that since the Torah preceded the world by two thousand years, and before the world was created there was no concept of sin, etc.—it must be that in every matter and word in the Torah there is (also) an inner and spiritual interpretation, according to which everything is good.

Likewise, it must be regarding the prohibition of striking one's fellow in general, and specifically this matter of "one who raises his hand against his fellow... is called wicked"—that it can also be interpreted positively.

The explanation for this:

In addition to the fact that the matter of "you shall strike your fellow" as it is literally understood also exists in holiness, such as the "striking" of the physician that is required to heal a person, and furthermore—the mitzvah of *bris milah*, which comes about through "inflicting injury" and the drawing of blood, in such a way that the one circumcised has the status of a sick person, to the point that on the third day we desecrate the Shabbos for him, all of which is in a manner that it is a desired and good thing [and it is understood that already at the time of the beginning of this act (healing or *milah* and the like), similar to "one who raises... even if he did not strike," there is already the quality of the good itself]—

From the perspective of the *pnimiyus haTorah* regarding the interpretation of "raising his hand against his fellow," that this is from the perspective of the change effected in the reality of the hand, contrary to the purpose of its creation—it can be said that likewise, it also exists positively: that a person strives in a manner of changing his nature and his habit regarding the benefit of his fellow, to the point of "raising," that he raises his "hand" (his power of influence) above measurement and limitation, until he is in a state of "above his fellow"—above his fellow, because his influence of *chesed* is more than what is necessary for his fellow, to the point that he gives more than the capacity of his fellow.

(And to note the two ways of giving to one's fellow: "sufficient for his need," "to make him wealthy.")

And therefore "he is called wicked" (*rasha*)—from the expression "and in all that he turns he will succeed" (*yarshi'a*), which means "he will prevail", "he will succeed", meaning success not in the usual way, but above measurement and limitation.

And this is also the instruction for action from this halacha: in addition to the simple matter, the utmost caution regarding any matter of raising a hand against one's fellow, there is—from this halacha, a person must also learn an instruction at the other extreme—that his conduct should be the conduct of "one who raises his hand against his fellow" positively, to strive for the benefit of his fellow—"the hand that distributes *tzedakah*"—more than his nature and habit, to the point of raising and bringing out his hand (with his entire reality) from all measurements and limitations, "with all your might."

(From a Sicha of Shabbos Parshas Noach, 5748)

## Sicha 2

## 8

### Sicha 2

1. Regarding the verse "And Moshe feared and said, 'Indeed, the matter is known,'" the Tanchuma states that Moshe said, "There is lashon hara among you; how then are you worthy of redemption?"

We must understand this: We find in the Midrashim of our Sages that during the Egyptian exile, there were those among Bnei Yisrael who worshipped idols, and yet they were redeemed from Egypt. If so, why was Moshe so categorical that because "there is lashon hara among you, how then are you worthy of redemption"—as if the sin of lashon hara alone is enough to render them unworthy of redemption?

Our Sages spoke at great length regarding the severity of the prohibition of lashon hara, stating, "Whoever speaks lashon hara is as if he denied the fundamental principle [of faith]," and even that "Anyone who speaks lashon hara increases sins to the level of the three [cardinal] sins: idolatry, forbidden sexual relations, and bloodshed." Nevertheless, it is puzzling that the sin of idolatry in practice did not prevent their redemption, while the sin of lashon hara is an impediment to the redemption of Bnei Yisrael!

Furthermore, we find in our Sages that there is a severity regarding lashon hara even compared to idolatry in the context of victory in war—as it is stated in the Yerushalmi: "The generation of David were all righteous, but because they had informers (*diltorin*), they would go out to war and fall... The generation of Achav were idolaters, but because they did not have informers, they would go out to war and be victorious." But seemingly, we only learn from this that there is a unique quality in peace (and consequently, a deficiency in lashon hara and discord) regarding victory in war. But what is the connection to the redemption of Israel, to the extent that their redemption from the subjugation of the nations would depend on this?

## 9

2. Furthermore:
In Shemos Rabbah on this verse, the sin of lashon hara is highlighted even more as something that prevents redemption. It states: "Moshe was contemplating in his heart, saying, 'What have the Israelites sinned that they are subjugated by all the nations?' When he heard their words, he said, 'There is lashon hara among them; how can they be worthy of redemption?' Therefore he said, 'Indeed, the matter is known'—now I know for what matter they are being subjugated." This is even more explicit in Rashi on the Torah, where he first brings: "And its Midrash—he worried because he saw among the Israelites wicked informers, [and] he said, 'From now on, perhaps they are not worthy to be redeemed.'" And then (in the entry "Indeed, the matter is known"), he brings: "And its Midrash—the matter has become known to me, for I was wondering about it: what have the Israelites sinned [more] than all the seventy nations to be crushed with backbreaking labor? But I see that they are worthy of it."

According to this, the fact that there are "wicked informers" among the Israelites is a reason not (only) for them not being worthy of redemption, but (also) for them being "worthy of subjugation," "to be crushed with backbreaking labor" more than all the seventy nations. And it is puzzling: what is the severity of the sin of lashon hara regarding this matter—more than other sins?

And furthermore, and this is the main point: how is it possible to say that lashon hara existed among the Israelites more than among the seventy nations, such that Bnei Yisrael specifically were subjugated and not the seventy nations?

All of this is also in Rashi—(in the case at hand, the Midrash that resolves) the plain meaning of the verse (*peshuto shel mikra*).

Moreover, regarding the content of the Shemos Rabbah and Rashi, we must examine: if the sin of lashon hara is a reason to be worthy of subjugation and to be crushed with backbreaking labor, what is the novelty in the fact that "he said, 'From now on, perhaps they are not worthy to be redeemed'"—is this not self-evident?

Furthermore, it is not understood: in the words of Dasan and Aviram, there was not only the sin of lashon hara, but also the sin of informing (as they informed on Moshe to Pharaoh)—and the sin of informing is greater in its severity than the sin of lashon hara (and includes it). Why do our Sages emphasize that Moshe's fear was (only) because of the sin of lashon hara, and not because of the primary severity in their words—the sin of informing?

## 10

3. Now, in another place it is stated: "Rav Huna said... For four things the Israelites were redeemed from Egypt: they did not change their names, they did not change their language, they did not speak lashon hara, and there was not found among them one who was promiscuous."

Here, lashon hara is not listed as a separate, independent reason, but is included within the four things. Whereas according to the aforementioned Tanchuma, it is implied that the sin of lashon hara alone has the power to prevent them from redemption.

We must understand what the point of their disagreement is.
[And seemingly, it is forced to say that they disagree regarding the severity of the sin of lashon hara—that according to the Tanchuma, the sin of lashon hara "increases sins to the level of the three sins" (and therefore this sin alone is sufficient to prevent redemption); while Rav Huna, who says they were redeemed for four things (and lashon hara is only one of them), holds the view of the one who says that whoever speaks lashon hara is (only) as if he denied the fundamental principle.]

4. The core explanation for this can be said to be as follows:
It is known that at the time of the Exodus from Egypt, the birth of the Jewish people occurred, for until then, Bnei Yisrael were not in the category of a "nation" (even according to the plain meaning) in their own right. Only at the Exodus did they become a nation in their own right, as it is written, "To take for Himself a nation from the midst of a nation." This matter—their being a nation in their own right—effected unity within them, that they became one nation, meaning that they would be a single entity (*cheftza*) of a nation, and not merely a collection of seventy souls or twelve tribes and the like (from one lineage, etc.).

Therefore, in order for them to be worthy of redemption—that is, to be one nation in their own right—it is impossible for their conduct to be in a manner of division and discord, which is the opposite of being one nation.

And even though the matter of unity within a nation is not, seemingly, unique to the Jewish people—for this is also the case with all nations, that their being a nation depends on them uniting together, and without this, it is impossible to be a nation—nevertheless, for the Jewish people, the definition of unity and its necessity is in an entirely different manner (which is why the impediment to redemption due to informers is specific to the redemption of Israel).

5. This can be explained by prefacing what the Rambam writes at the beginning of the Laws of Idolatry, after describing the manner in which the Name of Hashem was publicized by Avraham and subsequently by Yitzchak and Yaakov: "And the matter continued to grow among the sons of Yaakov and those who joined them, and a nation was formed in the world that knew Hashem, until the days of the Israelites in Egypt lengthened, etc."

And it is difficult: if, while in Egypt (before the subjugation), the children of Yaakov were already a "nation"—what was renewed later at the Exodus regarding this matter, that it was then the "birth of the Jewish people" (as explained in many places, as above)?

It can be said that this is understood based on the continuation of the words...

## 13

"...and there was not found among them one who was promiscuous"—their content and matter is that by being distinguished in these virtues, Bnei Yisrael were separated as one nation in their own right—from the Egyptians.

And even though the emphasis in this statement of our Sages is not only on the negation of the deficiency that impairs their unity as one nation (that they did not... speak lashon hara, etc.), but also on the (positive) virtues in the action and conduct of each one individually—that each one did not change his name, etc.—nevertheless, the main point in this is that they are separated from the Egyptians as mentioned above. Therefore, lashon hara is also included among the four things, because the fact that "one did not inform on his fellow" refers to the Egyptians, who had the power to harm Bnei Yisrael.

This is unlike the Midrash on the verse here, where Moshe worried that perhaps they were not worthy of redemption. The intention there is from the perspective of a general deficiency in the "nation" due to the fact that there were "informers" among them (and not the deficiency of lashon hara as one of the four things)—because the concern here is not that they might inform on him to Pharaoh, but rather that they are in a state of division among themselves (which is the third matter mentioned above in section 6).

8. Based on the above, we can explain (inwardly) why Rashi divides the words of the Shemos Rabbah here—"Moshe was contemplating, etc."—into two entries (as mentioned above in section 2):
"And its Midrash—he worried, etc., [and] he said, 'From now on, perhaps they are not worthy to be redeemed'"; "And its Midrash—the matter has become known to me, for I was wondering about it: what have the Israelites sinned... to be crushed with backbreaking labor? But I see that they are worthy of it." And by prefacing:
The (inner) reason why "lashon hara and informers" harms only the "Jewish people" and not the other nations is understood based on what is known (and also in the plain meaning) that holiness is defined by unity [which is why it is said regarding Yaakov "a soul" (*nefesh*), in the singular—even though there were seventy], whereas the opposite side is in...


## וארא

## Sicha 2

### 28

A. At the beginning of the section regarding the plague of blood, it is written: "And Hashem said to Moshe, 'The heart of Pharaoh is *kaved* (heavy); he refuses to let the people go.'" Rashi quotes the word *kaved* and explains: "Its translation is *yakir* (heavy/weighty), and not *atikar* (made heavy/hardened), because it is a noun, like [the phrase] 'for the matter is too *kaved* (heavy) for you.'"

The reason Rashi found it necessary to rule out the interpretation of *atikar* (a past-tense verb meaning "it has already become heavy")—rather than simply providing the correct translation, *yakir*, as is his custom—is not to establish the correct version of the Targum. It is obvious that it is not the purpose of Rashi’s commentary on the Torah to clarify or establish versions of the Targum. Rather, it is because there is a *kas d’am* (a plausible initial assumption) in the plain meaning of the verse to interpret *kaved* here as a past-tense verb.

The explanation for this is as follows: In the preceding verse, it is written, "And Pharaoh’s heart was *vayechzak* (hardened/strengthened)," the translation of which is *v’itakaf* (similar to *atikar*). Therefore, there was room to interpret—as the Targum Yonasan, Rashbam, and Ibn Ezra did—that what is stated in the subsequent verse, "Pharaoh’s heart is *kaved*," is also in the same vein (like *vayechzak*), meaning *atikar*, a past-tense verb. For this reason, Rashi needed to rule out this interpretation: "Its translation is *yakir* and not *atikar* (and the reason is) because it is a noun, like 'for the matter is too *kaved* for you.'"

However, this itself requires explanation. In the words of the Re’em: Who told the translator that it is a noun and not a past-tense verb? If it is because we find "for the matter is too *kaved* for you" where it is a noun, we also find "a youth and an *zaken* (old man)," where it is a noun, yet we also find "and it was when Yitzchak was *zaken* (had aged)," which is a past-tense verb. Furthermore, the word *me’en* (refuses), which follows immediately after and is a past-tense verb, compels *kaved* to be a past-tense verb, because *me’en*, being a past-tense verb, indicates that his heart had already become heavy in the past.

The Gur Aryeh explains: "And what compelled the Targum to interpret it as a noun and not a past-tense verb is that one cannot say that 'Pharaoh’s heart is *kaved*' [means] he has already refused to let the people go, and therefore 'go to Pharaoh'—for perhaps even though Pharaoh’s heart had already been hardened, now it is not hardened."

However, the *Levush HaOrah* rejects the explanation of the Gur Aryeh (and upholds the difficulty raised by the Re’em), because in the first five plagues, the Ribono shel Olam wanted to make it known

## 29

that Pharaoh would not truly intend to repent. This is what "Hashem said to Moshe: 'You have already seen that Pharaoh’s heart was *kaved* and he refused to let the people go because his heart was already hardened. Therefore, so that people should not say, "Even though his heart was already hardened, perhaps now his heart has softened and he will repent and let them go," I do not want to bring the plague of blood upon him without a warning. Rather, go to Pharaoh, etc., and warn him. If his heart softens now, he will certainly say, "I will let the people go," and I will not need to bring the plague of blood upon him. But if his heart remains hardened, he is the one who has caused his own loss, and everyone will know that he is still defying Me, and it is just that I bring the plague of blood upon him.'"

According to this, the difficulty of the Re’em remains (as mentioned above): What is the necessity to interpret *kaved* as a noun, especially since it does not fit with the continuation of the verse, "*me’en* [refuses]... etc.," which is a past-tense verb?

We must therefore conclude that Rashi’s interpretation and insistence on the Targum’s version, " *yakir*... because it is a noun," is not based (solely) on the proof from the verse "for the matter is too *kaved* for you," but rather from the flow of the section itself. He merely uses the verse "for the matter is too *kaved* for you" as support for his interpretation (that *kaved* is a noun).

B. It can be said that Rashi’s intention here is to resolve a general question, seemingly, in this text:

Initially, it is recounted that the Ribono shel Olam said to Moshe, "Pharaoh’s heart is *kaved*; he refuses to let the people go," and afterwards, in the subsequent verses, the text continues to recount that the Ribono shel Olam told him, "Go to Pharaoh, etc., and say to him, 'Hashem... has sent me to you, saying: Let My people go, that they may serve Me... you have not listened until now... By this you shall know that I am Hashem: behold, I will strike, etc., upon the waters, etc., and they shall turn to blood.'" This means that in these words as a whole, there are two matters: (1) An announcement to Moshe—"Pharaoh’s heart is *kaved*, etc.," (2) The words of his mission to Pharaoh—"Go to Pharaoh... and say to him, etc."

This announcement is different from the announcements of the other plagues, in all of which the Ribono shel Olam began His words to Moshe immediately with the words of the mission to Pharaoh.

[Except for the plague of locusts, where there was also a special announcement as a preface to the words of the mission: "Come to Pharaoh, for I have hardened his heart, etc., in order that I might place these signs of Mine in his midst, and in order that you may recount, etc., what I have toyed with in Egypt, etc." (And as explained elsewhere, the reason for this announcement is that it explains why He commands Moshe to warn Pharaoh (as per the precision of Rashi's language, "Come to Pharaoh, and warn him") even though "I have hardened his heart, etc.," which is "in order that you may recount, etc., what I have toyed with in Egypt," as Rashi explains, "I mocked," meaning He makes a mockery of Pharaoh as if he has the choice to listen or not).]

From the very fact that this announcement of "Pharaoh’s heart is *kaved*; he refuses to let the people go" was said to Moshe only as a preface to his mission to Pharaoh (and not as part of the words he was to say to Pharaoh), it is proven that this matter is not (only) something that needs to be conveyed to Pharaoh, but that it also concerns the knowledge of Moshe himself, and also, that this is the truth of the matter: that the reason the plague of blood came upon Pharaoh is because "Pharaoh’s heart is *kaved*; he refuses to let the people go."

## 30

And it is puzzling: Above, in the account of sending Moshe to Pharaoh, it is said that the Ribono shel Olam told Moshe, "And I will harden Pharaoh’s heart, etc., and Pharaoh will not listen to you." From this, it is understood that when Moshe saw (after the sign of Aharon’s staff swallowing the others) that "Pharaoh’s heart was hardened and he did not listen to them," he certainly thought that this was because the Ribono shel Olam had fulfilled His promise and hardened his heart. Since this is so, how can it be said that "Pharaoh’s heart is *kaved*" is the cause for the punishment of the plague of blood—after all, it was the Ribono shel Olam who hardened his heart?

That Moshe was commanded to convey this to Pharaoh and tell him that he is being punished because "you have not listened until now"—this is understood, because the Ribono shel Olam wanted Pharaoh to eventually let Bnei Yisrael go of his own free will through word after word.

[And similarly, one should not ask: How is it truly possible for punishment to come to Pharaoh for his lack of desire to let Bnei Yisrael leave his land, since the cause for this was (not Pharaoh’s desire, but) that the Ribono shel Olam strengthened his heart? For this question has already been answered by Rashi above on the verse "And I will harden," where he writes: "After he acted wickedly and defied Me, and it is revealed before Me that there is no satisfaction in the nations of the world to give a whole heart to repent, it is better for Me that his heart be hardened in order to multiply My signs through him and that you may recognize My power." That is, the fact that the Ribono shel Olam hardened his heart was a punishment for "that he acted wickedly and defied Me."]

But from the fact that this announcement was also for Moshe himself, as mentioned above, it must be that he too needs to know (and that this is the truth of the matter)—that since "Pharaoh’s heart is *kaved*," he truly deserves punishment. And it is puzzling, as above, how this fits with the words of the Ribono shel Olam to him previously, "And I will harden Pharaoh’s heart, etc."?

C. Seemingly, one could answer based on Rashi’s commentary on the verse "And I will harden": "And nevertheless, in the first five plagues it is not said 'And Hashem hardened Pharaoh’s heart,' but rather 'Pharaoh’s heart was hardened,'" which from the superficial reading of the language implies that in the first five plagues, Hashem did not harden the heart of the king of Egypt, but rather he hardened it himself.

But when you examine it properly, it is impossible to answer this way, because in Rashi’s commentary it is only stated that (in the text) "it is not said 'And Hashem hardened Pharaoh’s heart'"; but in the words of the Ribono shel Olam to Moshe, we do not find that He informed him that in the first five plagues He would not harden his heart (but only in the last five).

Especially if one says, as the commentators explain, that even in the first five plagues Hashem hardened Pharaoh’s heart, but in the text it is not written "And Hashem hardened Pharaoh’s heart," but rather "Pharaoh’s heart was hardened" [as per the precision of Rashi’s language: "And nevertheless, in the first five plagues it is not said 'And Hashem hardened Pharaoh’s heart,' but rather 'Pharaoh’s heart was hardened'"—and he did not write in simple language, "In the first five plagues Hashem did not harden Pharaoh’s heart" (and the like)].

## 31

And as explained in the commentaries, "In the first five plagues, the Ribono shel Olam orchestrated actions that his heart became hardened, such as... in the plague of blood, where they said, 'You do this through sorcery.' And this is 'Pharaoh’s heart was hardened,' but 'And Hashem hardened' means that the Ribono shel Olam hardened him explicitly, and not through actions and causes."

And the difficulty returns to its place—since Moshe heard "And I will harden Pharaoh’s heart, etc.," how is it possible, consistent with this, to inform him that "Pharaoh’s heart is *kaved*" is the reason for the punishment of the plague of blood?

D. To resolve this question, Rashi explains *kaved*—"Its translation is *yakir* and not *atikar*, because it is a noun"—with which Rashi intends to emphasize clearly that the intention of the Ribono shel Olam in "Pharaoh’s heart is *kaved*" is to inform him that this is not (only) because He, may He be blessed, hardened his heart (in which case there is no room to bring this as a reason for Pharaoh’s punishment), but rather it refers to the state of Pharaoh’s heart from its own side. That he "refused to let the people go" was not (only) because the Ribono shel Olam hardened his heart, but that (also) he, from his own side, made his heart heavy not to let Bnei Yisrael go, and therefore he is worthy of being punished for this.

[Similar to the answer in the *sefarim* to the question—how did the Ribono shel Olam punish the Egyptians for working Bnei Yisrael with rigor, when the Ribono shel Olam had decreed upon them "and they shall serve them and afflict them"?—that the fact that the Egyptians worked, etc., and afflicted Bnei Yisrael was not to fulfill the decree of the Ribono shel Olam, but because of the wickedness of their hearts, and therefore they were punished for it.]

Therefore, Rashi explains that *kaved* means "*yakir* and not *atikar*"—because through this it is clearer that the intention is to the heaviness of Pharaoh’s heart from its own side. If the intention of the word *kaved* were a "past-tense verb," meaning that there was an act of hardening here, there is no emphasis that Pharaoh from his own side hardened his heart. On the contrary—simply, it would have to refer to the act of hardening which is the deed of the Ribono shel Olam, explicitly mentioned above in the words of the Ribono shel Olam to Moshe, "And I will harden, etc." (and then there is no room to say that this matter would be a preface and reason for Pharaoh’s punishment). Therefore, Rashi explains that *kaved* means "*yakir* and not *atikar*," a "noun" describing the state of Pharaoh’s heart—according to which it is reasonable to say that the intention is that Pharaoh’s heart was (essentially) in a state of *kaved*, that he had a heavy heart essentially, and therefore he deserves punishment for not letting Bnei Yisrael go, as mentioned above.

E. The *pnimiyus* (inner dimension) of Torah in Rashi:

Heaviness of the heart, in simple terms, means stubbornness of the heart—that it is not affected by anything, and even when his intellect shows him that he should act in the opposite way, and even the emotion in his heart tends to the other side—he remains stubborn not to change his conduct. And this is the essence of the "*kelipah*" of Pharaoh—*Paroh* (Pharaoh) is the letters of *ha-oref* (the neck), which indicates stubbornness, "stiff-necked."

Based on this, one can explain the depth of the intention of the Midrash, which explains " *kaved* (Pharaoh’s heart)"—that Pharaoh’s heart became like (the part of the body called) the liver, "Just as the liver is angry, so too this one’s heart became heavy": It is known that there are three rulers in the body, the brain, the heart, and the liver, corresponding to the three parts (faculties) of the soul—the intellect (in the brain), the emotions (in the heart), and action (in the liver). And this is the connection between the heaviness of the heart and the "liver" in the body—because the matter of stubbornness belongs to the faculty of action of the person (which is under the control of the "liver"), that even when he has no explanation (in the intellect in the head) and no emotion (in the emotions in the heart)—he does so only because of his own stubbornness.

And in this, Rashi adds—"*kaved*... *yakir* and not *atikar*"—because the stubbornness itself can be in two ways: (a) The conduct in a manner of stubbornness, stubbornness in action—"*atikar*" (a verb form); (b) This conduct permeates his entire existence, until he becomes a stubborn person essentially, and this is his noun/adjective—"*yakir*." That is to say: It is not only a matter of conduct of stubbornness, but that the source of the heaviness in the soul is revealed in him, and by way of consequence, every matter for him is in a manner of stubbornness. And so it was with Pharaoh, that the meaning is not that he had to overcome himself in order to be stubborn against the Ribono shel Olam, but that he was "*kaved*" (stubborn) essentially.

F. The plagues that the Ribono shel Olam brought upon Pharaoh, their inner intention was to break the *kelipah* of Pharaoh, and this was done specifically through Moshe Rabbeinu, as it is said, "See, I have made you a god to Pharaoh," and Rashi explains, "A judge and a ruler to rule over him with plagues and afflictions."

But it is known that in order to nullify a bad trait and the like, it must be "in its kind and its likeness"—in a trait of its likeness on the side of *kedushah*. For "this opposite that, Hashem has made," that every bad trait is "opposite" its likeness on the side of *kedushah*. And so it is in the matter of the "*kaved*," that opposite the "*kaved*" of *kelipah*, there is the "*kaved*" of *kedushah*, which is the matter of accepting the yoke of the Kingdom of Heaven that is above reason, knowledge, and emotion. For in service that is according to reason and knowledge, and likewise according to the emotions in the heart, there are changes from time to time, as is seen by sense that a person does not always stand on the same level in comprehension, and certainly not in the emotion of his heart in the love and fear of Hashem; whereas the service of accepting the yoke, the "*kaved*" of *kedushah*, which is in a manner of stubbornness (heaviness), is "far from changes."

From this it is understood in our context, that in order for Moshe Rabbeinu to be able to "rule" and break the *kelipah* of Pharaoh, he needed to awaken in his soul the "*kaved*" of *kedushah*.

And it can be said that this is the hint and the inner explanation in the words of Rashi here, "*kaved*—its translation is *yakir* and not *atikar*": The "translation" of Pharaoh’s *kaved*, meaning, the matter of the "*kaved*" as it is explained in *kedushah*, its meaning is "*yakir*," and this is the matter of the


## בא

## Sicha 1

## 46

### Sicha 1

A. Regarding the story of the plague of darkness, the verse states: "Moshe stretched out his hand... and there was thick darkness in all the land of Egypt for three days. They did not see one another, and no one rose from his place for three days; but for all the Children of Israel there was light in their dwellings." The Midrash Rabbah states: "During the three days of darkness, the Ribono shel Olam gave the people favor in the eyes of the Egyptians, and they lent to them. The Israelites would enter the homes of the Egyptians and see their silver vessels, gold vessels, and garments. If they said, 'We have nothing to lend you,' the Israelites would say to them, 'It is in such-and-such a place.' At that moment, the Egyptians would say, 'If these people wanted to deceive us, they would have taken them during the days of darkness, and we would not have felt it, for they had already seen them. Since they did not touch them without our knowledge, they will not steal them.' Thus, they would lend to them, to fulfill what is said: 'And afterwards they shall go out with great wealth.' This is what is meant by 'but for all the Children of Israel there was light,' etc. It does not say 'in the land of Goshen,' but 'in their dwellings' — meaning that wherever a Jew entered, light entered and illuminated for him what was in the barrels, chests, and hidden caches."

Similarly, it is stated (briefly) in Tanchuma: "And so in Egypt, the cloud illuminated for the Israelites and darkened for the Egyptians, as it is said, 'They did not see,' etc., 'but for all the Children of Israel there was light.' It illuminated for the Israelites and showed them all the silver and gold vessels, garments, and all the good of Egypt; what was in the chests, barrels, and hidden caches was shown to them. They would ask, and they would give to them against their will, for the Israelites would say to them, 'Here you have such-and-such a vessel in such-and-such a place.'"

The essence of this Midrash is brought briefly in Rashi: "Why did He bring darkness upon them? Because they... And furthermore, the Israelites searched and saw their vessels, and when they left and asked of them, and they said, 'We have nothing in our possession,' he would say to him, 'I saw it in your house, and it is in such-and-such a place.'"

Now, on the face of it, it appears that Rashi’s intent is to interpret the verse as the Midrash does—that the fact that Bnei Yisrael saw the Egyptians' vessels

## 47

is the interpretation of the verse, "but for all the Children of Israel there was light in their dwellings." 

However, it is a great stretch to interpret Rashi’s intent this way, because:
1) The aforementioned precision in the Midrash—"It does not say 'in the land of Goshen,' but 'in their dwellings'—that wherever a Jew entered, light entered"—is not the simple meaning of "in their dwellings" (on the contrary, it is the opposite of the simple meaning). If Rashi held this view, he should have stated it explicitly.
2) Most importantly: Rashi wrote his commentary on the words (in verse 22), "and there was thick darkness... for three days," as the reason for the plague of darkness—"Why did He bring darkness upon them?"—and he did not mention at all in his commentary the words (from the subsequent verse, verse 23), "but for all the Children of Israel there was light in their dwellings."

This implies that according to Rashi, there were two distinct matters here: a) "but for all the Children of Israel there was light in their dwellings"—which refers (as the simple meaning of the language suggests) to the homes of the Israelites (in the land of Goshen). b) The fact that Bnei Yisrael "saw their vessels" in the homes of the Egyptians—and this matter is learned (not from the words "but for all the Children of Israel there was light in their dwellings," but rather) from the very nature of the plague of darkness, as this was the purpose of the plague, so that Bnei Yisrael could see their vessels and ask for them later from the Egyptians at the time of their departure from Egypt.

B. According to the above, we learn that there is a fundamental difference between the Midrash and Rashi in the interpretation of the verses here:
According to the Midrash, the statement "but for all the Children of Israel there was light in their dwellings" is also part of the plague of darkness, because the plague included two details: (a) darkness (miraculous) for the Egyptians, (b) light (miraculous) for the Israelites, "that wherever a Jew entered, light entered and illuminated for him," etc.

Whereas according to Rashi, there was only one miracle here—the darkness that the Ribono shel Olam brought upon the Egyptians. The reason He brought this plague upon them was for the sake of Bnei Yisrael, for through the darkness, the ability was given to Bnei Yisrael to search in the homes of the Egyptians and see the location of their vessels, but there was no additional miracle that Bnei Yisrael could see what was inside the Egyptians' homes.
[And that which the verse continues later, "but for all the Children of Israel there was light in their dwellings," is a separate matter, which comes to emphasize that the plague was only in Egypt and not in the place of the Israelites' dwellings in the land of Goshen. That is to say: the fact that it did not become dark for the Israelites in the Egyptian's house is part of the plague of darkness, because that is the intent and goal of the plague; whereas the intent of the statement "but for all the Children of Israel there was light in their dwellings" is, on the contrary, to inform us that there was no trace of the darkness in the dwellings of the Israelites. Similar to what is said regarding the plague of hail: "only in the land of Goshen, where the Children of Israel were, there was no hail," even though seemingly it is simple without this, for the plague was only upon Egypt.]

This difference between the Midrash and Rashi is emphasized and precise in the changes of language between them:

## 48

In the Midrash, the language is that the light was "illuminating for the Israelites and showing them... what was in the chests, barrels, and hidden caches," whereas in Rashi: (a) it is not mentioned at all that there was any "light" here (he only mentioned the matter of Bnei Yisrael seeing, "they saw their vessels"), (b) he specifies that Bnei Yisrael "searched"—unlike the Midrash, where the light "showed them," (c) he omits that Bnei Yisrael saw the vessels that were "in the chests, barrels, and hidden caches."

And according to the above, the reason for the differences in the simple meaning is understood:
According to the Midrash, that "but for all the Children of Israel there was light in their dwellings" is part of the miracle, then it was a special and miraculous light that was "showing them... what was in the chests, barrels, and hidden caches." For this, natural light is not sufficient to see through it what is inside vessels and (all the more so) in hidden caches; rather, it was a special and miraculous light. 

Whereas according to the simple meaning of the verse (the path of Rashi in his commentary on the Torah), there was no miraculous light here, only that the darkness did not darken for Bnei Yisrael, and consequently they were able to see what was in the Egyptians' homes (with regular, natural light). Therefore, they needed to search, etc., until they saw the Egyptians' vessels (and based on this, it can be said that it is possible Bnei Yisrael did not find all the vessels that were hidden in the caches, etc.).

[And based on this, another difference between the Midrash and Rashi can be explained: In Rashi, it is explicit that the fact that Bnei Yisrael would enter the Egyptians' homes and see their vessels was (one of) the reasons for the plague of darkness, whereas from the language of the Midrash, it is implied that this is not the reason for the plague of darkness, but an additional thing that occurred during the time of the plague of darkness. And according to the above, the reason can be explained: because according to the Midrash, which holds there was a miraculous light that showed the Israelites all the Egyptians' vessels, there is no need (so much) to bring darkness upon them, for even if there were light for the Egyptians, the Israelites would see the Egyptians' vessels through this miraculous light; whereas according to Rashi, who holds there was no miraculous light, the possibility to see the Egyptians' vessels was only due to the darkness that the Ribono shel Olam brought upon the Egyptians.]

C. And we must explain the reason for Rashi, who deviated from the Midrash’s interpretation, by prefacing:
It is known that in fulfilling the command of the Ribono shel Olam, one must strive that the fulfillment be in a natural way, not through a miracle—meaning, both that the object with which the mitzvah is performed should be a natural object and not a miraculous creation, and that the act of fulfilling the mitzvah be done in a natural way.

And more than this is explained in other places at length, that this includes not only the fulfillment of the mitzvah itself, but

## 49

also the preparation for the mitzvah, that the preparation also needs to be natural. As we learn from the well-known story regarding the Kiddush Levanah of the Alter Rebbe, who asked the official in charge of the ship to stop it so that he could sanctify the moon while standing and with a settled mind. When the official refused, the Alter Rebbe stopped the ship miraculously, but he did not sanctify the moon then until the official agreed to stop it himself—and one of the reasons for this is, because he wanted the preparation for the fulfillment of the mitzvah to also be in a natural way and not through a miraculous matter. 

And based on this, we can explain why Rashi did not interpret as the Midrash does, that Bnei Yisrael’s seeing the Egyptians' vessels was through a special miracle (through light that was not according to nature)—because according to that interpretation, the intent of the plague of darkness was so that the Israelites could fulfill the command of Hashem, "and let them ask, every man of his neighbor and every woman of her neighbor, silver vessels and gold vessels," until it would be "and you shall empty out Egypt"—"Teroikinnun." 

According to this, it turns out that the preparation for the fulfillment of this command was not according to nature, but in a miraculous way, because only through this miraculous light did they see the silver and gold vessels of the Egyptians and what was hidden, etc., and only through this did they fulfill the command to ask them for all their vessels that were in the caches, etc.

Therefore, Rashi changes and interprets that the fact that they saw their vessels was not through a special light and in a miraculous way, but according to nature—that the Israelites searched in the Egyptians' homes and saw their vessels with natural light—so that the preparation for the mitzvah would also be fulfilled in a natural way.

[And even though the entire essence of the miracle of the plague of darkness was for the sake of Bnei Yisrael being able to see the Egyptians' vessels—this is only in the category of removing the obstacle, or at most in the manner of a cause and inducement, and not a preparation and qualification for the mitzvah, which was specifically in that Bnei Yisrael searched and saw their vessels, and in such a case—this is not "not according to nature."

And this matter can also be learned from the aforementioned story of the Alter Rebbe, for seemingly, the fact that the official agreed to stop the ship—was not because of himself (truly), but because he saw that it was in the Alter Rebbe's power to stop the ship himself in a miraculous way.
However, that is only a mere inducement; the preparation itself, the stopping of the ship for the sake of saying the blessing of Kiddush Levanah, was done by the official himself and not in a miraculous way.]

D. However, the fact that the Midrash is not concerned with this, and interprets that there was a special and miraculous light, can be explained because it holds that in this case, it did not have to be so—and for several reasons:
a) The condition that the fulfillment of mitzvos must be in a natural way is primarily for mitzvos after Matan Torah [for this is a primary matter in Matan Torah, that the mitzvos should act within the physicality of the world, in the language of the Midrash, "the upper ones descended to the lower ones," and therefore most of the mitzvos are in physical things, and in that itself, specifically in natural ways, as explained in other places]—whereas in this case, it is speaking before Matan Torah.
b) The content of the matter of "and let them ask, every man of his neighbor," etc., is not (so much) in the category of a "mitzvah" and an obligation (of Bnei Yisrael), but on the contrary, it is the receiving of a reward (from Above), that through this the promise of the Ribono shel Olam was fulfilled, "and afterwards they shall go out with great wealth." It is just that the Ribono shel Olam clothed this payment of reward in natural ways, by Bnei Yisrael asking the Egyptians for silver vessels, etc., but the content of the matter is—receiving a reward from Above.

[And therefore we find that the entirety of the matter of "and let them ask, every man of his neighbor," etc., was not so dependent on the hands of the Israelites, but came in a miraculous way from Above, as is explicit at the beginning of the matter, "And I will give this people favor in the eyes of the Egyptians," and as it is said in the verse afterwards that it was in practice, "And Hashem gave the people favor in the eyes of the Egyptians," "And Hashem gave the people favor in the eyes of the Egyptians, and they lent them."]

It is understood that in the fulfillment of such a command, we do not mind if the preparation is done through a miracle.
[And it should be added, that in this case it was necessary (according to the Midrash) for the miracle to illuminate the "hidden caches"—because the purpose and goal of the command "and let them ask," etc., was (not only that Bnei Yisrael would go out with "great wealth," but furthermore)—"and you shall empty out Egypt," "Teroikinnun."
Therefore, the miracle of "but for all the Children of Israel there was light" was necessary to illuminate the hidden caches in the Egyptians' homes, for specifically through this miraculous light did they have the possibility to see everything that was hidden of silver and gold vessels among the Egyptians to fulfill what is said, "and you shall empty out Egypt" (whereas if they had only seen the vessels outside and by searching in the Egyptians' homes, they would not have been able to fulfill this command to empty out Egypt).]

E. According to this, we can explain (according to the inner dimension of the matters) also the difference between the Midrash and Rashi in the interpretation of what is written, "but for all the Children of Israel there was light in their dwellings"—whether this is also part of the plague of darkness or not:
It is stated in the sefarim that the "great wealth" that Bnei Yisrael took out of Egypt also hints at the sparks of holiness that were found in the land (and matters) of Egypt, for in that Bnei Yisrael took out the silver vessels, etc., of Egypt, they took out the sparks of holiness (that were in them) from the evil of Egypt and returned them to the domain of holiness.

And the content of this avodah (of "birur ha-nitzotzos") is a primary matter in the avodah of every single one of Yisrael, for in addition to the obligation of Torah study and the avodah of tefillah, which are primarily within the person himself, and in that itself—in his spirituality (the understanding of Torah in his intellect and the emotion of the heart in tefillah)—there must also be the fulfillment of mitzvos (most of which are in physical things), and also the doing of his optional matters for the sake of Heaven, for through this he draws holiness into the physicality of the world and elevates the sparks of holiness that are in physical things to the domain of holiness (as explained at length in the Tanya).

## 51

And it can be said that according to Rashi, this is the difference between the two matters in the verse—(a) the essence of the plague of darkness, which gave the possibility for Bnei Yisrael to enter the Egyptians' homes and see their vessels, (b) "but for all the Children of Israel there was light in their dwellings"—which correspond to the two types of avodah mentioned above: the avodah of Torah and tefillah, through which the light of holiness shines "in their dwellings" (in his soul and spirituality), and the avodah of refining the world, to take out the sparks of holiness that are in physical things and elevate them to holiness.

According to the path of the simple meaning—that is, as things appear simply and externally—they are two different types of avodah, and perhaps even opposite types.

However, in the Midrash—the aggadah in the Torah, that "most of the secrets of the Torah... are hidden in the aggados" —it interprets that even this, that "but for all the Children of Israel there was light in their dwellings," is part of the plague of darkness, because from the perspective of the inner intent, these two types of avodah are not different matters, but they are related and connected to one another, for even the avodah of Torah and tefillah, its purpose is for the sake of the avodah of refining and purifying the world (as explained in the Tanya, that the soul did not descend below to fix itself, but to fix the body and its animal soul and its portion in the world).

And this is the inner content in the words of the Midrash, that even this, that "for all the Children of Israel there was light in their dwellings," is part of the plague of darkness, and this is because even the light that shines for the Israelites when they are "in their dwellings," which is the light that shines in their soul through Torah and tefillah, they must draw and bring into the "homes of the Egyptians" (the darkness of the world), so that through it they can take out from there the sparks of holiness that are found there and return them to the domain of holiness.

F. And more deeply, it can be said, according to the "wine of Torah" in Rashi—that the fact that according to Rashi the conclusion "but for all the Children of Israel there was light in their dwellings" is not part of the plague of darkness, is because therein is hinted an even higher matter, which comes after the completion of the avodah of refining and purifying the world.
For it is explained in several places that the descent of the soul and its avodah below is a necessity for its ascent, that after the completion of its avodah in refining and purifying the world, the soul ascends to a higher level than it was before its descent, until it merits "to be absorbed in the body of the King." 

And this is the inner interpretation of the verse, that after the completion of the avodah in refining and purifying the world (which is hinted at in the plague of darkness, through which Bnei Yisrael took out the wealth of Egypt and brought it into the domain of holiness)—they merit "but for all the Children of Israel there was light in their dwellings," that the soul comes to its dwelling and its true place, Yisrael and the Kudsha Brich Hu are all one, where there is no place at all for the reality of Egypt and the matters of Egypt (even not as they are refined and elevated to holiness), but only (in the language of the Zohar) Yisrael and the King alone.

G. And from this we can also learn great encouragement in our avodah now, in this our final exile:
Even though we are in a time of the "darkness of exile," and in that itself—a double and redoubled darkness of the footsteps of Moshiach, the Ribono shel Olam causes causes, and even to ways that are above nature, so that Bnei Yisrael can perform their avodah without any obstacles and delays.

And even though all the aforementioned matters must be in natural ways—this is regarding the fulfillment of the mitzvah from the side of Bnei Yisrael, but regarding the situation (in the world) required so that Yisrael can perform their avodah in its fullness, there are no limitations, and the Ribono shel Olam does even open miracles to enable Yisrael to perform their avodah in natural ways [as in the plague of darkness, where the Ribono shel Olam performed an open miracle and brought miraculous darkness upon the Egyptians, so that Bnei Yisrael could search and find the vessels in a natural way (from their side); and similarly in the aforementioned story of the Alter Rebbe, who acted through a miracle, that the official himself would want to stop the ship].

And all the more so according to the interpretation of the Midrash, that there was also a miraculous light here—that certainly this matter is also not just a one-time thing that happened in the redemption of Egypt, but a semblance of this is also in the redemption from this final exile (which is "as in the days of your coming out of the land of Egypt"), that even while we are in the "land of Egypt" (before the coming of the redemption), a light that is above nature shines, and in the language of the verse (brought in the continuation of the matter in the aforementioned Midrash), that even when "darkness shall cover the earth and thick darkness the nations (nevertheless) and upon you Hashem shall shine," etc.

And more than this, that this light gives the strength to Bnei Yisrael to transform into holiness even the matters that are in the darkness of the world, which externally appear to be things that conceal, hide, and prevent the person from fulfilling the command of the Ribono shel Olam,
until they themselves assist and help Bnei Yisrael to fulfill the commands of Hashem, similar to what Pharaoh himself assisted and helped Bnei Yisrael to leave the exile of Egypt.

And no verse departs from its simple meaning, and this is the main thing, that at the end of the time of exile there is "light in their dwellings" of the entirety of Yisrael, and in each and every one of Yisrael, simply in physicality and in spirituality, that each one of Bnei Yisrael has expansion and rest in all his matters,
and they prepare themselves with joy and goodness of heart to receive the face of Moshiach Tzidkeinu, with our youth and our elders, with our sons and our daughters, and their silver and their gold with them, simply and also in the spirituality of the matters, very soon, in the immediate future.

## Sicha 2

### 53

1. We learned in Maseches Pesachim that the *chagigah* brought with the *pesach* "is eaten for two days and one night." The Gemara there states: The Mishnah does not follow Ben Teima, for it is taught that Ben Teima says the *chagigah* brought with the *pesach* is like the *pesach* and is only eaten for a day and a night, etc. What is the reason for Ben Teima? [It is written,] "And there shall not remain until morning the sacrifice of the festival of the *pesach*." The "sacrifice of the festival" refers to the *chagigah*, and the "pesach" is to be taken literally, and the Merciful One said it shall not remain.

The Gemara there continues: A question was raised for Ben Teima: Is it eaten roasted, or is it not eaten roasted? Did the Merciful One equate it to the *pesach* only regarding the prohibition of leaving it overnight (*linah*), but not regarding roasting? Or perhaps there is no difference? Come and hear: "This night, all of it is roasted", and Rav Chisda said this is the opinion of Ben Teima. We may infer from this [that it is eaten roasted].

The Rambam writes in *Hilchos Chametz U’Matzah* (in the text of "Mah Nishtanah"): "On all other nights we eat, etc., and this night, all of it is roasted." The *Lechem Mishneh* raises a difficulty: It is puzzling that our Master [the Rambam] rules in *Hilchos Korban Pesach* that the *chagigah* is eaten for two days and one night, not like Ben Teima. If so, for us, it is certainly not eaten roasted. And we cannot say "this night, all of it is roasted," for the Gemara stated that this is according to the opinion of Ben Teima, who holds that there is an *hekash* (a scriptural analogy) and that the *chagigah* is eaten roasted. But for us, since the *chagigah* is not eaten roasted, we cannot say "all of it is roasted," for there is the *chagigah* [which is not roasted].

The *Tzelach* answers that the Rambam holds that even according to the Sages, the *chagigah* is eaten roasted. Their reason is that we find that Scripture calls it a *pesach*, as the Rambam cites : "Regarding the *chagigah* of the fourteenth it is said : 'And you shall slaughter the *pesach* to Hashem, your G-d, sheep and cattle.'" However, the Sages hold that it is not equated to the *pesach* in all respects, but only regarding roasting (which is a matter of eating).

2. Now, regarding the reasoning behind the Sages' view that the *chagigah* is equated to the *pesach* only for roasting and not for all matters of eating (such as *linah*, for "the warning against *linah* is a command regarding eating") — one could seemingly suggest:

The reason the *chagigah* is brought with the *pesach* is "so that the *pesach* be eaten while one is satiated". Thus, the *chagigah* serves as a secondary element and a preparation for the *pesach*, and therefore the *chagigah* is equated to the *pesach*. However, for this very reason, only this eating of the *chagigah*—which comes before (and as a preface to) the eating of the *pesach*—is equated to the *pesach* and is dragged along after the eating of the *pesach*. Therefore, it is eaten roasted like it. This is not the case after the completion of the eating of the *pesach*, when the *chagigah* is no longer secondary to the *pesach* and is not equated to it.

Based on this, one could seemingly say that the Sages and Ben Teima disagree on the definition of the law that "the *chagigah* brought with the *pesach* is eaten first so that the *pesach* be eaten while one is satiated":

According to Ben Teima, the statement that through the *chagigah* the "pesach is eaten while one is satiated" is a reason not only for eating the *chagigah* before the *pesach*, but also for the entire essence of bringing the *chagigah* on the fourteenth. The reason for bringing the *chagigah* on the fourteenth is solely so that the *pesach* be eaten while one is satiated. It follows that the entire essence of the sacrifice is merely a preparation for the *pesach*. Therefore, Ben Teima holds that in all aspects of the sacrifice, it is equated to the *pesach* ("all matters of the *pesach* apply to the *chagigah* through that *hekash*").

But according to the Sages, the reason "so that the *pesach* be eaten while one is satiated" applies (as the simple reading of the *baraita* suggests) only to the precedence of eating the *chagigah* before the *pesach*, but not to the actual offering of the *chagigah*. Therefore, this *chagigah* has two legal statuses: the sacrifice itself bears the name *chagigah* and follows the laws of *chagigah*; and only in the act of its eating—and within that, only in the eating that serves as a preface to the eating of the *pesach*—does it have the status of a preparation for the act of eating the *pesach*. Regarding this, it is equated to the *pesach*, and therefore the laws of eating the *pesach* (that it must be roasted) apply to the *chagigah* at that time.

However, it is difficult to explain the dispute between Ben Teima and the Sages in this way, because the simple reading of the Gemara —"And in a case where there was little [meat] (when the *pesach* was insufficient for the members of the group), what is the reason? It comes as taught: the *chagigah* brought with the *pesach* is eaten first so that the *pesach* be eaten while one is satiated"—implies that the entire essence of bringing the *chagigah* is only so that the *pesach* be eaten while one is satiated (and this is also explicitly stated in *Tosafos* and other *Rishonim*, that the *chagigah* of the fourteenth is not an obligation, meaning it "is brought only so that the *pesach* be eaten while one is satiated"). If so, even according to the Sages, the entire essence of bringing the *chagigah* is only a preparation for the *pesach* (and not just the act of eating it on the night of the fifteenth).

Furthermore: The explanation mentioned above (regarding the Sages' view) that the eating of the *chagigah* is a "preparation" for the eating of the *pesach* does not, on the face of it, provide a sufficient reason for it to bear the status of a *pesach* to such an extent that there would be an obligation to eat it specifically roasted, like the *pesach* itself. We must conclude that the fact that it is eaten roasted is not only because the *chagigah* has the status of a "preparation" for the *pesach*, but (as the *Tzelach* wrote above) because the *chagigah* itself is called by the name *pesach* ("And you shall slaughter the *pesach*").

And since even according to the Sages the sacrifice itself is called by the name *pesach*, a reason is required: Why do the laws of the *pesach* apply to it only regarding roasting?

3. The explanation for this may be found by first explaining the Rambam's view on the law of *heseibah* (reclining):

Regarding the law of *heseibah* on the night of *pesach*, the Rambam writes : "In every generation, a person is obligated to show himself as if he personally went out now from the slavery of Egypt, etc. Therefore, when a person eats on this night, he must eat and drink while reclining in the manner of freedom." At the end of the following *halacha*, after explaining the details of the laws of *heseibah* (who is obligated in *heseibah*, and the manner of *heseibah*), he writes: "And when is *heseibah* required? At the time of eating the *kezayis* of *matzah* and at the time of drinking these four cups."

One still needs an explanation: Why does the Rambam divide the matter into two separate *halachos* [one *halacha*—"When a person eats on this night, he must eat and drink (in general) while reclining in the manner of freedom," and a second *halacha*—"And when is *heseibah* required? At the time of eating the *kezayis* of *matzah*, etc., and the rest of his eating, etc."] and consequently also writes at length? Seemingly, he should have been brief and written immediately in the first *halacha*: "Therefore, when a person eats on this night, he must recline at the time of eating the *kezayis* of *matzah*, etc." (and the like).

It has been explained elsewhere that according to the Rambam's view, there are two legal aspects to *heseibah*: (a) A general commandment in its own right, that a person must show on the night of *pesach* conduct in the manner of freedom, through eating and drinking while reclining (and in this, it makes no difference which eating and drinking is involved). (b) A detail and condition in other commandments (eating *matzah* and the obligation of drinking the four cups)—that one of the conditions of these commandments (and obligations) is that it must be done in the manner of *heseibah*.

This is the distinction between the two *halachos*: In the first *halacha*—which comes in continuation of the previous *halacha* that "in every generation a person is obligated to show himself as if he personally went out now from the slavery of Egypt, etc. (and continues) therefore when a person eats on this night he must eat and drink while reclining in the manner of freedom"—the Rambam's intent is the obligation of *heseibah* in and of itself; and in the (conclusion of the) following *halacha* (after he detailed the specific laws of the obligation of *heseibah*), the Rambam writes "And when is *heseibah* required," by which he intends the second aspect of the obligation of *heseibah*: that *heseibah* is (not a general obligation in its own right, but) a condition in other commandments of this night (in the commandment of eating *matzah* and the obligation of drinking the four cups).

These two aspects of the obligation of *heseibah* are emphasized even more in the golden language of the Alter Rebbe in his *Shulchan Aruch*—whose language and arrangement of words in this matter follow the style of the Rambam:

At first, he explains : "And he shall prepare his place so that he sits while reclining in the manner of freedom, in the way that kings and great people eat, because in every generation a person is obligated to show himself, etc. Therefore, he must perform all the acts of this night in the manner of freedom." And after several paragraphs (detailing several laws of *heseibah*), he continues and explains the things that require *heseibah*: "When must one recline? At the time of eating the *kezayis*... of *matzah*, etc., eating the *koreich*, etc., *afikoman*, etc., drinking the four cups (and he explains the reason), because all these things are a remembrance of the redemption and freedom, etc. Therefore, they require *heseibah* in the manner of freedom."

From this it is proven that in *heseibah* there are two obligations and definitions: (a) A commandment in its own right, which is what is stated in the previous paragraph: "that he sits while reclining in the manner of freedom, etc., because in every generation, etc. Therefore, he must perform all the acts of this night in the manner of freedom." This is a general obligation to conduct oneself in the manner of freedom on this night. And afterwards, he details (b) the things that "require *heseibah* in the manner of freedom," meaning that this is (not the fulfillment of the obligation of *heseibah* in its own right, but) a detail and condition in other things (and as his language precisely states: "Therefore, they require *heseibah* in the manner of freedom"—he did not write "Therefore, one must recline during them," and the like).

4. From all this, we have learned that according to the Rambam, *heseibah* is (a) a general obligation in its own right, that a person must recline "in the manner of freedom" (as the Alter Rebbe's language above states: "he shall prepare his place so that he sits while reclining in the manner of freedom, in the way that kings and great people eat"); (b) *heseibah* becomes a condition in other commandments of *pesach*, in such a way that by eating *matzah* (for example) while reclining, one fulfills two laws: (a) the general obligation of *heseibah*; (b) the fulfillment of the commandment of eating *matzah* in its entirety.

Now, seemingly, one must examine what is the reasoning and necessity to innovate that *heseibah* is also a condition and detail in the other commandments of the night of *pesach*, rather than saying that there is a general obligation of *heseibah*, and that the Sages merely decreed that the most appropriate time to fulfill this obligation is at the time of fulfilling certain (other) commandments of this night?

[Especially since these commandments do not have as their main essence "to show" freedom, for even though "these things are a remembrance of the redemption and freedom"—nevertheless, the act of the commandment itself (in these things) is not an action that indicates freedom.
