For example—eating *matzah*: even though the reason for eating it is because *matzah* is a remembrance of the redemption from Egypt (that "they could not delay, etc."), it is simple that there is nothing in the act of eating *matzah* that indicates "the manner of freedom" (as in the act of *heseibah*, where the *heseibah* itself indicates conduct of a free person, in the way of kings and great people). On the contrary—*matzah* is the bread of poverty.]

It may be said that the foundation of the Rambam's view mentioned above regarding the definition of the law of *heseibah* (of the Sages) is the (Torah) law of "roasted" in the *korban pesach* (for everything the Sages decreed, they decreed in the likeness of the Torah's laws). As follows below.

5. Regarding the law of "roasted" for the *pesach*, the Rambam writes : "When a person eats the *pesach*, he cuts the meat and eats, and cuts, etc., and when he reaches the *gid hanasheh* (sciatic nerve), he removes it and places it with the other nerves and bones and membranes that are removed at the time of eating, for he does not clean it... and they do not cut it, but rather they roast it whole" (and the Ra'avad disagrees: "There is no greater prohibition than roasting the *pesach* with the *gid hanasheh* and its fat, etc.").

It requires study, for seemingly the entire law of roasting the *pesach* is connected to its eating: "And they shall eat the meat on this night, roasted, etc.". If so, it is entirely a detail in the eating of the *pesach*. What is the connection to the command that it be roasted (whole—also) with the nerve, etc., which is not relevant to eating?

This proves the Rambam's view that the law of roasting it is not (only) a detail in the eating of the *pesach*, but rather it is part of the *making* of the *pesach*.

[It may be said that its foundation is that the Torah established the details of the laws in the manner of the act of roasting—"roasted with fire, its head with its legs and its innards", from which it is implied that "roasted, etc." is not only a detail in the manner of eating the *pesach*, but that the act of roasting itself is a law and commandment in its own right in the act of the sacrifice.]

The explanation for this may be: In *Sefer HaChinuch*, it is explained that "the fact that we were commanded to eat it specifically roasted" is "because that is the way of the children of kings and ministers to eat roasted meat, which is good and tasty food, etc., and we, who eat the *pesach* as a remembrance that we went out to freedom, etc., it is certainly fitting for us to conduct ourselves in its eating in the manner of freedom and nobility."

Based on this, one can say that the Rambam holds that the obligation to eat roasted meat contains two matters: (a) One of the conditions of the commandment of eating the *pesach*. (b) Part of the general obligation that "a person is obligated to show himself as if he personally went out now from the slavery of Egypt" —by performing "all the acts of this night in the manner of freedom." And the eating of roasted meat indicates "the manner of freedom and nobility." For this reason, the act of roasting itself has an importance in its own right, to the extent that special laws apply to it (which do not concern the eating of the *pesach*, as mentioned above from the Rambam that they roast the nerve)—because the matter of "roasted" is not (only) a detail and condition in the commandment of eating the *pesach*, but it has (also) a matter in its own right—to indicate the matter of freedom (which is a general matter and obligation in its own right on this night).

Thus, the law of "roasted" teaches us that the Torah established the act of "the manner of freedom" of this night in such a way that, in addition to the fact that the obligation of "freedom" (to perform actions that indicate ("to show") "the manner of freedom") is a general matter in its own right, this rule also becomes a condition and detail in the details of the commandments of the festival (which in themselves do not come to show freedom)—like the law of "roasted," which is a condition in the eating of the *pesach*, for the act of eating the meat of the *pesach* itself does not contain the act of "the manner of freedom" [for the sacrifice is only a remembrance of the redemption from Egypt in the manner of "the *pesach* of Hashem", but the act of eating the meat is not an action that indicates "the manner of freedom" (similar to the above regarding the eating of *matzah*)]—and the Torah innovated that this eating also must be in the manner of "freedom and nobility," i.e., roasted.

From this, the Rambam also learned regarding the law of "the manner of freedom" of the Sages—the obligation of *heseibah*—that although it is a general obligation in its own right, the Sages established it in such a way that it would become a condition in the performance of the commandments of this night, as explained at length above.

6. Based on the above, one can also explain the Rambam's view on the *chagigah* of the fourteenth:

Eating "while satiated" is a significant eating, "in the way that kings eat". Thus, the law of eating the *pesach* "while satiated" is part of the fact that its eating must be in the manner of "freedom and nobility."

Based on this, the reasoning to distinguish between the law of "roasted" and the other conditions of the *pesach* is also understood (and to say that the fact that the *chagigah* is equated to the *pesach* is only regarding the law of "roasted"): Since the entire essence of bringing the *chagigah* is connected to this matter that in the eating of the *pesach* it must be ("while satiated"—) "in the manner of freedom and nobility," therefore the law of "roasted" that is in the eating of the *pesach* also applies to it, for its reason is also "in the way that kings eat". This is not the case for the other details of the laws of the *pesach*, which are not connected to the matter of freedom within it, and thus are not connected to the matter of the *chagigah*.

And since the Rambam's view on the law of "the manner of freedom" of this night is that it is not only a general obligation in its own right, but that the Torah established "the manner of freedom" also in the other acts of the night (which in themselves are not actions of "the manner of freedom"), he holds that even according to the Sages, the obligation of "the manner of freedom" (roasted) applies to the *chagigah*—which is brought on this night for the *pesach*—and for this reason the Torah called the *chagigah* by the name "*pesach*" ("And you shall slaughter the *pesach*").

[And the dispute between Ben Teima and the Sages is: According to Ben Teima, it is impossible for the definition of "freedom" to apply to a sacrifice that does not bear the name *pesach*, and therefore he holds that when the Merciful One equated the *chagigah* to the *pesach*, it became part of the essence of the *pesach* and all its laws are like the *pesach* ; but according to the Sages, the law of the act of freedom applies (also) to "all the acts of this night," even to the commandments of the festival which in themselves are not actions of the manner of freedom (to the extent that it becomes a condition and part of the commandments of *matzah* and drinking the four cups, as mentioned above). And so too regarding the *chagigah* of the fourteenth, that the law of eating roasted (eating "in the manner of freedom") applies also to a sacrifice that is not a *pesach*, for even though it has a law and name in its own right (the *chagigah* of the fourteenth, "and it is eaten for two days and one night like all peace offerings"), the law of the act of freedom applies to it, and it is eaten roasted like the *korban pesach*.

One can connect this with the two opinions —derive from it and from it, derive from it and establish it in its place. But this is not the place to elaborate.]

7. Now, this matter—that on the night of *pesach* there are two aspects to the act of freedom within it: (a) as it is in its own right, (b) as it acts and influences (also) another matter that is not an act of freedom in its own right—is because this is the case in the general relationship of the content of the (remembrance of the) exodus from Egypt to the entirety of the Torah and its commandments.

The explanation: *Sefer HaChinuch* writes regarding the recounting of the exodus from Egypt that "it is a great foundation and a strong pillar in our Torah and our faith" (and therefore "it is no wonder if many commandments, positive and negative, came to us regarding this"). He uses two terms here—foundation and pillar, which indicate two matters: A foundation has a superiority over a pillar, being the foundation upon which the entire building stands, and when the foundation is nullified, the entire building is nullified, unlike a pillar, which seemingly is only to support the building that stands; however, on the other hand, the foundation is something hidden and covered and is not the body of the building itself, unlike a pillar, which is revealed and stands on the outside, and is part of the body of the building itself.

And such is the relationship of the exodus from Egypt to the commandments in the Torah:

Regarding the entirety of the Torah and commandments, the exodus from Egypt is their "foundation," as it is written in the first utterance (the first commandment) of the Ten Commandments : "I am Hashem your G-d who brought you out of the land of Egypt," and the foundation belongs to and establishes the entire building; but on the other hand, this is not their specific (revealed) content, but rather it is their foundation—in a "hidden" manner.

However, there are "many things that the Holy One, Blessed be He, commanded us to do as a remembrance of the exodus from Egypt... such as the separation of the firstborn and all the festivals", in which the matter of the exodus from Egypt is not in the manner of a foundation but in the manner of a pillar, meaning that it is part of the content of these commandments, and it is one of the "pillars" of the commandment.

And like the commandment of wearing *tefillin*, where one must intend when wearing *tefillin* "that the Place commanded us to wear these four sections which contain the unity of His Name and the exodus from Egypt... so that we remember the exodus from Egypt through the miracles and wonders that He did for us, which indicate His unity, that He is one in His world, and that He has the power and the dominion in the heavens and on the earth to do with them as He wills."

Even though the essence and content of wearing *tefillin* is not limited to the matter of the exodus from Egypt—and on the contrary: its main essence is the submission of the heart and the mind, etc. —nevertheless, the matter of the exodus from Egypt also becomes a "pillar" of this commandment, to the extent that this is one of the intentions of wearing *tefillin*, as mentioned above.

And it may be said that in this way, the greatness of the matter of the remembrance of the exodus from Egypt is expressed even more—that it is not only a fundamental and general matter in the entirety of the Torah and commandments, but that it also penetrates into the details of many commandments, and becomes a pillar of many commandments.

May it be His will that as in the days of your going out from the land of Egypt, I will show you wonders, in the true and complete redemption through Mashiach Tzidkeinu, speedily and in our days, truly.

(From a *sicha* of Acharon Shel Pesach, 5743)

## Sicha 3

### 61

A. Regarding the verse, "And it shall be when your son asks you tomorrow, saying, 'What is this?' you shall say to him, 'With a strong hand Hashem brought us out of Egypt...'" (at the end of our Parsha), Rashi quotes the words "when your son asks you tomorrow" and explains: "There is a 'tomorrow' that is now, and there is a 'tomorrow' that is after a time, such as this, and such as, 'Tomorrow your children will say to our children' regarding the sons of Gad and the sons of Reuven [stated in the Book of Joshua, when they returned to the other side of the Jordan after the conquest and division of the land]."

The source for this Rashi is (seemingly) the Mechilta here, which states: "There is a 'tomorrow' that is now, and there is a 'tomorrow' that is after a time. 'What is this'—this is 'tomorrow' after a time. 'Tomorrow this sign will be' [stated above regarding the plague of Arov]—this is 'tomorrow' now. 'Tomorrow your children will say to our children'—this is 'tomorrow' after a time."

Now, the reason Rashi omitted the proof that "there is a 'tomorrow' that is now" from the verse "Tomorrow this sign will be," is seemingly (as written in the commentaries) because Rashi does not require a proof that there is a "tomorrow" that means "now," as this is the simple meaning of "tomorrow" in every place. Furthermore, the "primary intent is to explain here a 'tomorrow' that is after a time."

But based on this, it is difficult—why does Rashi bring this introduction ("there is a 'tomorrow' that is now") at all? There is no novelty in this introduction, and he should have said briefly, "There is a 'tomorrow' that is after a time, such as this, and such as..."?

It is worth noting that later in Parshas Va'eschanan, regarding the verse "When your son asks you tomorrow, saying, 'What are the testimonies...'" Rashi writes briefly, "When your son asks you tomorrow, there is a 'tomorrow' that is after a time," without introducing "there is a 'tomorrow' that is now." However, it can be said that there, the novelty is the opposite—that there is a "tomorrow" that is at a great distance in time, after many generations, until the matter of the Exodus from Egypt has been forgotten and they ask, "What are the testimonies..."

B. One must also analyze Rashi’s wording in the proof he brings that "there is a 'tomorrow' that is after a time"—"and such as, 'Tomorrow your children will say to our children' regarding the sons of Gad and the sons of Reuven"—for seemingly:

1) In the vast majority of proofs that Rashi brings for his words from a verse in Tanach, it is not his custom to write in which context (or in what matter) the verse was said, but rather he only copies the language of the verse. Why does Rashi emphasize here that this text was said regarding "the sons of Gad and the sons of Reuven"? It must be that through this addition, further explanation and understanding is added to the proof that "there is a 'tomorrow' that is after a time."

2) What is Rashi’s reason for placing "the sons of Gad" before "the sons of Reuven"—when Reuven is the firstborn? And the wonder is greater: Rashi reverses their order from how it appears in the verses of Joshua (from where his proof is derived), where the verse precedes—every single time—the sons of Reuven to the sons of Gad!

3) Why does Rashi bring the proof that "there is a 'tomorrow' after a time" from this verse ("regarding the sons of Gad and the sons of Reuven"), and not from a verse stated earlier in the Book of Joshua—"So that this may be a sign among you, when your children ask tomorrow, saying, 'What are these stones to you?'" And furthermore: what is the need to bring a "colleague" from the Prophets—when there is an explicit verse in the Chumash, in Parshas Va'eschanan, as mentioned above—"When your son asks you tomorrow, saying, 'What are the testimonies...'" (the simple meaning of which is that the Exodus from Egypt was already forgotten, as is explicit in Rashi there, as mentioned above, "there is a 'tomorrow' that is after a time").

[And even though this proof in Rashi is the same as the one brought in the Mechilta as mentioned above—(a) even in the Mechilta, the reason for this needs explanation; (b) it has been explained many times that Rashi's intent in his commentary on the Torah is not to copy the teachings of Chazal, but only when they align with the simple meaning of the verse. It must be that for the understanding of our verse, it is necessary to bring the proof specifically from the verse of the sons of Gad and the sons of Reuven.]

One must also understand why Rashi copies the words "When your son asks you" in the heading, even though (seemingly) he is only explaining the word "tomorrow."

C. This will be understood by prefacing what has been explained many times regarding Rashi’s approach in his commentary on the Torah—that his style is clear, such that to understand it, there is no need to study the sources of Rashi, and it is understood even by a student who is only concerned with the simple meaning of the verse, which is—as Chazal say in their Mishnah—"five years old for Scripture."

From this it is understood that even when Rashi uses the language of Chazal, his intent is the simple meaning of that language and expression, even if it is possible that this language is interpreted (in its source) in Chazal in a different way.

And likewise in our case: what Rashi says, "there is a 'tomorrow' that is now," his intent is the simple meaning of the word "now," meaning that there is a "tomorrow" (which does not mean the day following this day—but rather that which comes after the event, even though) "that is now", immediately on this day. For since it comes after the event being discussed, it is a "tomorrow".

[It can be said that this is the reason Rashi did not bring the proof in the Mechilta for "there is a 'tomorrow' that is now" from the verse "Tomorrow this sign will be"—because in the Mechilta, the intent of "now" is the day following it (and it is called "now" relative to "tomorrow that is after a time"), whereas in Rashi, the intent is that "there is a 'tomorrow' that is now" in its simple sense, in the present, and consequently, the verse "Tomorrow this sign will be" has no relevance here.]

According to this, the continuation of Rashi’s words is explained: According to the regular interpretation of the word "tomorrow"—the day after this day—it is puzzling: why would the son’s question be "tomorrow" specifically? If the son does not understand the reason for the mitzvah, why does he not ask immediately at the time of the act, today, but waits until the next day?!

Therefore, Rashi explains that "tomorrow" does not mean in every place its simple meaning—the day after—because it has other interpretations (each appropriate to the content of the verse, the matter in which it is said): "There is a 'tomorrow' that is now," meaning, the time coming immediately after the thing or the act (of that day)—this is "after," "tomorrow"; "and there is a 'tomorrow' that is after a time"—on one of the days following it, or years, or generations to come.

And Rashi continues, "such as this, and such as, 'Tomorrow your children will say to our children' regarding the sons of Gad, etc.," meaning that in the case at hand, the interpretation of the word "tomorrow" is (not "now," but specifically)—"after a time," as explained in the commentaries, that the son’s question is only possible "after a time," "for (if it were a son of that time and period) he saw and knew the Exodus from Egypt and all the plagues and that the dough did not have time to become leavened, so certainly 'tomorrow' is after a time."

D. According to this, it can also be explained why Rashi needed to preface "there is a 'tomorrow' that is now"—even though this is not the interpretation of "tomorrow" in this verse—and with this introduction:

In addition to the aforementioned difficulty in this verse—how the term "tomorrow" fits here—there is also a more general difficulty: what is the practical difference in this emphasis here, that the son’s question will be "tomorrow"? And in particular, that this content—the son’s question—we have already found earlier in the Parsha, "And it shall be when you come to the land... and you shall keep this service, and it shall be when your children say to you, 'What is this service to you?' and you shall say, 'It is a Pesach sacrifice...'"—and there it is not said that the son’s question will be "tomorrow"; why does the verse here emphasize that the son’s question "What is this" will be "tomorrow"?

And one cannot answer that there it is understood by itself, because the verse prefaces "And it shall be when you come to the land...", and consequently it is simple that it is not speaking of the son’s question at the time of the Exodus, but a question "after a time" (when they come to the land) and there is no need to write "tomorrow"—for here too it is explicit (as an introduction to the son’s question) "And it shall be when Hashem brings you to the land of the Canaanite...", and if so, it is not speaking of the son’s question in the generation of the Exodus, and the difficulty remains—why does the verse add the word "tomorrow" here?

It must be that the intent of the verse in adding the word "tomorrow" is not to emphasize the time, when the son’s question will be (now or after a time), but to explain the cause of the question.

Explanation of the matter:
There is a question of "your son" whose cause is only—his being "your son," meaning, because he is a son who lacks study and knowledge, and therefore when he sees something for which he has not yet learned the reason, he asks "What is this?" [Although in this itself there are several manners and levels, as Rashi brings here in the heading regarding the four sons, "The Torah speaks against four sons, the wise, the wicked, the simple, and the one who does not know how to ask"—but the common denominator in all of them is that their questions come because they are sons, who have not yet learned, etc.].

And this is the emphasis and addition of the Torah here, "When your son asks you tomorrow"—that the cause of this question is not only because he is a son (like the question above—"And it shall be when your children say to you, 'What is this service to you?'"), but primarily because it is speaking of "tomorrow": the fact that they are in a different time and a different period is what causes this question. And as will be explained in section VI.

E. According to this, the reason is understood why Rashi did not suffice with writing briefly, "tomorrow—after a time," but elaborates in his commentary: (a) he prefaces "there is a 'tomorrow' that is now," (b) he brings a proof and example for "there is a 'tomorrow' that is after a time" from what is stated in Joshua, "Tomorrow your children will say to our children," (c) he adds at the end of the proof, "regarding the sons of Gad and the sons of Reuven":

The interpretation of "there is a 'tomorrow' that is after a time" can be explained in two ways:
1) Even according to this, "tomorrow" is a concept and measure of time; however, this time is not limited specifically to the day following this day, but it includes every "tomorrow" from the days that will come in the future. 2) "Tomorrow" is not a concept determined by the order of times, but depends on and relates to the subject being discussed, where the time after this subject is called "tomorrow" relative to the present time. According to this, the intent of "after a time" is not only to the future in general, but to a new period, coming after the end of the period that is in the present.

It can be said that according to Rashi’s interpretation here, the title "tomorrow... after a time" is in the second sense—a completely new period (and this is the reason for the son’s question). Therefore, Rashi prefaces "there is a 'tomorrow' that is now"—to prove that the word "tomorrow" can be interpreted not only as a measure of time, but also in the sense of "afterwards," and in such a sense—"there is a 'tomorrow' that is now" in the present (and it is called "tomorrow" because it comes after the act); from which it is also understood regarding "there is a 'tomorrow' that is after a time"—that the intent is not only that it is a matter of the future (in general), but that it comes after the (end of the) period of the present.

And therefore, Rashi brought the proof from the verse "Tomorrow your children will say to our children regarding the sons of Gad and the sons of Reuven" specifically (and not from the verse in Parshas Va'eschanan, "When your son asks you tomorrow, saying, 'What are the testimonies...'", or from what is stated in Joshua, "When your children ask tomorrow, saying, 'What are these stones to you?'")—because specifically in this verse, it is emphasized most strongly that the possibility of this statement is only because they are in a completely new period—"your children will say to our children," a new generation and from both sides, and only because of this is such a statement possible.

And as is understood from the simplicity of the story in Joshua there, that when the sons of Gad and the sons of Reuven returned to the other side of the Jordan, after fulfilling their promise to Moses, "And we will arm ourselves quickly before the children of Israel... until the children of Israel inherit each his inheritance," they built an altar there, and explained their reason to the rest of the children of Israel, that this is "a witness... between us and you and between our generations", that if "tomorrow your children will say to our children, saying, 'What have you to do with Hashem, the G-d of Israel? And Hashem has set a border between us and you... the Jordan...'", they will say, "Behold the pattern of the altar of Hashem which our fathers made..."—and this fear, that "Hashem has set a border between us and you," has no place at all during the entire duration of that generation, for the sons of Gad and the sons of Reuven sacrificed their lives in practice for the rest of the children of Israel in order to help them inherit their land. And only in the generation after that, when there are only "children"—"your children" and "our children," then it is possible that they will forget the deeds of the sons of Gad and the sons of Reuven and it will occur to them that there is a "border" and a separation between them and the rest of Israel.

And with this, Rashi’s precision is also explained, that he places "the sons of Gad" before "the sons of Reuven"—for even though Reuven is the firstborn and is generally mentioned before Gad, nevertheless, in the matter of their self-sacrifice and their war for all of Israel, the sons of Gad preceded the sons of Reuven [as Rashi brings that the sons of Gad were mighty, "as it is said regarding Gad, 'And he tears the arm, even the crown of the head,'" and as explained elsewhere that in the matter of the war, the sons of Reuven were secondary to the sons of Gad, and therefore we find in every place the expression "the sons of Gad and the sons of Reuven," placing the sons of Gad before the sons of Reuven]—and this is the emphasis here, that since it is speaking of "the sons of Gad and the sons of Reuven" who sacrificed their lives for the rest of Israel, therefore all the fear of "they will say..." belongs only "tomorrow, after a time"—after the rise of a new generation of children who do not remember the deed of the sons of Gad and the sons of Reuven, as mentioned above.

F. However, according to this, one must wonder, what is the difference between the question of the children above—"And it shall be when your children say to you, 'What is this service...'" and the question here at the end of our Parsha, "And it shall be when your son asks you tomorrow, saying, 'What is this?'" that specifically here the cause of his question is—"tomorrow" (his being in a new period)?

And this can be explained simply—according to the difference between the content of the questions:
The question above is regarding the Pesach sacrifice, for previously the Torah commanded, "And it shall be when you come to the land... and you shall keep this service" (the bringing of the Pesach sacrifice), and regarding this the verse continues, "And it shall be when your children say to you, 'What is this service...' and you shall say, 'It is a Pesach sacrifice to Hashem, who passed over...'"; whereas here at the end of the Parsha, it is speaking about the mitzvah of the firstborn, as is explicit at its beginning, "And it shall be when Hashem brings you to the land of the Canaanite... and you shall set apart all that opens the womb to Hashem and every firstborn that is dropped by a beast... and every firstborn of a donkey you shall redeem with a lamb... and every firstborn of man among your sons you shall redeem," and regarding this the verse continues, "And it shall be when your son asks you tomorrow, saying, 'What is this?' and you shall say to him, 'With a strong hand Hashem brought us out of Egypt... and Hashem killed every firstborn in the land of Egypt... therefore I sacrifice to Hashem all that opens the womb, the males, and every firstborn of my sons I redeem.'"

And according to this, the difference in the cause of the questions is also understood:
The matter of the Exodus from Egypt (in general) is not something that could be forgotten by the children of Israel, for every single day they mention and also do several things as a "remembrance of the Exodus from Egypt", and the question of "What is this service to you?" on the night of Pesach comes because it is speaking of a "son" who has not yet learned, etc.;
Whereas regarding the mitzvah of the firstborn, there is a difference in which generation is being spoken of: the generation of those who left Egypt certainly knew and remembered the saving of the firstborn among them, and therefore when the Holy One, Blessed be He, commanded (on the 15th of Nissan), "Sanctify to Me every firstborn, whatever opens the womb among the children of Israel... it is Mine"—as Rashi says, "I acquired them by striking the firstborn of Egypt"—there was no room for a question among them, for they themselves were acquired by Hashem, through His saving them from death; and only when a new generation arises, who were not in Egypt, for them the question is relevant regarding the mitzvah of the firstborn—"What is this?" and there is a need to explain to them, because since "Hashem killed every firstborn in the land of Egypt..."—"therefore I sacrifice to Hashem all that opens the womb..."

G. Based on the above that here it is speaking of the mitzvah of the firstborn—there is another reason to say why Rashi prefaces at the beginning of his commentary, "there is a 'tomorrow' that is now":

Regarding the verse, "And it shall be when Hashem brings you to the land of the Canaanite," Rashi says, "There are some of our Rabbis who learned from here that they did not sanctify the firstborn born in the desert, and the one who says that they did sanctify them explains this 'bringing' to say: if you fulfill it in the desert, you will merit to enter the land and fulfill it there." And this is, that according to the first (and primary) interpretation, the firstborn born in the desert were not sanctified (and simply, Rashi’s intent is only regarding those born in the desert, but the firstborn who were in Egypt, according to the simple meaning, were certainly sanctified, as is explicit above, "Sanctify to Me every firstborn" (without the condition "And it shall be when He brings you..."), and as is explicit in Rashi as mentioned above, "I acquired them by striking the firstborn of Egypt"); but there is a second interpretation that even those born in the desert were sanctified.

And according to the second opinion, it is difficult—why is the term "tomorrow" used here, for it is impossible to interpret that the question is because they are in a new period, because the period of their stay in the desert is not a (truly) new period, for the generation of those who left Egypt were still alive and existing? Therefore, Rashi prefaces "there is a 'tomorrow' that is now," and with this Rashi hints that according to the opinion that even the firstborn born in the desert were sanctified and the beginning of the mitzvah of the firstborn is not after they come to the land, it is reasonable to interpret "tomorrow" here as—"a 'tomorrow' that is now", that when they see that the father is fulfilling the mitzvah of the firstborn, he will ask immediately after this—"What is this?" (as mentioned in section C regarding the interpretation of "there is a 'tomorrow' that is now").

H. The inner dimension of Torah in Rashi:
In this Rashi—"there is a 'tomorrow' that is now and there is a 'tomorrow' that is after a time"—it can be said that it hints to two types of "sons" who ask questions "tomorrow." The "sons" of "tomorrow" are the children of the next generation, and there are two manners among them: "There is a 'tomorrow' that is now"—he is indeed a son, from the next generation, but in the content of his essence, he is alive and exists "now," for he is not separated from his father, but he lives the same life as the father, the life of Judaism, and constitutes a direct (but) continuation ("tomorrow") of the father. And in this there is no novelty, for when this "son" asks questions in Judaism (due to his still being in the state of a son)—one must strive to answer him so that he can serve Hashem with joy and vitality.

But—"there is a 'tomorrow' that is after a time," such a son who has no connection or relevance to the character of the father’s life, and he is from a "new period," a new generation "that did not know Joseph"; and it is understood that the type of "questions" of this "son" do not come only because of a lack of knowledge, but that the manner of life according to Torah and mitzvos with beauty, etc., is a new and strange thing to him and is not according to his spirit.

And this is the instruction of the Torah—that even such a "son," since he is "your son," it is your responsibility (and your merit) to answer the questions of this son, and to bring him under the wings of the Shechinah.

And by engaging with all types of sons of the people of Israel, we establish the "armies of Hashem", who will merit very soon to the true and complete Redemption through Mashiach Tzidkeinu, "As in the days of your coming out of the land of Egypt, I will show him wonders", and speedily in our days, truly.

(From a Sicha of Shabbos Parshas Bo, 5730)


## בשלח

## Sicha 2

### 77

A. Regarding the verse "The Sanctuary, O Hashem, which Your hands have established," the Mechilta states (and is cited by Rashi on the verse): "The Beis Hamikdash is beloved to the Ribono shel Olam, for when the Ribono shel Olam created His world, He created it with only one hand, as it is written, 'My hand also founded the earth.' But when He came to build the Beis Hamikdash, it was, as it were, with two hands, as it is written, 'The Sanctuary, O Hashem, which Your hands have established.' When will You build it with two hands?... 'Hashem shall reign forever and ever'" (in the time to come, when all the kingship will be His. Rashi). This means that the interpretation of "Your hands have established" is literal: the Mikdash is the handiwork of the Ribono shel Olam.

However, in the Gemara it states: "The deeds of the tzaddikim are greater than the deeds of heaven and earth. For regarding the deeds of heaven and earth, it is written, 'My hand also founded the earth' (one hand, Rashi), and 'My right hand has spanned the heavens.' But regarding the handiwork of the tzaddikim, it is written, 'The place for Your dwelling which You have made, O Hashem; the Sanctuary, O Hashem, which Your hands have established'". Rashi explains: "The Sanctuary is the handiwork of the tzaddikim" [and even though the verse says "The Sanctuary, O Hashem, which Your hands have established"—the handiwork of the tzaddikim is "called... the handiwork of the Ribono shel Olam"].

Seemingly, there is room to say that the dispute between the Gemara and the Mechilta (whether "The Sanctuary, O Hashem, which Your hands have established" refers to the handiwork of the Ribono shel Olam (literally) or the handiwork of the tzaddikim) depends on the well-known dispute as to whether the Third Beis Hamikdash will be built by human beings or whether it will be built by the Ribono shel Olam Himself (and all agree that the verse refers to the Third Beis Hamikdash).

Based on this, one could say that Rashi’s inclusion of the Mechilta’s teaching on the verse is because he follows his own consistent approach throughout the Shas that "the future Sanctuary for which we hope, which is built and perfected, will be revealed and come from heaven, as it is written, 'The Sanctuary, O Hashem, which Your hands have established'".

## 78

However, according to this, one must wonder on the other hand: why did Rashi (in his commentary to the Shas) write regarding the Third Beis Hamikdash that "the Sanctuary is the handiwork of the tzaddikim"—the opposite of his position (mentioned above) in other places that the future Sanctuary "will be revealed and come from heaven"?

Therefore, it seems more plausible to explain that the dispute concerns which Sanctuary is being discussed in the verse "The Sanctuary, O Hashem, which Your hands have established" : According to the Mechilta (and Rashi on the Torah), it refers to the Third Beis Hamikdash, and therefore "Your hands have established" means the Sanctuary is the handiwork of the Ribono shel Olam. Conversely, according to the Gemara, it refers to the First (and Second) Beis Hamikdash, which all agree are the handiwork of man.

One must understand what the foundation and reason for their dispute is: whether the Song of the Sea speaks of the First (and Second) Beis Hamikdash or the Third Beis Hamikdash of the future.

B. We find two approaches among the Torah commentators on this passage. According to many commentators, it refers to the Mishkan and the First Beis Hamikdash built by David (and Shlomo). What is written regarding it, "The Sanctuary, O Hashem, which Your hands have established," is like saying, "According to all that I show you" ; "And you shall erect the Mishkan according to its pattern which you were shown on the mountain", for "the Ribono shel Olam showed Moshe the pattern of the entire Mishkan built", and "so David said, 'All this in writing from the hand of Hashem, He gave me insight'". Therefore, it is called by the name of the Ribono shel Olam. However, according to Rashi (and others), it refers to the Sanctuary that will be built in the time to come, as mentioned above.

Seemingly, one should note: the method and approach of Rashi on the Torah is to explain the simple meaning of the verse (*peshuto shel mikra*). Simply put, this verse appears as a continuation of its beginning: "You shall bring them and plant them in the mountain of Your inheritance, the place for Your dwelling which You have made, O Hashem"—that is, the entry into the Land and the building of the First Beis Hamikdash. Why does Rashi divide the verse into two, separating them by the time of all the exiles, and then say the end of the verse refers to the future Sanctuary? [And in any case, this interpretation seems further from the simple meaning than the interpretation that "Your hands have established" refers to the Sanctuary in general (the first one) (which was built by human beings and is called "Your hands have established" because "All this in writing from the hand of Hashem, He gave me insight")].

Furthermore, from a logical standpoint, it is not understood: how is it appropriate that in the (midst of) song and praise for the miracles of the splitting of the sea, Bnei Yisrael would mention the future Beis Hamikdash, which emphasizes that the Sanctuary they would build upon entering the Land was destined to be destroyed [as emphasized in the language of the Mechilta here: "When will You build it with two hands? A parable of robbers who entered the king’s palace, looted his treasures, killed, etc., and destroyed, etc. After a time, the king sat with them in judgment, seized them, killed, etc., and sat in his palace, and afterwards His kingship was made known to the world"]—that by this addition they mixed matters of calamity into the song !

## 79

C. It can be said that the explanation for this is:
When Rashi says, "And when will it be built with two hands? At the time when 'Hashem shall reign forever and ever,' in the time to come, when all the kingship is His," he does not mean that this was the intention of Moshe and Bnei Yisrael when they sang this song—to mention the building of the future Sanctuary (after Rashi's time). Rather, this is Rashi’s explanation: that in actuality, this will be in the time to come. But when they sang the song, Yisrael intended that this would be in the Sanctuary in general (the First), that the Ribono shel Olam would build this Sanctuary with His two hands, and then "Hashem shall reign forever and ever" would be fulfilled. As is explained in many places, had they not sinned, Yisrael would have entered Eretz Yisrael in a manner of complete and eternal redemption, after which there would be no exile.

With this, the reason for the difference between Rashi and the Mechilta is simply understood: The Mechilta elaborates with a parable (as mentioned above): "When will You build it with two hands? A parable of robbers... (all of which happened over a long time and) after a time the king sat with them in judgment... and afterwards His kingship was made known to the world"—meaning that it speaks of the Sanctuary that will be built in the future, a long time after the (First and Second) Sanctuaries are destroyed. Rashi, however, did not bring this elaboration, because according to Rashi, the intention of Yisrael was that the Sanctuary to be built immediately upon their entry into the Land would be built with the "two hands" of the Ribono shel Olam.

That is to say: According to the Mechilta, the end of the verse, "The Sanctuary, O Hashem, which Your hands have established," does not refer to the Sanctuary discussed in the first part of the verse, "the place for Your dwelling which You have made, O Hashem" [which Bnei Yisrael simply said in connection to the First Beis Hamikdash built when "You shall bring them and plant them in the mountain of Your inheritance"; and it should be noted that "the throne below is aligned with the throne above"—which is the meaning of "the place for Your dwelling which You have made, O Hashem" (as stated in the Mechilta)—applies to all Sanctuaries, including the First and Second]. Conversely, according to Rashi, Yisrael did not intend a different Sanctuary, but the same Sanctuary as at the beginning of the verse [only that in actuality the sin caused it not to be fulfilled, and it will not be until the time to come, as Rashi explains].

We have learned that there are three methods in interpreting "The Sanctuary, O Hashem, which Your hands have established": According to the Mechilta, it refers to the future Sanctuary, which will be built (a long time) after the first Sanctuaries are destroyed; according to the Gemara, it refers to the First (and Second) Sanctuary, which are the handiwork of the tzaddikim; and according to Rashi, although it refers to the Sanctuary built then upon their entry into the Land, the intention is that the Ribono shel Olam would build it with His two hands, "and when (it actually comes to pass) etc."

D. It can be said that these three methods depend on the three ways of learning and expounding the language of the song:
According to some exegetes, the way of the verses in parables and songs and the like is to repeat the matter in different words, for the beauty of the rhetoric alone. On the other hand, in the study of *drash*, every matter in the song is expounded as a separate subject.

## 80

It can be said that Rashi’s method in his commentary on the Torah is a third method, intermediate between them: Even in the song, Rashi holds that there is no mere repetition, but only when a new detail or matter is added [as Rashi writes (in the old commentary) regarding the Song of the Sea, "For He has triumphed, triumphed... the repetition comes to say... and so you will find the entire song repeated," and Rashi explains in the verses the reason for the repetition of the matter]. However, on the other hand, the addition is not a (completely) new matter, but an additional detail of the same matter [similar to "And the doubling of the dream to Pharaoh twice is because the thing is established by the Ribono shel Olam, and the Ribono shel Olam hastens to do it"—where the doubling adds strength and speed to this very matter].

So it is in our verse—which has a doubling of language: "the place for Your dwelling which You have made, O Hashem; the Sanctuary, O Hashem, which Your hands have established." The intention of the doubling can be explained in three ways: (a) that it is a complete doubling, only for the beauty of the rhetoric, (b) that it adds a completely new matter, (c) that it is an additional detail of the same matter.

This is the difference between the three methods mentioned above:
In the Mechilta—where our Sages expounded—they expound the doubling "The Sanctuary, O Hashem, which Your hands have established" as a new matter (not spoken of previously)—the future Sanctuary that will be built after the first and second are destroyed.
The interpretation of the Gemara that it refers to the building of the First (and Second) Sanctuary by tzaddikim fits the methods that the doubling in the verse is for the beauty of the rhetoric, and accordingly, the intention of the verse "The Sanctuary, O Hashem, which Your hands have established" is the same as the beginning of the verse, "the place for Your dwelling which You have made, O Hashem".
And according to Rashi, this doubling comes to add an additional detail to this Sanctuary that would be built upon their entry into the Land: that in addition to the fact that the Ribono shel Olam made a "place for Your dwelling" above, He would also build the Sanctuary below with His two hands [only that in actuality this was not fulfilled upon their entry into the Land the first time, and when will it be fulfilled—"in the time to come"].

E. One can explain more deeply the content of the difference between the three interpretations, by first explaining the reason why the "Beis Hamikdash" and its greatness ("the place... the Sanctuary...") are mentioned at all in the Song of the Sea (the content of which is praise for the miracle of the splitting of the sea and the killing of the Egyptians)—for seemingly, what does it have to do with here?

That the entry into the Land is mentioned in the song—"You shall bring them and plant them in the mountain of Your inheritance" —is understood, because this is the continuation and the completion of the redemption from Egypt, as it is written, "I will bring you up from the affliction of Egypt to the land of the Canaanite, etc." (and as they said before this in the song itself, "You have led in Your kindness the people whom You have redeemed; You have guided them with Your strength to Your holy abode"). And since the splitting of the sea is the conclusion and end of the exodus from Egypt (for only then did all fear of the Egyptians cease), they included it in the Song of the Sea, that now that the exodus from Egypt was finished, they were

## 81

ready for the purpose of the exodus—"You shall bring them and plant them in the mountain of Your inheritance."

It should be added that this is not just a request for the future (that the Ribono shel Olam should finish the matter of the exodus until its purpose, which is the entry into the Land), but that this is also part of the praise for the past, the miracles of the splitting of the sea—because the miracles of the splitting of the sea, besides their effect on the Egyptians (until they were destroyed so that not one remained), also helped Bnei Yisrael to be able to enter the Land easily, as they said in the song, "Then the chiefs of Edom were alarmed... all the inhabitants of Canaan melted away." This is their intention when they said, "Terror and dread shall fall upon them... until Your people pass over, O Hashem, until this people whom You have acquired pass over," as Rashi explains, "Terror on the distant ones and dread on the near ones," that this dread remained upon them until they crossed the Jordan (as Rashi brought on this the verse from Yehoshua, "For we have heard how Hashem dried up..."). Therefore they said, "You shall bring them and plant them, etc.," because even the manner of entry into the Land was connected with the miracles of the splitting of the sea, as mentioned above.

But it is not understood—what does the building of the Beis Hamikdash have to do with this?
[With difficulty, one could say that the building of the Beis Hamikdash indicates the completion of the entry into the Land, as was the case in actuality, that the entire Land was not conquered until the days of David, and also in the content of the matter, it is explicit in the verse that the building of the Beis Hamikdash came after the rest from the enemies round about. However, from the language of the verses here, it implies that the matter of the Beis Hamikdash comes here not as a part and conclusion of the conquest and entry into the Land, but as a matter and praise in its own right.]

Furthermore: what is the practical difference here for the detail that the Sanctuary was built with "Your hands"?

F. The explanation for this is:
At the end of the song (in the verse that follows), it says, "Hashem shall reign forever and ever." The Ramban explains, "He says that He showed now that He is King and ruler over everything, for He saved His servants and destroyed those who rebelled against Him. May it be the will before Him to do so in all generations forever." This means that the content of the song is not only praise and thanksgiving for the very miracles and wonders that the Ribono shel Olam did in destroying Egypt, etc., but (primarily) praise for the fact that through the splitting of the sea, the kingship and rule of the Ribono shel Olam was revealed, both in the very destruction of Pharaoh and Egypt, and in its manner, that the Ribono shel Olam overturned the systems of nature, and all for the sake of the salvation and redemption of Yisrael.

Now, the ultimate perfection in the revelation of the kingship of the Ribono shel Olam in actuality was in the fulfillment of the command, "And they shall make Me a Sanctuary, and I will dwell among them", in the building of the Beis Hamikdash, that He descended to dwell on earth. Therefore, in the building of the First Beis Hamikdash, the redemption of Yisrael was in its completeness from all the enemies round about, so that there was no longer any rule of the nations of the world in the Land.

This is also the content of the building of the Sanctuary with two hands: The difference simply between a deed done with one hand and a deed done with two hands is that a deed with two hands means the doer is occupied only with this, whereas in a deed with one hand, at the time of the deed, he can be occupied with a completely different matter, as in the example above—at the time of the creation of the earth with the left, He created the heavens with the right.

## 82

This is the content of the difference between the world, which was created with one hand, and the Sanctuary, which was built with two hands: In the "world," from its own perspective, the truth of its existence is not (so) clearly recognizable—that is, that it exists only from the truth of His existence —because it was created "with one hand." Conversely, in the Beis Hamikdash, His *Shechinah* dwelled, and ten miracles were performed in it openly, in such a way that the limitations of the nature of the world were nullified, etc.—because it was built with two hands.

Therefore, specifically in the Beis Hamikdash, they would pronounce the Name as it is written.
Now, this matter applies to all Sanctuaries, including the First Beis Hamikdash, and in accordance with this is the interpretation of the Shas that this verse refers to the Sanctuaries that were the handiwork of man.

However, according to the interpretation of the Mechilta and Rashi, that this verse and the one that follows ("Hashem shall reign forever and ever") are one matter, one cannot interpret that "The Sanctuary, O Hashem, which Your hands have established" refers to the First Beis Hamikdash, for in it the kingship of the Ribono shel Olam was not in its completeness, as it was destroyed. Therefore, they interpret that the end of the song, which is the completeness of the kingship of the Ribono shel Olam, is the Beis Hamikdash of the future.

However, there is a difference here between the Mechilta and Rashi—which depends on the general intention of the Song of the Sea:
The Song of the Sea is the first song explicitly mentioned in the Torah. Accordingly, it is understood that among the ten songs brought in the Mechilta, it is the most general song, the opening for all the songs. It can be said that this is what they learned in the Mechilta : "'Then Moshe sang'—it is not written here, but 'Then Moshe will sing'—from here we learn the resurrection of the dead from the Torah." The intention in this (is also) that in this song is included the entire continuation of the songs regarding the redemption of Yisrael until the song of the time to come. For just as the redemption of Egypt was the beginning and opening for all the redemptions until the future redemption, likewise the Song of the Sea regarding the redemption of Yisrael and the kingship of the Ribono shel Olam at the time of the splitting of the sea includes all the redemptions and the revelation of the kingship of the Ribono shel Olam until the end of all generations, and this is in the Beis Hamikdash of the future, of which it says, "Hashem shall reign forever and ever."

Therefore, the Mechilta brings the parable of the robbers who entered the king’s palace, looted, etc., and destroyed the king’s palace; after a time the king sat, etc., and sat in his palace, and afterwards His kingship was made known to the world—because the emphasis on the completeness of the kingship of the Ribono shel Olam in the Beis Hamikdash of the future is in the fact that it comes after the destruction of the Beis Hamikdash that preceded it.

However, according to Rashi in the simple meaning, the content of the song is the song of the exodus from Egypt, the kingship of the Ribono shel Olam that came to revelation in the miracles and wonders at the sea and in the redemption of Bnei Yisrael from Pharaoh and Egypt. But this completeness of the kingship of the Ribono shel Olam—that is, the kingship of the Ribono shel Olam in the redemption of Bnei Yisrael from Egypt—is in the arrival to the Land and in the building of the Beis Hamikdash by the Ribono shel Olam, so that "all the kingship will be His," and this is the content of the matter of the building of the Beis Hamikdash with two hands.

G. One can explain the difference between the language of the Mechilta, "and afterwards His kingship was made known to the world," and the language of Rashi, "that all the kingship is His":
In the revelation of the kingship of the Ribono shel Olam, there are two matters :
1) The destruction of those who rebel and oppose His kingship, as brought in the *maamar* "Basi L'Gani", the *maamar* given by the Rebbe Rayatz to be studied on the day of his *yahrzeit*, that the nullification and destruction of the opponent pertains to the completeness of the kingship, and therefore, for the sake of the victory over the opponent, the king spends all his treasures and the treasures that his fathers had stored. And so it is with the kingship of the Ribono shel Olam, as it is written, "He brought forth wind from His treasuries," which struck the firstborn of Egypt, for Egypt are the *metzarim* (limitations) and boundaries, and the firstborn of Egypt are the strength and power of the *kelipah*, and for this He brings forth wind from His treasuries.
2) That all the inhabitants of the world will recognize and know that "to You every knee shall bow," and all will accept the yoke of His kingship, as it will be in the time to come, that "then I will turn to the peoples a clear language, that they may all call upon the name of Hashem, to serve Him with one shoulder".

It can be said that this is the difference between the Mechilta and Rashi:

## 84

In the Mechilta, the emphasis of "His kingship was made known to the world" is (primarily) on the destruction of the opponent, as is understood from the parable: "After a time the king sat with them in judgment, seized them, killed them, crucified them, and sat in his palace, and afterwards His kingship was made known to the world."
Conversely, according to Rashi, the main matter is not the destruction of the opponent, but that "all the kingship is His," as Rashi writes in the explanation of "Hashem our God, Hashem is One" : "Hashem who is our God now and not the God of the nations... He will be 'Hashem is One' in the future, as it is written, 'For then I will turn to the peoples a clear language, that they may all call upon the name of Hashem,' and it is said, 'On that day Hashem will be One and His name One.'"

(From the Sichos of Shabbos Parshas Beshalach and Shabbos Parshas Yisro, 5744)

## Sicha 3

### 85

1. In our Parsha, near the conclusion of the section concerning the *Man*—following the detailed account of the *Man*’s descent, the manner of its gathering, and the specific events of Friday and Shabbos, when on Friday they gathered a double portion and on Shabbos the *Man* did not descend, until the conclusion of the matter, "So the people rested on the seventh day"—it is stated: "The House of Israel called its name *Man*; it was like coriander seed, white, and its taste was like a wafer made with honey." Subsequently, the command was given to keep it "as a keepsake for your generations...".

This requires clarification:
a) Why did the text wait to provide the description and characterization of the *Man* ("it was like coriander seed...") until the conclusion of the entire matter, including the events of the week, rather than describing it at the beginning of the narrative (especially when Moshe told Bnei Yisrael, "It is the bread which Hashem has given you to eat")?

b) From the phrasing "The House of Israel called its name *Man*," it is implied that only after all the intervening events (including the details of Friday and Shabbos) did they decide to call it by this name. This is difficult to understand: immediately upon seeing the *Man* for the first time, Bnei Yisrael inquired and said to one another, "*Man hu*" ("It is *Man*"), which Rashi explains as "a preparation of food". Yet at that time, it was not in the category of "naming" it, as the verse continues, "for they did not know what it was," and as Rashi explains, "They did not know... to call it by its name". What changed in the interim that they now knew and decided to call it by the name "*Man*"?

c) Similarly, it is difficult regarding the command to keep it "as a keepsake for your generations." Seemingly, this command should have been given at the beginning of the *Man*’s descent, when Bnei Yisrael saw that Hashem was preparing food for them—for this is the intent of the command "as a keepsake for your generations," as explained in Rashi: "In the days of Yirmiyahu, when he was rebuking them, 'Why do you not engage in Torah?' and they replied, 'From where shall we be sustained?' he brought out the jar of *Man* and said to them, 'You, see the word of Hashem... see with this your ancestors were sustained; the Omnipresent has many messengers to prepare food for those who fear Him.'" Why delay this until after the details of Friday and Shabbos?

## 86

We must conclude that the events between the beginning of the *Man*’s descent and the end of the section, including Friday and Shabbos, "contributed" to the understanding of the *Man*’s essence and, consequently, to the choice and establishment of its name. Therefore, the text waited until this point to describe it and to command that it be kept "as a keepsake for your generations."

2. The core of the explanation is this: the details of Friday and Shabbos are not additional or peripheral matters regarding the *Man*; rather, they belong to the very content of the *Man*. In our case: the definition of this food necessitated that it not descend on Shabbos.

That is to say: the reason the *Man* did not descend on Shabbos is not merely because of the prohibitions of Shabbos associated with gathering and preparing the *Man* (which would make the Shabbos aspect a peripheral issue, a "lion lying upon it" that prevents its descent on Shabbos). Rather, it is from the perspective of the *Man* itself: the content of the *Man* requires that it not descend on Shabbos (as will be explained in section 4 and onwards).

Based on this, it follows that until Shabbos (when the *Man* did not descend and they ate the "double portion" that descended on Friday), the purpose of the *Man* had not yet been completed.

This is the reason why the naming of the *Man* by Bnei Yisrael occurred only after "the people rested on the seventh day." 

When Bnei Yisrael initially said to one another "*Man hu*" ("a preparation of food"), it was not in the category of naming it. Rather, because of their lack of knowledge ("for they did not know... to call it by its name")—since it was immediately at the beginning and they did not yet know the essence of this food and its details—they only said "*Man hu*," meaning "there is a preparation of food here."

Only after the completion of an entire cycle (week) of the *Man*’s descent (and specifically, the two parts of this: its descent during the weekdays, and the negation of its descent on Shabbos) did the knowledge and recognition of the *Man* come to Bnei Yisrael, and they were able to call it by a name (which indicates its content and essence).

This is also the reason why the description of the *Man* ("like coriander seed...") and the command to take it "as a keepsake for your generations" appear in the text only after the narrative regarding the absence and negation of its descent on Shabbos. First, the text concludes the narrative regarding the *Man*’s descent (which ends with the account of the *Man* not descending on Shabbos, as mentioned), then comes the description of the *Man* itself, and finally, the command for the generations.

3. The aforementioned point—that the absence of the *Man*’s descent on Shabbos is not caused by the prohibitions of Shabbos—is understood and necessitated according to Rashi’s commentary on the Torah, which implies that the entire matter of Shabbos was only renewed at the time of the *Man*’s descent.

After Bnei Yisrael had gathered the *Man* for several days, on Friday they gathered a double portion, and then "all the princes of the congregation came and told Moshe". Rashi explains: "They told Moshe, they asked him, 'What is different about today from other days?' From here we learn that Moshe had not yet told them..."

## 87

"...the section of Shabbos that he was commanded to tell them, and it was on Friday, and they prepared..." until they asked this.

This is perplexing, for it is explicitly stated in Rashi earlier that the section of Shabbos had already been given to Israel at Marah. What room was there for the question "What is different about today from other days?"—implying that the princes of the congregation did not know at all about the uniqueness of Friday, on which they were obligated to prepare Shabbos food, etc., until they wondered "What is different about today," and Moshe had to tell them and introduce, "Tomorrow is a rest day, a holy Shabbos to Hashem; bake what you will bake... and cook what you will cook"?

However, this question is answered precisely by Rashi’s own language, where he wrote that at Marah, "He gave them some sections of the Torah to occupy themselves with, such as Shabbos..." That is to say, at Marah they were not commanded (according to Rashi) regarding Shabbos in practice, but were only given the section of Shabbos to occupy themselves with (studying the section), as there was not yet a matter of observing Shabbos in practice (only studying the section of Shabbos in the Torah)—and therefore they asked, "What is different about today from other days?".

Based on this, one can also answer another incomprehensible matter: in the continuation of the narrative, it is told that even after Moshe informed Bnei Yisrael that "Tomorrow is a rest day, a holy Shabbos to Hashem," and that "(the *Man*) will not be in it", "some of the people went out to gather".

Seemingly, the departure to the field to gather *Man* was certainly with vessels to bring the *Man* home, and thus they violated the prohibition of carrying (*hotza'ah*) by this departure?

One cannot answer that this is the intent of Hashem’s words to Moshe, "How long do you refuse to keep My commandments and My laws? See that Hashem has given you the Shabbos... let no man go out of his place on the seventh day," meaning that they violated "to keep My commandments"—because the plain meaning of the verse implies that only then, after they went out to gather, were they commanded, "Let no man go out of his place," that they should not go out to gather *Man* on Shabbos (for in the previous verses it was only stated that the *Man* would not be in it, but there was no explicit prohibition to go out to gather). Thus, Bnei Yisrael did not violate a command of Hashem.

[The intent of the statement "How long do you refuse..." is not that Bnei Yisrael violated a command of Hashem, but only that they lacked faith; since they saw that Hashem gave them a double portion on Friday, stating the reason explicitly that on the seventh day "you will not find it in the field... it will not be in it", there was no place to go out to the field to find *Man*.]

It is puzzling: since Moshe had already told them that "Tomorrow is a holy Shabbos to Hashem," they were forbidden to go out to gather *Man* on Shabbos due to the prohibition of carrying (and even if they did not find it in the field, they took out the vessels, as mentioned)?

Based on the above, one can say: according to the *peshat* (plain meaning), the command regarding the observance of Shabbos in its entirety did not come until the Giving of the Torah. What was said here regarding the *Man*, "Tomorrow is a rest day, a holy Shabbos to Hashem," is not a command regarding the observance of Shabbos in its entirety, but only regarding its observance in matters pertaining to the gathering and preparation of the *Man*. Therefore, since Bnei Yisrael were initially told only about the prohibition of baking and cooking, they were not (yet) forbidden from carrying from one domain to another, etc., and were permitted by law to go out, etc., and only when Hashem explicitly said, "Let no man go out of his place," were they forbidden in this.

## 88

4. We have learned that the interpretation is not that the prohibitions of Shabbos caused the *Man* not to descend on Shabbos (for at the time the *Man* began to descend, Israel had not yet been forbidden from this Shabbos labor), but rather the opposite: the content of the *Man* is what caused the commandment of Shabbos to be given to Israel (at least regarding the labors pertaining to the gathering and preparation of the *Man*).

Simply, one could explain this based on Rashi’s commentary on the verse, "Behold, I will rain down for you bread from the heavens... that I may test them, whether they will walk in My Torah..."—"if they will keep the commandments dependent upon it, not to leave over from it and not to go out on Shabbos to gather." Thus, one of the tests of the *Man* (which Hashem rained down from the heavens "that I may test them") was regarding the matter of gathering it on Shabbos.

But this itself requires explanation: what is the logic for the *Man*’s test being specifically regarding the matter of Shabbos? And especially (according to the above) that this would be the reason why Shabbos was given to Israel at that time.

It must be that the content of the *Man* is connected with the content of Shabbos.

The explanation for this is:
Earlier, regarding the verse, "In the morning, you shall see the glory of Hashem," Rashi explains that "the bread that you asked for, for the sake of its descent in the morning, you will see the glory of the light of His countenance, that He will bring it down to you in a way of affection in the morning, so that there is time to prepare it, with dew from above and dew from below, as if placed in a box."

From this it is understood that beyond the general innovation in the giving of the *Man*—that it was "bread from the heavens,"

## 89

arriving to Bnei Yisrael without any toil (at least without the toil required for bread from the earth)—there is this as well: that Hashem gave it in a manner of "way of affection," to minimize as much as possible the toil of Bnei Yisrael in obtaining this food.

Thus, the definition of the *Man* is one with the definition of Shabbos—for *Man* is the bread that arrives to Bnei Yisrael in a manner of rest, tranquility, and pleasure, without toil and exertion on their part.

Therefore, the perfection of the affection in the giving of the *Man* is that, together with the giving of the *Man*, Hashem gave Bnei Yisrael the Shabbos, in the manner of "See that Hashem has given you the Shabbos," "See with your own eyes that Hashem in His glory is warning you about the Shabbos, for a miracle was performed every Friday to give you bread for two days"—meaning, that their food was given to them in its entirety on Friday so that on Shabbos they would have complete rest, without any toil or worry regarding their food for that day.

5. Based on this, the continuation of the verses is understood: only after the narrative regarding the matter of Shabbos ("So the people rested on the seventh day")—then "they called its name *Man*."

For this name indicates the unlimited pleasure within it, as "each one of Israel would taste whatever he desired" without limitation—which is why it cannot be described with a limited title, and should only be called "*Man*," "a preparation of food"—and the recognition of the "pleasure" in the *Man* reached its perfection only after "the people rested on the seventh day."

The text continues to emphasize the greatness of the pleasure in the *Man*—"and its taste was like a wafer made with honey" (for only then did they feel the complete pleasure in the *Man*).

And afterwards comes the command to keep it "as a keepsake for your generations," for in the "keepsake for your generations" it is relevant to know that the *Man* was given in such a way that not only were there minimal toils during the six weekdays, but furthermore, when Shabbos arrived, Hashem prepared their food from Friday, in such a way that on Shabbos they had

## 90

complete rest and tranquility, "So the people rested on the seventh day." In this is the perfection of the knowledge and recognition (even to the point of sight—"so that they may see the bread") that Hashem can sustain Israel in a way that they do not need to engage in any labor and can observe (occupy themselves with) Torah in complete rest.

6. The aforementioned explanation regarding the connection of the *Man* to the day of Shabbos is in agreement with the words of the Zohar, that even though the *Man* did not descend on Shabbos, this is only in its descent and transmission down into this physical world, but above it is the opposite—that the blessing of the *Man* was specifically on the day of Shabbos, and from it all the days are blessed, for through this blessing on the day of Shabbos, the *Man* descended below during the six weekdays that followed.

Seemingly, this requires explanation: since "it was sanctified with the *Man* that it did not descend at all on Shabbos"—why is its blessing above specifically on the day of Shabbos?

According to what was said above, it is understood: since the content of the *Man* is the influence of food in a manner of pleasure, "with a shining countenance... in a way of affection", therefore the appropriate time for the blessing on this influence is specifically on the day of Shabbos, which is the time of rest and pleasure.

7. Now, in addition to the fact that the entire Torah is eternal, one can say that the fact that the physical *Man* is "a keepsake for your generations," eternal (and as the Sages say, it was hidden with the Ark)—contains a special emphasis that its content exists always in every single one of Israel. That is to say, even when a person’s food is "bread from the earth," and in a way that he himself must toil in it (and as the statement goes: many did like Rabbi Yishmael—that one must "gather your grain..."—and it succeeded in their hands)—nevertheless, even in this food, the content of the *Man* exists.

The matter is this:
It is known the difference between Israel and the nations: to the nations of the world, an abundance of influence is bestowed, for with them the influence is not calculated and comes to them "for free—from the *mitzvos*", without (spiritual) *avodah*, and therefore the influence is in abundance, since it is not dependent on *avodah*. Not so the influence to Israel; it is a measured influence, for it is bestowed according to their *avodah*, as it is written, "If you walk in My statutes... I will give your rains in their time." And since the influence is calculated, it is therefore a limited and measured influence, according to the *avodah* of Israel.

However, the abundance of influence that the nations have is only an abundance in quantity, but there is no pleasure in this influence, in the language of the Tanya, that Hashem bestows vitality upon them only "like one who throws over his shoulder to his enemy, not according to His will." Not so the influence to the Children of Israel; although it is a measured influence, in this influence there is "pleasure," including the pleasure from above, that Hashem takes pleasure from this influence to the Children of Israel, His close ones, and "in the light of the King’s countenance is life."

This is also the merit of Israel, that they choose Hashem, meaning that they forgo the abundance of influence that they could receive like the nations of the world, and choose to receive their vitality and influence from holiness specifically, even though it is a measured influence, since only in this influence is there the pleasure from above, "in the light of the King’s countenance is life."

## 91

8. This merit in the influence to Israel finds expression and revelation in the matter of the *Man*:
On one hand, there is no abundance in quantity in the *Man*; on the contrary, the *Man* was given in the most precise measurement, only for one day, and only an *omer* per head; "he who gathered much had nothing over, and he who gathered little had no lack". But on the other hand, this *omer* was given "with a shining countenance... in a way of affection," in which Bnei Yisrael saw "the glory of the light of His countenance" (for in the light of the King’s countenance is life).

And just as it is from the "above" perspective, that in the influence from holiness the pleasure from above is enclothed—so too it is from the perspective of Israel (the recipients of the influence), that the influence is received by them in a manner of pleasure. As it came in revelation and simply in the *Man*, that beyond the fact that it arrived to them without toil, they would taste in the *Man* any taste they desired, and furthermore, that together with the *Man*, precious stones and pearls descended for them, whose (primary) purpose is nothing but for pleasure.

Therefore, the *Man* did not descend on Shabbos (even though the blessing of the *Man* above is specifically on Shabbos, as mentioned), because the purpose of the enclothement of pleasure in this influence is to effect in Israel the matter of pleasure, and therefore specifically through the fact that there is a blessing on the influence of the *Man* on Shabbos, it causes it not to descend below to Israel on the day of Shabbos, in such a way that the rest of Israel on the day of Shabbos will be in complete perfection.

This is one of the explanations for the difference between Israel and the nations on Shabbos—that Bnei Yisrael are obligated in the observance of Shabbos, while a non-Jew who observes Shabbos is liable for death: for Israel, since a primary matter in their influence is the matter of pleasure, it therefore causes and brings the pleasure of the person, a day of rest and pleasure [and likewise the opposite: in order for a Jew to be a vessel to receive this influence, there must be the matter of resting from labor]; not so for the nations, for whom Hashem bestows influence "like one who throws... not according to His will," the matter of "will" and rest is negated for them, but rather exertion and toil specifically.

This is also the eternal lesson from the matter of the *Man* for all generations ("a keepsake for your generations")—that for the Children of Israel, the value of the influence that comes from holiness must be held dear, which is in the manner of "in the light of the King’s countenance is life," even if because of this they must forgo the abundance of influence that comes from "throwing over the shoulder." And specifically through this, they reach the true rest and pleasure,

## 92

and the little (in physicality) at first is immediately blessed to satiety, until they merit from the outset—abundant sustenance, an abundance in physical influences, until the abundance of influence that will be "at that time," as explained, explicitly stated, and detailed by the Rambam at the end and conclusion of his book—and speedily in our days, literally.


## יתרו

## Sicha 1

### Sicha 1

A. Regarding the verse, "Thus shall you say to the House of Jacob and tell the Children of Israel," the Mechilta states: "'Thus shall you say to the House of Jacob' — these are the women; 'and tell the Children of Israel' — these are the men. Another interpretation: 'Thus shall you say to the House of Jacob' — speak to the women in a soft language, tell them the *rashei devarim* (general principles); 'and tell the Children of Israel' — and be precise with them."

This requires clarification: The language of the Mechilta ("Another interpretation...") implies that these are two distinct explanations. Yet, this is puzzling: What are the two interpretations of the verse? These seem to be two separate matters. First, it defines who "the House of Jacob" and "the Children of Israel" are—"these are the women... these are the men"—and subsequently, it defines the manner of speech: to the House of Jacob, in a soft language, and to the Children of Israel, to be precise with them.

In Midrash Rabbah on this verse, it is stated: "'Thus shall you say to the House of Jacob' — these are the women; He said to him: Tell them the *rashei devarim* that they are able to hear. 'And tell the Children of Israel' — these are the men; He said to him: Tell them the *dikdukei devarim* (detailed minutiae) that they are able to hear."

At first glance, it appears that this aligns with the second interpretation in the Mechilta (or a synthesis of both).

One must understand the intent of the Midrash: (1) Why does it omit the phrase "in a soft language"? (2) Why does it add "that they are able to hear" (and similarly adds "that they are able to hear" regarding the men)?

Seemingly, the Mechilta and the Midrash disagree on what Moshe told the women. According to the Midrash, he told them only "general principles," whereas according to the Mechilta, he told them (also) "soft" words.
