As the commentaries explain, this refers to words of appeasement—that is, explaining the virtue of the *mitzvos* and the reward for their fulfillment, etc.

[It should be added that Rashi’s commentary on the Torah implies that "soft language" includes two matters: both words of appeasement, etc., and "general principles," which are also called "soft language" because they are easy to understand.

Rashi’s words on the verse are: "'To the House of Jacob' — these are the women; say to them in a soft language. 'And tell the Children of Israel' — explain punishments and details to the males, words as hard as sinews." This means: 1) He did not specify the meaning of "soft language"; 2) Regarding "tell the Children of Israel," he wrote "punishments and details," which suggests that he interprets "soft language" for the women as including both matters: a) The opposite of punishments, meaning he spoke of the reward for *mitzvos*; b) The opposite of "details"—only general statements and speech, the generalities of the *mitzvos*.]

We must clarify the reason for the disagreement between the Mechilta and the Midrash as to whether he told the women only "general principles" or (also) "soft" words regarding the reward for *mitzvos*, etc.

Furthermore, it should be noted that the Midrash begins with "these are the women" and then details "tell them general principles, etc." (and similarly regarding "tell the Children of Israel," it first defines "these are the men" and then details the content of the speech—"tell them detailed minutiae"), whereas the Mechilta (in the second interpretation) begins with the content of the speech—"in a soft language, etc."—and concludes that this applies to "the women."

B. The core of the explanation is as follows:

The two interpretations in the Mechilta differ regarding the reason why the matters of Torah were stated in two ways—"say" (*tomer*) and "tell" (*tagid*). According to the first interpretation, the reason for this division is the recipients of the Torah: "these are the women... these are the men." The difference in the nature of the souls of women and men brought about two modes (types) of transmitting the words of Torah. In contrast, according to the second interpretation, the division in the mode of transmission is rooted in the *cheftza* (the essence) of the words of Torah itself. It is required by the Torah that one must first transmit the "general principles" of Torah, and only afterwards the "details." The verse then adds that these two types of Torah matters are divided into two types among the Children of Israel, the recipients of the Torah: "Thus shall you say ('soft language... general principles') to the House of Jacob" (the women), and "tell the Children of Israel and be precise with them."

Based on this, it can be said that the Midrash does not align (as the superficial reading suggests) with the second interpretation in the Mechilta. On the contrary, it interprets and explains the first interpretation of the Mechilta: the reason for this division, "these are the women... these are the men," is because it depends on what they are able to hear. That is to say, from the perspective of the Torah itself, there is no necessity for this division; rather, since the Torah must be transmitted to the Children of Israel—men and women—Moshe divided the transmission of the Torah to each group according to what it is able to hear.

There is no contradiction here from the fact that the Midrash also uses the same term as the Mechilta—"general principles"—implying that the same things were said to the women in both sources. For the "general principles" in the Midrash are not the same as the "general principles" in the Mechilta.

C. Explaining the matter:

There are two aspects to the definition of "general principles":

1) "General principles" in the sense of the Sages' teaching, "A person should always teach his student in a concise way." A Rav teaching his student must condense the entire length of the debate and the multitude of details into "general principles"—concise words—so that the student can grasp them.

2) "General principles" that are, on the contrary, the general rule from which all the details of the matter branch out. This is similar to the language of the Mishnah, which is "a short statement that includes many matters," where all the details of the debate in the Gemara are hinted at in the language of the Mishnah.

We find this also at the Giving of the Torah: initially, the Ten Commandments were given, which are the generalities of the entire Torah, for "all 613 *mitzvos* are included in the Ten Commandments." Within this itself, we heard only the first two commandments, "I am" and "You shall have no other," from the mouth of the Almighty, for they are "the generalities of the entire Torah" ("I am" includes all 248 positive *mitzvos*, and "You shall have no other" includes all 365 negative *mitzvos"). This is the order of the Giving of the Torah: it begins with the statement of the "generalities," the "general principles," in which all the details are included.

[Based on this, one can explain what the Sages said, that initially the Holy One, Blessed be He, said all the Ten Commandments in one utterance, and only afterwards were all the commandments stated individually, "as He returned and explained each commandment by itself." Seemingly, this is puzzling: since He returned and explained each commandment by itself, what was the reason for needing to say them first in one utterance, which the ear cannot hear? Rather, this is the order of the Giving of the Torah from the Holy One, Blessed be He: first there is the "great general rule" of the entire Torah, where the entire Torah is only "one utterance," and afterwards comes the detailing of the Torah: first to the Ten Commandments, each commandment by itself, and afterwards to the 613 *mitzvos*, and within that—the multitude of the details of the *mitzvos*, until "there are no number of maidens."]

This is the difference between the "general principles" in the Mechilta and the "general principles" in the Midrash: both speak of the "general rule" in the Torah. However, the Midrash refers to the generalities of the Torah "that they [the women] are able to hear," meaning concise words that the student can receive. Since women have a "shortened" (quick/intuitive) intellect, they are told only "general principles" and not the multitude of details. In contrast, the "general principles" in the Mechilta refers to "general principles" in their essence—the general rules of the Torah from which all the details of the Torah branch out [and according to the Mechilta, the general rules of the Torah belong specifically to the women, as will be explained].

D. Accordingly, there is a fundamental difference between the Mechilta and the Midrash regarding the manner in which women relate to the words of Torah:

According to the Midrash, women were given only "what they are able to hear," meaning only the matters that pertain to them. In contrast, according to the Mechilta, which does not mention the condition "that they are able to hear," women were given the essence of the Torah and its generalities—the "general principles" from which the details told to the men branch out.

It can be said that this has a practical implication regarding positive time-bound *mitzvos* from which women are exempt (as well as Torah study, from which women are exempt):

According to the Midrash, these matters were not given to them from the outset; they are not commanded in them at all, because they were only given "what they are able to hear."

In contrast, according to the Mechilta, since they were given "general principles"—the generalities of the entire Torah—they have a connection to the entire Torah. The exemption is only regarding their fulfillment in action, but the giving of the Torah and its *mitzvos*—all the Torah and its *mitzvos* (even positive time-bound *mitzvos*)—were given to women as well. The fact that they are not obligated in their performance does not diminish the giving and the receiving at all.

This is further clarified by what the Arizal wrote: "When the male performs the *mitzvah*, there is no need for the women to perform it separately, for she is already included with him at the time he performs that *mitzvah*... but regarding negative *mitzvos*, etc." He concludes, "And this is the secret of what the Sages said, 'His wife is like his own body,'" as is also found in the Zohar that the man and the woman are each a "half-body." [This matter applies not only to married women but also to the daughters of Israel who are not yet married, for since a man and a woman together are one soul, and each one individually is a "half-body," the action of the man is effective for his "second half," the woman, even before the two halves are joined together.]

E. Based on this, we can explain why only the Mechilta precedes with "in a soft language," whereas the Midrash does not: "Soft language" refers to words of appeasement, as mentioned above. It can be said that this refers to the very fact that they were told they belong to the "general principles," which is the essence and the generalities of the Torah. [Based on this, what is written in the Mechilta, "In a soft language, say the general principles," means that the saying of "general principles" is the "soft language," contrary to what was suggested in section A.] Therefore, according to the Mechilta, in this order, the virtue of women over men is evident, for only to them are the "general principles"—the general rules of the Torah—spoken. Thus, the Mechilta emphasizes that he spoke to the women in a "soft language," the virtue of women over men. In contrast, according to the Midrash, it is the opposite: "Thus shall you say to the House of Jacob" indicates the contraction and the descent of the words of Torah to women, for the words of Torah are not revealed to them as they are to men because they are not able to hear as much, and therefore the Midrash omits this expression.

Based on this, the continuation of the Midrash is also understood. After this interpretation, it continues, "Another interpretation: Why to the women first? Because they are zealous in *mitzvos*." Seemingly, this is difficult: the first interpretation does not mention at all that women precede men, so what is the intent of the Midrash's "Another interpretation: Why to the women first?" Based on the above, it can be said that the intent of the Midrash is that the difficulty in the first interpretation of the Midrash is: since they are told only what they are able to hear (which is less than the men), then "why to the women first?" Therefore, the Midrash explains that there is a matter in which the women take precedence—"because they are zealous in *mitzvos*."

However, according to the second interpretation in the Mechilta, the reason for the women's precedence over the men is explained simply: this is the order of the transmission of the words of Torah, that first come the generalities of the Torah ("general principles"), and afterwards the details (as with the Ten Commandments, as mentioned above).

F. However, this requires explanation: Why, according to the Mechilta, were the "general principles"—the "generalities of the Torah"—said specifically to the women and not to the men?

It can be said that this is in accordance with a general virtue we find in women regarding the men in the matter of the service of Hashem in general. We see that among women, there is more of a revelation of faith in Hashem and fear of Hashem. Although the reason for this in the revealed sense is because "their intellect is not as strong (therefore) they are closer to accepting with (simple) faith and they act without inquiry," the root of the matter is that faith shines within them as it is from the perspective of Hashem.

Perhaps it can be said that for this reason, there is a difference in the matter of lineage: the essence and the general identity of Israel depend specifically on the mother—one born to a Jewish mother is a Jew (even when the father is not Jewish)—and only the details and the divisions within the Children of Israel themselves, such as Kohen, Levi, and Yisrael, depend on the father's (family).

Since the Torah and *mitzvos* were given specifically to Israel, therefore, to the mother, upon whom the holiness of Israel depends, there was a special giving of the generalities of the Torah—"general principles"—which pertain to faith in Hashem and fear of Hashem, etc. (which are the generalities of the entire Torah, and in the language of the verse, "And Hashem commanded us to do all these statutes to fear Hashem..."). And to the father, upon whom the divisions and the specific levels of Israel depend, were given specifically the "details"—the particulars of the Torah and *mitzvos*.

It can be said that this is the very reason why women are exempt from the performance of positive time-bound *mitzvos*. For regarding the *mitzvos* themselves, the negative *mitzvos* and the positive *mitzvos* that are not time-bound were not drawn down and brought into the details of time limitations like the positive time-bound *mitzvos*. Therefore, women, because they belong uniquely to the generalities of the Torah and *mitzvos*, are obligated only in the negative *mitzvos* and the positive *mitzvos* that are not time-bound.

[Perhaps it can be said that this is the content of the Arizal’s explanation that "there is no need for the women to perform it separately, for she is already included with him at the time he performs that *mitzvah*," because this matter—that in the engagement with Torah and *mitzvos*, the action in the details of time limitations pertains to the second "half-body"—the male part of the soul.]

It is written, "As in the days of your exodus from the land of Egypt, I will show you wonders." Just as it was at the Exodus from Egypt, where in the merit of the righteous women of that generation the Children of Israel were redeemed from Egypt, and through this they received the entire Torah ("When you bring the people out of Egypt, you shall serve the Almighty on this mountain"), so too it is in the future redemption that is to come: in the merit of the righteous women, we will go out of this exile and merit to learn the Torah of Mashiach. He will teach Torah to the entire people, from the smallest to the greatest, and his teaching will be in a manner of "seeing." He will teach the "great general rule" of the Torah, and it is known that in the future, the essence of the inner dimension of the Torah will be revealed, speedily in our days, truly.

## Sicha 2

### 99

**A.** The Rambam writes in *Hilchos Shabbos* : "It is a positive commandment from the Torah to sanctify the day of Shabbos with words, as it is stated, 'Remember the day of Shabbos to sanctify it' — meaning, remember it with a remembrance of praise and sanctification. One must remember it at its entry and at its departure: at its entry with *Kiddush*, and at its departure with *Havdalah*."

The *Maggid Mishneh* writes that from the words of the Rambam, "it is clearly evident that he holds that *Havdalah* is also a matter of Torah law, and that everything is included in 'Remember'."

Regarding the source for the fact that *Havdalah* is a commandment from the Torah, we find several opinions:
The *Maggid Mishneh* there (and elsewhere) writes that we derive it from what is written, "And to distinguish between the holy and the profane".

The *Sefer Mitzvos Gadol* (Smag) writes that the two remembrances are learned from two verses — "One at its entry, which is 'Remember,' and one at its departure, which is 'And you shall remember,' which is written in the section of 'Observe', etc."

The *Migdal Oz* explains the connection of the two remembrances to the two commandments of "Remember" and "Observe" (which were spoken in a single utterance) — that "the term 'Remember' is a positive commandment to remember it at its entry, by sanctifying it with words... and the term 'Observe' applies to its departure, that one should not conduct oneself with the sanctity of the holy in a manner of the profane until one distinguishes it and establishes it, etc."

However, from the fact that the Rambam cited only the verse "Remember the day of Shabbos," it implies that he holds that *Havdalah* is also included in the positive commandment of "Remember the day of Shabbos to sanctify it." This is also explicitly stated in *Sefer HaMitzvos* in the commandment of *Kiddush* : "That He commanded us to sanctify the Shabbos and to speak words at its entry and its departure, etc., and this is His statement, may He be blessed, 'Remember the day of Shabbos to sanctify it,' meaning... [sanctify it at its entry and sanctify it at its departure], meaning *Havdalah*, which

## 100

is also a part of the remembrance of Shabbos and the fulfillment of the commandment."

Seemingly, this requires explanation: what is the connection of *Havdalah* to the matter of "Remember the day of Shabbos to sanctify it"?

**B.** In *Sefer HaMitzvos* there, he wrote: "That He commanded us to sanctify the Shabbos and to speak words at its entry and its departure, in which we will remember... the holiness of the day and its stature, and its distinction from the other days that preceded it and that follow it."

From these words, it is understood that the reason *Havdalah* is also included in the positive commandment of sanctifying the day is that the definition of the positive commandment of sanctifying the day is that one remembers "its stature and its distinction from the other days that preceded it and that follow it." Therefore, *Havdalah* is included in the positive commandment of "Remember the day of Shabbos to sanctify it," because in *Havdalah* as well, one remembers "its stature and its distinction from other days" (from the days that follow it).

However, on the face of it, it is forced to impose this interpretation upon the *Yad* (Mishneh Torah), where he defines the positive commandment as "remember it with a remembrance of praise and sanctification," and he does not mention that the remembrance is to distinguish it from other days (preceding or following it).

Furthermore: In *Sefer HaMitzvos*, he wrote immediately at the beginning of his words, "That He commanded us to sanctify the Shabbos and to speak words at its entry and its departure," which means he includes the matter of "speaking words at its entry and its departure" within the definition of the commandment of sanctifying the Shabbos. The reason, simply put, is that according to the Rambam's explanation in *Sefer HaMitzvos*, the definition of the commandment necessitates mentioning the "stature and distinction" of the day of Shabbos at its entry and departure (in order to distinguish it from the days preceding it and following it).

But in the *Yad*, he divided the times of remembrance into separate clauses; first, he wrote the definition of the commandment: "A positive commandment from the Torah to sanctify the day of Shabbos with words, as it is stated, 'Remember'... meaning, remember it with a remembrance of praise and sanctification." Afterward, he adds in a separate clause: "And one must remember it at its entry and at its departure; at its entry with *Kiddush* of the day, and at its departure with *Havdalah*."

If he held in the *Yad* as he explains in *Sefer HaMitzvos*, he should have included the times of remembrance of *Kiddush* and *Havdalah* within the explanation of the definition of the commandment (similar to his language in *Sefer HaMitzvos*): A positive commandment to sanctify the day of Shabbos with words at its entry with *Kiddush* and at its departure with *Havdalah*.

**C.** We must preface this by explaining the definition of the positive commandment of "Remember the day of Shabbos to sanctify it," which can be explained in several ways:

1) The definition of the positive commandment is "to sanctify it," meaning that through the act of speech, one sanctifies the Shabbos. Similar to the sanctification of the month (*Kiddush HaChodesh*) and the sanctification of the Jubilee year, which are sanctified (through speech), and this is something done at their entry, once. Likewise, in this manner, one sanctifies the Shabbos with words at its entry.

According to this, *Havdalah* does not contain the element of the sanctification of the day of Shabbos; rather, it is an entirely different entity and object.
It could be said that this is the reason for the opinions (mentioned above in section A) that there is a special derivation for the obligation of *Havdalah* — for even though according to them *Havdalah* is from the Torah and belongs to the commandment of "Remember," it is nevertheless not the essence of the commandment (as the *Migdal Oz* puts it: "The *Kiddush* is the essence, and *Havdalah* is secondary to it").

2) Its definition is "Remember the day of Shabbos to sanctify it," meaning to remember through speech the matter of its holiness and its distinction (for holiness means distinction) from other days — as mentioned above from *Sefer HaMitzvos* — and thereby to establish its boundaries before it and after it.

According to this, *Kiddush* and *Havdalah* are two parts of one commandment, such that the fulfillment of the commandment of "Remember the day of Shabbos to sanctify it" occurs specifically when one remembers its distinction both from the days before it (at its entry) and from the days after it (at its departure).
However, even according to this approach, the content of the two parts of the commandment — *Kiddush* and *Havdalah* — differs from one another, in that the remembrance of the "distinction" of the day of Shabbos at its entry is through the *Kiddush* of the day (that one is now entering a holy day), whereas the remembrance of its "distinction" at its departure is through the *Havdalah* formula (that one is departing from the holy to the profane).

3) The definition of the positive commandment is only the remembrance of its holiness through speech, in the language of the Rambam in the *Yad*: "Remember it with a remembrance of praise and sanctification" — in whatever manner that may be. According to this approach, we find that its remembrance in *Kiddush* and its remembrance in *Havdalah* constitute one name and one definition — "a remembrance of praise and sanctification".

**D.** From the practical differences between these approaches, we can say:

1) According to the third approach, there is room to say that even if one recited the *Kiddush* formula of Shabbos on *Motzaei Shabbos*, one fulfills the commandment of *Havdalah* (and likewise, conversely, by reciting the *Havdalah* formula at the entry of Shabbos, one fulfills the commandment of *Kiddush*), because the essence of the commandment is not concerned with the specific formula one recites (though it is a matter of inquiry whether one fulfills the Rabbinic obligation since one deviates from the structure established by the Sages). This is not the case according to the first and second approaches, where the content of the *Havdalah* formula is of the very definition of the commandment.

2) When one did not perform *Havdalah* at all — according to the second approach, where both together constitute a single object of "remembrance of Shabbos," it could be said that there is a deficiency in the fulfillment of the commandment of *Kiddush*, the positive commandment of "Remember the day of Shabbos to sanctify it," which is not the case according to the first and third approaches.

3) Whether women are obligated in *Havdalah*: According to the second and third approaches, where *Havdalah* is of the essence of the commandment of "Remember the day of Shabbos to sanctify it," it is simple that they are obligated in *Havdalah*, "just as they are obligated in *Kiddush* from the Torah (because 'Remember' is equated to 'Observe')" ;
This is not the case according to the first approach — it could be said that it depends on the opinions (mentioned above in section A) regarding the source of the obligation of *Havdalah*: according to the opinion of the *Migdal Oz* that the obligation of *Havdalah* belongs to the commandment of "Observe," they are obligated, "because 'Remember' is equated to 'Observe'" ;

## 102

but if you say that we derive it from "to distinguish between the holy and the profane," there is room to say that women are exempt from it, because *Havdalah* is a different name and definition and has no connection to the definition of *Kiddush* at its entry.
[Similar to the explanation of the *Admur HaZaken* regarding the two opinions (according to the view that *Havdalah* is only Rabbinic) — that "there is one who says that women are exempt from it just as they are exempt from all positive commandments that are time-bound... and even though in all matters of Shabbos, man and woman are equal," this is only in matters that are "because of the remembrance of Shabbos or its observance, but *Havdalah* is not a matter of the observance of Shabbos, rather it is a separate matter that the Sages established to distinguish between holy and profane, and they found a support for it from the Torah, as it is stated, 'to distinguish between the holy and the profane.' And there are those who disagree with this and say that *Havdalah* is a matter of the remembrance of Shabbos and its holiness, that we remember the distinction between its holiness and the profane, and therefore women are obligated in them."]

**E.** Based on all of the above, the reason the Rambam in the *Yad* divided the matter into three clauses is well understood: (1) "A positive commandment to sanctify the day of Shabbos with words... meaning, remember it with a remembrance of praise and sanctification," (2) "And one must remember it at its entry and at its departure," (3) "At its entry with *Kiddush* of the day and at its departure with *Havdalah*." He did not combine them to say, "And one must remember it at its entry with *Kiddush* of the day and at its departure with *Havdalah*" (similar to his language in *Sefer HaMitzvos*).

This is because for the Rambam, these are three details: (1) The definition of the commandment, which is the essence of the remembrance. (2) The times of remembrance (at its entry and at its departure), and (3) the content of the remembrances (*Kiddush* of the day and *Havdalah*): a) The essence of the definition of the commandment is only "remember it with a remembrance of praise and sanctification," and the time and the formula (content) of the remembrance are not part of the body of the commandment of remembrance ; b) the time of the commandment — "and one must remember it at its entry and at its departure"; c) the formula appropriate for the time — "at its entry with *Kiddush* of the day and at its departure with *Havdalah*."

[This is unlike what he wrote in *Sefer HaMitzvos*, that the two remembrances (at its entry and at its departure) constitute one commandment, as discussed at length above; and this is also implied in the language of the *Chinuch*, who wrote, "To speak words on the day of Shabbos at its entry and likewise at its departure, that they should contain a remembrance of the greatness of the day and its stature and its distinction for praise from the other days before it and after it, as it is stated, 'Remember the day of Shabbos to sanctify it,' meaning, remember it with a remembrance of holiness and greatness," which includes the time of the commandment within the definition of the commandment itself.]

And based on this, what the Rambam wrote (in the continuation of the chapter) is also clarified: that "a person should sanctify over the cup on the eve of Shabbos while it is still day... and likewise he performs *Havdalah* over the cup while it is still day, even though it is still Shabbos, for the commandment of remembrance is to say it both at the time of its entry and departure, and even before this time, almost" — which proves that he holds that this is not merely a permission for one who needs to (such as one who needed to travel before the boundary), but rather that *ab initio* it is permitted to do so (as in *Kiddush* of the day),
because for the Rambam, the content of the matter of *Havdalah* (from the Torah) is not (only) that one distinguishes between Shabbos and the profane in general, but rather (like the definition of *Kiddush*) that one remembers "a remembrance of praise and sanctification" at its departure (just as one remembers at its entry).

**F.** However, the matter still requires explanation: Granted, according to what he wrote in *Sefer HaMitzvos*, the necessity is understood that the commandment of remembrance includes *Havdalah* as well, because since one must sanctify and "distinguish" the day of Shabbos from the days before it and after it, it is necessary that in the commandment of "Remember," etc., there should also be the matter of *Havdalah* on *Motzaei Shabbos*; and all the more so according to the opinions that there is a special derivation that one must remember the matter of *Havdalah* on *Motzaei Shabbos* (except that it is included in the commandment of remembrance of Shabbos because it is attached to it, etc.);
But according to the opinion of the Rambam in the *Yad* (based on what was explained above) — since the content of this commandment ("Remember," etc., "to sanctify it") is "remember it with a remembrance of praise and sanctification" (like the *Kiddush* at the entry of Shabbos), what is the necessity to say that the commandment includes the remembrance of these things also at the time of the departure of Shabbos ?

The explanation for this can be said by prefacing what the *Ramban* wrote: that in this verse ("Remember," etc., "to sanctify it") there are two commandments — the commandment of "Remember" and the commandment of "to sanctify it": the commandment of remembering Shabbos is a constant commandment, to remember the day of Shabbos always, every day, whereas the commandment of sanctification (which is derived from the word "to sanctify it") is the remembrance that is on the day of Shabbos itself.

And behold, even though according to the Rambam the commandment of remembrance and the commandment of sanctification are one and the same, as in his language "remember it with a remembrance of praise and sanctification," it can be said that the Rambam also holds something similar to the words of the *Ramban*, namely, that since "Remember" implies a constant remembrance (as mentioned above), therefore the Rambam holds that there are two parts to this commandment: a part that belongs to Shabbos itself, and a part that belongs to the days following Shabbos. And this is what he wrote, "And one must remember it at its entry and at its departure" — that through the remembrance at its departure, the remembrance of Shabbos is drawn also into the weekdays that follow it. And simply put, that by remembering the holiness of Shabbos at the time one departs from Shabbos to the profane, one remembers the day of Shabbos also during the weekdays.

[It can be said that this is emphasized in the law that the Rambam wrote (in the continuation of the chapter): "And if he did not perform *Havdalah* at night, he performs *Havdalah* the next day, and he performs *Havdalah* until the end of the third day," for from the continuation and the comparison (to what was written before) of "if he did not perform *Kiddush* at night, etc., he performs *Kiddush* throughout the entire day," it is understood that this is not merely a matter of *post facto* and compensation for the obligation (of *Kiddush* and) *Havdalah* that he was supposed to fulfill at its time, but rather that these days are also its time (as in *Kiddush*, where "its time is the entire Shabbos", except that "the essence of the *Kiddush* is at night"), and this is as mentioned above, because the essence of the content of this remembrance is to draw the remembrance of the day of Shabbos into the weekdays that follow Shabbos.

And based on this, the order in the Rambam is justified, that he wrote this law, "if he did not perform *Kiddush* at night, etc., and if he did not perform *Havdalah* at night, etc.," immediately after the formula of *Kiddush* and *Havdalah*, and before (the other) detailed laws of *Kiddush* and *Havdalah* — because this is not a specific law, a compensation for the commandment of remembrance (*Kiddush* and *Havdalah*) of Shabbos, but rather that even then its time of fulfillment continues.]

**G.** This explanation of the Rambam's view is in agreement with what is explained in the books regarding the inner content of the blessing of *Havdalah*, that its matter is to draw the holiness of Shabbos into the weekdays that follow it.

By this, the language of the Sages cited by the Rambam (in *Sefer HaMitzvos*), "Sanctify it at its entry and sanctify it at its departure" (as mentioned in section A), is understood simply, for it should also be interpreted according to the simple meaning of the language, as a matter of drawing down holiness; meaning: just as through the *Kiddush* "at its entry" one draws down the holiness of Shabbos (and through the *Kiddush* at a time before the entry of Shabbos one adds to the holiness of Shabbos — *Tosefes Shabbos*, as it is in the *Mechilta* on the verse "Remember," "Remember it from before it, etc., from here they said one adds, etc.") — in this same manner, the content of "sanctify it at its departure" is not only the mentioning of the stature and holiness of Shabbos at the time of the departure of Shabbos, but the drawing down of the holiness of Shabbos at its departure into the time that follows it.

It can be said that this also fits with the simple interpretation of "to sanctify it," the sanctification of the day of Shabbos itself, which is also the interpretation of "(sanctify it at its entry and) sanctify it at its departure," that even at its departure one sanctifies the Shabbos (and not only the weekdays that follow it). And the reason for this is that through *Havdalah* at the departure of Shabbos, the content of which is to draw the holiness of Shabbos into the weekdays as well — the perfection of the holiness of Shabbos is expressed, the greatness of the holiness of Shabbos and its stature, which has the power to act and to draw down holiness into the days that follow it.

**H.** And through the fulfillment of the commandment of Shabbos — both as it is on Shabbos itself, through cessation from physical labor and the fulfillment of the commandment of "Remember" and the sanctification of Shabbos, and through its effect on the weekdays to draw into them from the holiness of Shabbos, in the style of the Sages that the profane should be made upon the purity of the holy,

in addition to the fact that one must "do all your deeds for the sake of Heaven" and "in all your ways know Him",

"everyone who observes the Shabbos according to its law and honors it and delights in it according to his strength, it is already explained in the tradition that his reward in this world is greater than the reward hidden for the world to come, as it is stated, 'Then you shall delight in Hashem, and I will make you ride upon the high places of the earth, and I will feed you with the heritage of Jacob your father, for the mouth of Hashem has spoken.'"

(From the Sicha of 28 Iyar, 5745)

## 3

### 106

**Sicha 3**

A. It is stated in the Yerushalmi at the end of Maseches Eruvin: "He would say: 'Tongs are made by tongs' [the tongs are only made by means of another pair of tongs — Korban HaEidah]. 'The first pair of tongs, who made it?' [The first pair, who created it? — Korban HaEidah]. 'It was a *beriyah* [a creation from the Six Days of Creation, as it is among the things created on Erev Shabbos at twilight — Korban HaEidah].' Rabbi Chanina said before Rabbi Mana: 'How can you say this?' [i.e., how does this apply to the laws in our Mishnah? — Korban HaEidah]. 'From one pair of tongs, many tongs were learned, and from one *shevus* (prohibition), many *shevusim* were learned.'"

The *Pnei Moshe* explains the connection of this statement to our context: it is a response (by way of allusion) to the *ibya* (dilemma) in the Gemara there, mentioned earlier, regarding the Mishnah’s ruling on removing a *sheretz* (creeping thing) found in the Mikdash: "Rabbi Akiva says: from a place where one is liable for *kares* for intentional violation and a *chatas* for unintentional violation, from there one removes it." The Gemara asks: "If he removed it from a place where one is liable for *kares* and it fell into a place where one is not liable for *kares* — it was already fit to be removed [i.e., it was already fit to be removed initially, and it is obvious that one removes it even from there; rather, the dilemma is:] if he found another one beside it [in the place where it fell, where one is not liable for *kares*], does he remove both [since it was permitted for the first, it is permitted for the second] or does he only remove the one that was fit to be removed [initially]?"

As a response to this dilemma, Rabbi Mana replied "by way of allusion and parable... just as from one pair of tongs they learned to make many tongs, so too here, from one *shevus* they learned many *shevusim*. Since one *shevus* was permitted in the Mikdash—to carry and remove the one that was fit to be removed—he removes the second one with it in the place where it fell as well. For what is the difference? Since it was permitted, many *shevusim* were permitted" (since they are [dealt with] at the same time).

However, this explanation is difficult to accept. On the face of it, it is a very distant allusion, for there is no connection between the reasoning for permitting the second *shevus*—"since it was permitted (one *shevus*), many *shevusim* were permitted" (where the basis of the permission is "since they are at the same time")—and the parable of "tongs are made by tongs" (that from one pair of tongs, many tongs were made).

Furthermore, this explanation does not fit the language of the Yerushalmi, "from one *shevus* they learned many *shevusim*," for there is no matter of "learning" here, only a blanket permission: "since it was permitted for this one, it was permitted for that one as well."

Moreover: according to this interpretation, what is the practical difference (*nafka mina*) regarding the debate over how the first pair of tongs was created? After all, to explain that "from one *shevus* they learned many *shevusim*," it should be sufficient to say that "tongs are made by tongs."

### 107

Especially since there are opinions that it is not necessary to say the first pair of tongs was a *beriyah* by Heaven, because "it is possible that he made it with a mold, etc." Why, then, does the Yerushalmi need to enter into this debate and conclude that the first pair of tongs was a creation by Heaven [when even if it were made by human hands, many tongs would still be made from it]?

B. Now, the source of this statement is (as stated in *Pnei Moshe*) in the Tosefta here, which states: "The laws of Shabbos... are like mountains hanging by a hair; there is little Scripture and many laws, and they have nothing to rely upon. From here Rabbi Yehoshua said: 'Tongs are made by tongs; the first pair of tongs, what was it? Was it not a *beriyah*?'"

It is explicit that the comparison of "tongs are made by tongs" to the matter of *shevus* is that even though there is only "little Scripture" (in the Torah) regarding the *shevus* of Shabbos, nevertheless, from this "little Scripture" emerge "many laws" (to the point that they are like "mountains hanging by a hair," and even more so—"they have nothing to rely upon")—similar to the multitude of tongs, all of which are the result of the first pair of tongs that was created during the Six Days of Creation.

With this, the reason the Tosefta brings the mode of creation of the first pair of tongs—that it is by Heaven—is well understood. For this is the comparison to the *shevus* of Shabbos: the source and essence of the "many laws" of Shabbos is "little Scripture," the Written Torah, which is "by Heaven." From this, "many laws" subsequently branched out, up to the multitude of *shevusin*, decrees, and safeguards of the Sages—similar to the many tongs made by human hands, [deriving] from the power of the first pair of tongs.

Therefore, it seems to me that the Yerushalmi here (which also brought the debate regarding the creation of the first pair of tongs) also intends the same as the Tosefta, and the statement "from one *shevus* they learned, etc." does not refer to a *shevus* of the Sages (as the *Pnei Moshe* explains), but to the matter of *shevus* explicitly stated in the Torah. This is the "little Scripture" ("one *shevus*") from which they learned "many *shevusim*"—the multitude of *shevus* prohibitions of the Sages.

[As the Rambam writes: "It is stated in the Torah: 'You shall rest'—even from things that are not work, one is obligated to rest from them. And there are many things that the Sages prohibited due to *shevus*; some are prohibited because they resemble work, and some are prohibited as a decree lest one come to a prohibition of *skilah*." From the continuation of the Rambam's words, it is understood that the *shevus* prohibitions of the Sages are related to the positive commandment of "You shall rest." As is known, there is a debate regarding the interpretation of the Rambam's words: whether the intention is that the Torah prohibited matters of *shevus* but the matter was entrusted to the Sages, or that all *shevus* prohibitions are of Rabbinic origin and they only relied upon this verse, or that there are matters of *shevus* that are from the Torah and some that are of Rabbinic origin.]

Based on this, it can be said that the words of the Yerushalmi at the end of the Masechta are related to the conclusion of the final Mishnah of the Masechta, which is the statement of Rabbi Shimon: "A place where the Sages permitted you, they gave you from your own; they only permitted you due to *shevus*." The explanation of this statement of Rabbi Shimon (in which cases the Sages permitted *shevus*) depends on the explanation of the relationship between *shevusim* of the Sages (which are "by human hands," "from your own") and the source of the matter of *shevus* in the Torah ("little Scripture"), as follows.

### 108

C. This will be understood by prefacing the words of the Bavli at the end of the Masechta regarding the words of Rabbi Shimon:

"Where does Rabbi Shimon stand? ... For we learned in a Mishnah: 'He who became benighted outside the *techum* (boundary), even by one cubit, shall not enter. Rabbi Shimon says: even fifteen cubits, he may enter, because the surveyors do not measure the distances precisely due to those who err' [i.e., when they measure and mark the place where the two thousand cubits end, 'they do not measure precisely,' but rather place the marker within the *techum*, so that if one goes out, he does not commit a transgression]. The first Tanna says he shall not enter, and Rabbi Shimon says he may enter"—because "they gave you from your own, for he is already within his *techum*" (Rashi).

"'They only permitted you due to *shevus*'—where does he stand? ... For the first Tanna says: 'One may tie it' [a lyre string in the Mikdash that snapped, for the first Tanna holds that one may tie it on Shabbos], and Rabbi Shimon says: 'One may loop it.' The Sages permitted looping, which does not lead to a liability for a *chatas*, but they did not permit tying, which leads to a liability for a *chatas*." That is to say, "here too they did not give you anything but your own, meaning looping, which is a permitted matter" (Tosafos).

That is: the words of Rabbi Shimon, "A place where the Sages permitted you, they gave you from your own; they only permitted you due to *shevus*," refer to two matters in which he disagrees with the first Tanna: (a) the law of "he who became benighted outside the *techum*," where Rabbi Shimon is lenient and says "even fifteen cubits, he may enter," since "they gave you from your own," and (b) the law of a lyre string in the Mikdash that snapped, where Rabbi Shimon is stringent and prohibits tying, because "they only permitted you due to *shevus*," meaning "they only gave you your own," a permitted matter—looping.

In Tosafos there, they explain the reason "why he waited until here" [seemingly: the law of "he who became benighted outside the *techum*" was brought in this Masechta many chapters earlier, and likewise the law of "one may tie a string on Shabbos" was stated in a previous Mishnah—why were the words of Rabbi Shimon (regarding these two laws) stated specifically at the end of the Masechta?]—"because he wanted to conclude with matters of the Mikdash but not the provinces." As the Maharsha explains: "Because Rabbi Shimon only disagrees regarding the Mikdash, he did not want to conclude with all these Rabbinic prohibitions that everyone agrees upon in the provinces, and he waited until here to inform us that Rabbi Shimon disagrees regarding tying a string in the Mikdash and prohibits it."

However, the Maharsha himself rejects this explanation and writes: "All this is forced, to say that he waited until here for this reason, for the disagreement of Rabbi Shimon regarding tying a string was not mentioned explicitly in any Mishnah at all. And above, too, Rabbi Yehuda disagrees with the first Tanna regarding matters of the Mikdash alone, and he did not wait until here."

Therefore, he explains that the words of Rabbi Shimon were stated not only regarding these two laws, but "Rabbi Shimon came to teach a general rule. And therefore... he waited for the statement of Rabbi Shimon until here, which is the end of Masechta Eruvin, and he provided a general rule for all the laws of Masechta Eruvin, in which they were lenient in many places for the sake of a mitzvah and other needs, and because they said to prohibit and they said to permit, for all the laws of Eruvin are only of Rabbinic origin... Therefore, permission was given to the Sages to be lenient in them according to need. And this is what he said: any place where the Sages permitted being lenient in the laws of Eruvin, it is because 'from your own'—what you prohibited, was given to you to be lenient in. This is not the case with Torah prohibitions, where permission was not given to the Sages to be lenient in them. And this matter is explained throughout the Masechta."

[And the Gemara's intention, "Where does Rabbi Shimon stand?", is only "because the language 'any place' implies that it refers (also) to a specific matter in this subject that Rabbi Shimon already said. And he says: regarding 'they permitted you from your own,' we find a specific matter in the words of Rabbi Shimon where he said that even fifteen cubits he may enter. And likewise, regarding 'they only permitted you due to *shevus*,' we find that Rabbi Shimon said a specific matter regarding the prohibition of tying a string."]

### 109

D. And it should be added with greater depth:

Rabbi Shimon's intention is not only to distinguish between Torah and Rabbinic law (that only a Rabbinic *shevus* can be permitted, "because they said to prohibit and they said to permit"), but he comes to distinguish within the Rabbinic laws themselves. There is a *shevus* that is "from your own," and this they permitted; this is not the case for a *shevus* that is not "from your own" [as in the second law in the Bavli—a lyre string in the Mikdash that snapped, where Rabbi Shimon prohibits tying because it leads to a liability for a *chatas*. This is not a matter of a Torah prohibition (because it is a knot that is not intended to be permanent), but a *shevus* that is a decree lest one come to a liability for a *chatas*].

Based on this, it appears that Rabbi Shimon's general rule is not only "a general rule for all the laws of Masechta Eruvin," but is stated regarding the entirety of the laws of Shabbos. Regarding *shevus* prohibitions, we find several types in terms of their relationship to the Torah prohibition, and regarding this, Rabbi Shimon gave the rule that the Sages do not have the power to permit a *shevus* except of this type, which is "from your own."

Explanation of the matter:
In *shevus* prohibitions, there are two general types, as explained in *Tzafnas Paneach* on the Rambam: (a) there are *shevusim* that "the Sages relied upon a Torah-level work," and they have the name of "work" (on a Rabbinic level), (b) matters that the Sages prohibited doing on Shabbos, but these matters do not have the name "work" (even on a Rabbinic level).

It can be said that this is the intention of the aforementioned Tosefta, that the laws of Shabbos are "like mountains hanging by a hair; there is little Scripture and many laws, and they have nothing to rely upon"—for on the face of it, it is puzzling: in Mishnah Chagigah, it distinguishes between "the nullification of vows" and "the laws of Shabbos," that "the nullification of vows fly in the air and have nothing to rely upon," and "the laws of Shabbos... are like mountains hanging by a hair, for they are little Scripture and many laws"—and here the Tosefta says that even the laws of Shabbos "have nothing to rely upon."


## משפטים

## Sicha 1

### 112

a. We learned in the Mishnah: There are four types of guardians: the unpaid guardian, the borrower, the paid guardian, and the renter. The unpaid guardian takes an oath regarding everything; the borrower pays for everything; the paid guardian and the renter take an oath regarding a broken animal, a captured animal, or a dead animal, and they pay for a lost or stolen item.

In the Gemara, it asks: Who is the Tanna who holds there are four guardians? Rav Nachman said: It is Rabbi Meir. Rava said to Rav Nachman: Is there anyone who does not hold of four guardians? (Necessarily, we find four types of guardians, Rashi notes). He replied: This is what I meant to tell you: Who is the Tanna who holds that a renter is like a paid guardian? It is Rabbi Meir (who holds that the renter pays like a paid guardian). If so, there are three, not four! Rav Nachman bar Yitzchak said: There are four guardians, but their legal rulings are three.

This requires clarification:
From the simple phrasing of Rav Nachman, "This is what I meant to tell you," it implies that in his statement, "Who is the Tanna who holds that a renter is like a paid guardian? It is Rabbi Meir," he did not retract what he had said previously, "Who is the Tanna who holds there are four guardians?" Rather, he was clarifying his initial words—"This is what I meant to tell you." In other words, the question, "Who is the Tanna who holds that a renter is like a paid guardian?" is not a separate, new question, but rather an explanation and clarification of the first question, "Who is the Tanna who holds there are four guardians?" (which is resolved as stated above by his answer, "It is Rabbi Meir").

Seemingly, regarding these two questions—the fact that "a renter is like a paid guardian" (meaning the renter does not have a status of his own) results in there being only three guardians, unlike the "Tanna who holds there are four guardians" (as the Gemara continues immediately thereafter, "If so, there are three, not four").

b. One can say regarding this:
In Rashi, the reason for the dispute between Rabbi Meir and Rabbi Yehudah—whether the renter’s status is that of an unpaid guardian or a paid guardian—is explained:
The one who holds that his status is that of an unpaid guardian does so because "he pays the fee for his labor and does not receive a wage for his guarding." In other words, even though the renter derives benefit from the object (by using it), he does not thereby become a paid guardian, because this benefit does not come as a wage for his guarding; since "he pays the fee for his labor," and because "he does not receive a wage for his guarding," he is an unpaid guardian.

The reasoning of the one who holds that his status is that of a paid guardian is: "Since it is with him for his benefit, even though he pays the fee for his labor, he is a paid guardian; for if he did not pay a fee, he would be a borrower and liable for unavoidable accidents; now that he pays a fee, he is not a borrower, but a paid guardian." From Rashi’s language, it is implied that, in essence, the renter should have the status of a borrower, but since "he pays a fee, he is not a borrower, but a paid guardian."

In other words, it does not mean that this authority holds that the renter’s obligation to guard the object is a form of additional payment (beyond the rental fee for his benefit from the object), such that the benefit of the object is the receipt of a wage for his guarding, thus making him a paid guardian. Rather, the renter’s entire obligation is because the object is with him "for his benefit," which makes him like a borrower (and only because "he pays a fee, he is not [fully] a borrower, but [only] a paid guardian").

Explaining the matter: There is a fundamental difference between the cause of the obligations of guardians (unpaid and paid) and the obligations of a borrower. A guardian—as the name implies—is one who has accepted upon himself and obligated himself to guard another’s object until he returns it in its entirety to its owner. [The reason for the difference between the obligations of an unpaid guardian and a paid guardian is that a paid guardian, who receives a wage for his guarding, is obligated to guard the object with superior care, to guard even against theft and loss, whereas an unpaid guardian, who receives no wage for his guarding, is satisfied with lesser care and is exempt from theft and loss.] The obligations of a borrower, however, do not come (only) from his obligation to guard the object of the lender; rather, it is (also) as if he has purchased the object entirely, and consequently, he is obligated to return it (similar to a loan). Since "all the benefit is his," he has purchased the object to the extent of being obligated even for unavoidable accidents (which cannot be guarded against, and for which there is no definition of "guarding").

This is Rashi’s intent in explaining the view of the one who holds that a renter is like a paid guardian—"Since it is with him for his benefit": The basis of the renter’s obligation (according to this view) is not because of his obligation to guard the object, but because the object is with him (not for the sake of guarding the object for the depositor, but on the contrary—) "for his benefit," so that he can use it—like a borrower. However, since "he pays a fee, he is not a borrower, but a paid guardian," meaning that since "all the benefit is not his" (as he pays for his benefit), this diminishes and deducts from his obligation as a borrower, and "he is not a borrower, but a paid guardian." That is, the payment of the fee detracts from the status of a borrower, which would require liability for unavoidable accidents, and leaves him only with the obligation for theft and loss (like a paid guardian).

Thus, the renter is a kind of "intermediate" between a paid guardian and a borrower—in the quality of his obligation and his essence, he is a borrower (and in the quantity of his obligation and his legal ruling, he is a paid guardian).

c. From this, the continuation of the aforementioned Gemara (section a) is well understood:
The intent of the question, "Who is the Tanna who holds there are four guardians?" is: From the fact that the Tanna specified the count ("There are four guardians")—for seemingly, why do I need the count?—Rav Nachman learned that their division into "four guardians" is not merely an external division, where in reality there are four modes of an object being in the possession of a guardian (in Rashi’s words, "this one is for free, this one for a wage, this one is a borrower, and this one is a renter"), but that they are also divided in their definitions and essences.

Therefore, Rav Nachman said, "(Who is the Tanna who holds there are four guardians...) It is Rabbi Meir"—because specifically according to his view that the renter pays like a paid guardian, it is possible to say that the four guardians have four distinct definitions.
According to the one who holds that the renter pays like an unpaid guardian, even though the renter (for a fee) is not similar to an unpaid guardian, as mentioned above, nevertheless, regarding the definition of the obligation of guarding imposed upon the guardian (there are four guardians), the renter is exactly like an unpaid guardian (because "he does not receive a wage for his guarding"), and consequently, there are only three (types of definitions of) guardians, not four.

Conversely, according to Rabbi Meir, that the renter pays like a paid guardian—this is not because the definition and cause of the renter’s obligation is the definition of the paid guardian’s obligation, but only that his legal ruling is like a paid guardian (meaning, he is similar to a paid guardian only in the quantity of his obligation, but in the quality of the obligation, he is like a borrower). Therefore, he is indeed a special type of guardian, and thus there are four guardians.

This is what Rav Nachman explained when Rava asked him, "Is there anyone who does not hold of four guardians?" (thinking that the matter of "four guardians" is due to their division in reality, and "necessarily, we find four types of guardians")—"This is what I meant to tell you" (that what I said previously, "Who is the Tanna who holds there are four guardians... It is Rabbi Meir," the intent is) that it is one and the same: The Tanna’s emphasis in our Mishnah, "There are four guardians," that there are four distinct definitions of guardians, is only well-founded according to Rabbi Meir, who holds that a renter is like a paid guardian, as mentioned above.

This is also the intent of the Gemara in its conclusion, "If so, there are four, but they are three... four guardians, but their legal rulings are three": According to the reasoning of the questioner, these are two contradictory things—if the renter and the paid guardian are divided (not only in reality, but) in their very definition and essence, how can it be that they are equal in their obligations? And since there is no practical difference between them in the quantity of their obligations, it must be that "they are three" also in their very definition. To this, the Gemara answers, "four guardians, but their legal rulings are three," meaning that while they are indeed divided into four guardians, this is not a contradiction to the fact that regarding legal rulings, they are divided into only three, as mentioned above.

d. The above explanation regarding "four guardians, but their legal rulings are three"—that the paid guardian and the renter are divided in the very essence of their obligation of guarding (which is why there are "four guardians"), yet regarding legal rulings, the renter pays like a paid guardian ("their legal rulings are three")—can be said to correspond to the explanation of the four guardians in spiritual *avodah*. As it is brought in the *Shelah*, "Just as the four guardians apply between man and his fellow, so too do they apply between man and the Place, blessed be He."

The explanation of the matter is:
It is known that the existence of the world depends on the fulfillment of Torah and *mitzvos* by *Bnei Yisrael*. Thus, *Bnei Yisrael* are in the category of "guardians," for the Holy One, blessed be He, entrusted His world to their domain so that they would "guard" it, similar to what is said, "to work it and to guard it," and the guarding of the world is through the fulfillment of Torah and *mitzvos*.

Now, according to the simple meaning, the four guardians are divided into three general types regarding their relationship to the guarded object and its owner: (a) An unpaid guardian, where all the benefit from this guarding is for the depositor (for the guardian earns nothing from guarding the deposit, as it is only for the benefit of the depositor); (b) A borrower—where all the benefit is his (for the lender receives no benefit from the object); (c) A paid guardian and a renter, where the common point between them is that in both, there is benefit both to the owner and to the guardian [in a paid guardian—the depositor benefits from his object being guarded (and with superior care), and the guardian receives a wage for his labor; and a renter has the benefit of using the lender’s object, and the lender receives the rental fee in return].

According to this, the content of the "guardians" in the matter of "between man and the Place, blessed be He" (as explained in the *Shelah*) is also understood:
"An unpaid guardian is the highest level, not for the sake of receiving a reward at all; he guards only for the sake of His Name, blessed be He," and in the language of the Rambam—"serving out of love... not for the sake of anything in the world... and not in order to inherit the good (not even to merit the World to Come), but he does the truth because it is truth... because He is the Master worthy to serve Him."

"A paid guardian—one who serves in order to receive a reward," and likewise the renter—"is similar to one who says, 'This coin is for charity, on the condition that my son shall live'" (as below in section vi).

"A borrower, where all the benefit is his... does not believe in reward and punishment," and as explained in the *Shelah*, these are the wicked who do not engage in the service of Hashem at all.

e. Perhaps one can explain his words—and also explain with subtlety the matter of the "borrower" in a way that this level can also exist among those who keep Torah and *mitzvos* (and the simplicity of the matter will be sweetened, that all four types are called guardians—guardians of the world (as mentioned above), which is through their being guardians of Torah and *mitzvos*)—with the following introduction:

Since the entire world was created for the sake of Israel (as the saying goes, "For the sake of Israel, who are called the beginning"), every single Jew can demand and claim—and justly so—all his physical needs, children, life, and ample sustenance, because he is a son of Avraham, Yitzchak, and Yaakov (without any action or *avodah* on his part). Similar to the opinion regarding one who sets sustenance for a worker, that "even if he makes them a feast like Solomon’s in his time," he has not fulfilled his obligation, because "they are the children of Avraham, Yitzchak, and Yaakov," and since he is a son of Avraham, Yitzchak, and Yaakov, he is worthy of a feast greater than "Solomon’s feast in his time."

[And the Tanna who disagrees and says "Everything is according to the custom of the province" does not hold this because he believes that every Jew is not worthy of this, Heaven forbid, but because he holds that the homeowner’s intention was not to provide him with sustenance except according to the custom of the province (including the worker in that province), and therefore it is impossible to obligate him more.]

This is the matter of the "borrower" (spiritually), where all the benefit is his—that he benefits from the world (by receiving all his needs, etc.) without "payment" (*avodah*) on his part.
However, just as a borrower (literally) purchases the object to the extent of being liable for unavoidable accidents because all the benefit is his—so it is spiritually: whoever wants to benefit from the world without any *avodah*, the very receipt of this benefit obligates him to "return" his share in the world to the Holy One, blessed be He, in its entirety—which is through the fulfillment of Torah and *mitzvos*, upon which the existence of the world depends, as mentioned above—and he cannot exempt himself with the claim of "unavoidable accident" (and not even a "major unavoidable accident").

Thus, the fulfillment of Torah and *mitzvos* by the "borrower" is not in the category of *avodah*, "service of Hashem," whose purpose is to obligate and subjugate oneself in *avodah* for the Master, Hashem, blessed be He, because for the borrower, this is an obligation resulting from his benefit (like the obligations of the borrower that stem from the fact that all the benefit is his, that it is as if he purchased the object and is therefore liable even for unavoidable accidents, and this is like an obligation toward himself).

f. Based on what was explained above regarding the view of Rabbi Meir on the difference between a renter and a paid guardian, one should seemingly add an explanation to the words of the *Shelah* regarding their meaning in spirituality—that a paid guardian is one who serves in order to receive a reward, and the renter "is similar to one who says, 'This coin is for charity, on the condition that my son shall live'"—that these are not just different modes in the relationship of receiving the reward (and benefit) of the *avodah*, but that they are different types.

The explanation: Just as a paid guardian and a renter literally are divided from one another in their very essence (as explained at length above), that the paid guardian’s essence is—a guardian, who has obligated and subjugated himself to guard the depositor’s object, and for this he receives his wage; whereas with the renter, it is the opposite, that his main goal is that the object should be with him for his benefit, and to obtain this, he pays a rental fee—
So too is it in spirituality: The beginning and essence of the "paid guardian" is—service of Hashem—meaning that he wants to fulfill His will, blessed be He, and guard the world for His sake, blessed be He, but he has not yet reached the level of an "unpaid guardian" (who does not serve for the sake of receiving a reward) in a way that matters of reward do not occupy a place for him, and therefore he expects to receive the reward for his *avodah*. But the essence of the "renter" is the opposite—his desire and wish is to benefit from the world (and receive all his needs, etc.), but he knows and feels that he must "pay" the Holy One, blessed be He (the Master of the world) for this benefit, and therefore he fulfills Torah and *mitzvos*, etc.
[And therefore the *Shelah* brings the example of "on the condition that my son shall live," in which it is emphasized that the main act of giving charity is caused by his need for Hashem’s salvation—that "my son shall live."]

Thus, the renter in his essence is neither like a borrower nor like a paid guardian:
He is not like a borrower, for since his fulfillment of Torah and *mitzvos* contains an element of "payment" to the Holy One, blessed be He, for his benefit from the world, it is therefore in the category of service of Hashem, for he does his *avodah* for His sake, blessed be He (unlike the borrower, who demands his needs for free, whose fulfillment of Torah and *mitzvos* is not in the category of "service of Hashem," as mentioned in section e).
But he is also not like a paid guardian, for with a paid guardian, the very *avodah* of fulfilling Torah and *mitzvos* is for His sake, blessed be He (because he does not benefit in his guarding from the deposit itself, and his wage is a secondary matter); whereas the renter, whose fulfillment of Torah and *mitzvos* is from the outset in the category of "payment" in exchange for his benefit from the world, is not for the purpose of "guarding."

g. Yet, despite this, the renter’s legal ruling is like that of a paid guardian, and consequently, "their legal rulings (of the four guardians) are three":
Ultimately, since even with the renter there are actions of "service of Hashem," he is therefore legally included in the category of "service of Hashem (at least) not for its own sake," like the paid guardian,
And we were commanded—"A person should always engage in Torah and *mitzvos* even if not for their own sake, for out of 'not for their own sake' comes 'for their own sake,'"
Meaning, that ultimately everyone will reach—even the borrower (the lowest level of "not for their own sake")—the *avodah* out of love, where he does not care about his own existence, until "his soul is bound in the love of Hashem, and he is found to be infatuated with it always... and he will abandon everything in the world except for it"—"and the end of the good is to come because of it."

## Sicha 2

### 119

**1.** The Rambam writes: "Just as a person is commanded regarding the rest of his animal on Shabbos, so too is he commanded regarding the rest of his manservant and maidservant... as it is stated: 'That your ox and your donkey may rest, and the son of your maidservant and the stranger may be refreshed.' The manservant and maidservant regarding whom we are commanded concerning their rest are those who have undergone circumcision and immersion for the sake of servitude and have accepted the mitzvos that servants are obligated in. However, servants who have not undergone circumcision and immersion, but have accepted upon themselves the seven mitzvos that the Bnei Noach were commanded, are considered a *ger toshav* and are permitted to perform work on Shabbos for themselves in public, like a Jew on a weekday."

This requires clarification: What is the reason for the Rambam’s addition to the law—that "servants who have not undergone circumcision and immersion... are permitted to perform work on Shabbos for themselves"—that the manner of work permitted to them is "in public, like a Jew on a weekday"? Even if he had written only "they are permitted to perform work on Shabbos for themselves" and nothing more, the implication would have been that there is no restriction on this permission.

Simply put, it appears that the source and reason for this addition is based on the language of the *braisa* (which is the source of this *halacha*): "A *ger toshav* is permitted to perform work on Shabbos for himself like a Jew on *Chol HaMoed* (regarding a *davar ha'aved*—a loss-incurring matter); Rabbi Akiva says: like a Jew on *Yom Tov* (for *ochel nefesh*—food preparation); Rabbi Yose says: a *ger toshav* performs work on Shabbos for himself like a Jew on a weekday; Rabbi Shimon says: both a *ger toshav* and a [non-Jew] manservant and maidservant who are *toshavim* perform work on Shabbos for themselves like a Jew on a weekday." Regarding this, Rav Ada bar Ahava said: "The *halacha* follows Rabbi Shimon." The Rambam adopted this very phrasing of Rabbi Shimon (whose opinion is the *halacha*): "like a Jew on a weekday," thereby excluding the other opinions on the matter.

However, on the face of it, this reason is insufficient to explain this addition in the Rambam. Since the Rambam did not cite the dissenting opinions, even if he had written concisely, "and they are permitted to perform work on Shabbos for themselves," it would be clear that he intended all types of work. There is no room to interpret this as meaning that it is permitted only for *ochel nefesh* (like *Yom Tov*) or for a *davar ha'aved* (like *Chol HaMoed*), and that other work is forbidden, because the plain meaning of the phrase "permitted to perform work on Shabbos for themselves" implies all work (at most, he could have written "to perform any work, etc.").

[Especially according to the Rambam’s own words in his introduction to *Sefer HaYad*, where he writes that he composed his book "in clear language... so that all the laws are revealed to the small and the great," and in such a way that "a person reads the Written Torah first, and then reads this and knows from it the entire Oral Torah, and does not need to read any other book between them"—from this it is understood that a person studying this *halacha* in the Rambam—whether he knows that there is a dispute in the Gemara or does not know of it—must interpret it according to its plain meaning, which is that "they are permitted to perform work on Shabbos for themselves" means all work.]

### 120

**2.** But if you examine it carefully, it is proven from the words of the Rambam himself that his intention in the words "like a Jew on a weekday" is not merely to quote Rabbi Shimon in the *braisa*—for he adds the word "in public," which is not in the words of Rabbi Shimon.

This addition is a change in substance. The entire content of Rabbi Shimon’s words in the *braisa* ("they perform... like a Jew on a weekday") is to establish the *type* of work permitted—that according to the *Tanna Kamma*, it is permitted for *davar ha'aved* (like a Jew on *Chol HaMoed*); according to Rabbi Akiva, it is permitted only for *ochel nefesh* (like a Jew on *Yom Tov*); and according to (Rabbi Yose and) Rabbi Shimon, it is permitted for all work, "like a Jew on a weekday." But in the Rambam, it is explicit that the words "like a Jew on a weekday" are the reason for permitting the *manner* of performing the work—even "in public."

[The Radbaz wrote that the Rambam added the word "in public" to "explain the *braisa*." Seemingly, his intent is that for the Rambam, the dispute among the *Tannaim* (whether a *ger toshav* performs work like a Jew on *Chol HaMoed*, like a Jew on *Yom Tov*, or like a Jew on a weekday) concerns whether it is forbidden to perform work in public and permitted only in private, like a Jew on *Chol HaMoed*, or whether he may perform it even in public, like a Jew on a weekday.

However, this interpretation is difficult, for seemingly the plain meaning of their words—"like a Jew on *Chol HaMoed*," "like a Jew on *Yom Tov*," "like a Jew on a weekday"—indicates (as Rashi explains) that their dispute is regarding the *type* of work permitted, not the *manner*. This is also explicit in the Yerushalmi: the one who says a *ger toshav* on Shabbos is like a Jew on *Yom Tov* means: "Just as a Jew on *Yom Tov* may bake and cook but is forbidden in all other work, so too a *ger toshav* on Shabbos"; the one who says a *ger toshav* on Shabbos is like a Jew on *Chol HaMoed* means: "Just as a Jew on *Chol HaMoed* may gather from the field but is forbidden in all field work, so too a *ger toshav* on Shabbos"; and the opinion of Rabbi Shimon, that a *ger toshav* on Shabbos is like a Jew on the rest of the days of the year, means: "Just as a Jew on the rest of the days of the year may plow, sow, and reap, so too a *ger toshav* on Shabbos."]

The question remains: Why did the Rambam need to add that the permission to perform work is also "in public" (and because of this, to add the reason and explanation "like a Jew on a weekday")?

**3.** Simply put, the reason the Rambam added "in public" is:

The state of "servants who have not undergone circumcision and immersion" is not the desired state for a servant. A servant who is in the possession of a Jew or taken from the non-Jews must enter into the "category of Jewish servants" through circumcision and immersion. Only when the servant does not desire this, one negotiates with him for twelve months until he undergoes circumcision and immersion, and it is forbidden to keep him longer than that while he is uncircumcised. Thus, this state of a servant (who has not undergone circumcision and immersion) is only a form of preparation so that he will come into the category of proper Jewish servants—a servant in his completeness.

Therefore, one might have thought that his performance of work, as long as he has not undergone circumcision and immersion, must be in a manner that does not contradict or disrupt the state of preparation for his ultimate goal—to enter into the category of proper Jewish servants. Consequently, it stands to reason that even in the work he performs for himself, he should do so specifically in private and not in public.

In slightly different terms: One might have thought to say that after he undergoes circumcision and immersion and becomes obligated in *mitzvos*, including the full observance of Shabbos, there is a concern that he might say, "Last year, even though I did not perform work for my master on Shabbos, I performed it for myself in public; now, too, I will do so, etc.," as we find several such rabbinic decrees. [And also regarding a *ger*—according to the opinion of Beis Hillel, a "ger who converted on Erev Pesach" does not perform his *Pesach* offering, as a "decree lest he become tamei the following year and say, 'Did I not immerse and eat last year? Now, too, I will immerse and eat,' not knowing that last year he was a non-Jew and did not receive *tumah*, whereas now he is a Jew and does receive *tumah*."]

For this reason, the Rambam adds that he is "permitted to perform work in public," and we do not fear the situation that will arise in the future when he undergoes circumcision and immersion.

### 121

**4.** But more deeply, it can be said that the words of the Rambam are in accordance with his position regarding the definition of the rest of "the son of your maidservant and the stranger."

All this can be understood by first explaining the continuation of the Rambam’s words in this *halacha* (in explaining the verse "that the son of your maidservant and the stranger may be refreshed"). He writes: "Since a *ger toshav* performs work for himself on Shabbos, and a *ger tzedek* is like a Jew in every respect, regarding whom is it said, 'that the son of your maidservant and the stranger may be refreshed'? This refers to a *ger toshav* who is a hired worker or laborer of a Jew, like the son of his maidservant, who may not perform work for his Jewish master on Shabbos, but for himself he may perform work, and even if this *ger* were his servant, he performs work for himself."

The Rashba challenged the Rambam, who wrote that this refers to a "hired worker or laborer of a Jew," and held that every *ger toshav* ("even one who is not a hired worker for a Jew") is forbidden from performing work for a Jew. Similarly, regarding an uncircumcised servant, "the verse forbade them not only from the work of their masters, but for all Jews," because "an uncircumcised servant is like a *ger toshav*; just as a *ger toshav* is forbidden from our work even though he is not conquered under our hand, so too when the Torah forbade an uncircumcised servant to all Jews, it forbade him."

The *Maggid Mishneh* explained that the Rambam also holds like the Rashba, that "even if he is not his hired worker, it is forbidden by Torah law to tell him to perform work for the sake of a Jew... and we find no permission except when he performs his own work... and our Master, who wrote 'hired worker and laborer,' spoke of what is common... a *toshav* servant and a *ger toshav* do not perform work by Torah law for any Jew, whether his master or not his master."

However, our Master the Alter Rebbe in his *Shulchan Aruch* proved at length that what the Rambam wrote—"that the son of your maidservant and the stranger may be refreshed—this refers to a *ger toshav* who is a hired worker or laborer of a Jew, like the son of his maidservant"—means "that he specifically specified a hired worker and laborer... the essence of the prohibition is because he is a hired worker." The reason that only a "*ger toshav* who is a hired worker or laborer of a Jew" is forbidden from performing work for a Jew—and not any non-Jew who is a hired worker or laborer—is (as he explains there at length) because he must be "a hired worker or laborer similar to the son of his maidservant." This is only a *ger toshav*, because "the verse speaks when the Jews are dwelling on their land... and at that time they do not permit non-Jews among them unless they accept the seven mitzvos, for which reason he is called a *ger toshav*, because it is permitted to settle him among us."
