A *ger toshav* "who has settled to dwell on the land of Israel to be a hired worker and laborer... since his intention is to settle down to work for a Jew forever, and he is conquered under his hand and compelled in his work, he is like a *eved olam* regarding Shabbos. And because [the Jew] rules over his body to enslave him just like the son of his maidservant, the Torah was particular about him... not to perform work for his master on Shabbos."

Thus, the Rambam and the Rashba disagree as to whether a *ger toshav* who is not a hired worker or laborer of a Jew is commanded in the rest of Shabbos.

On the other hand, we find a (seemingly) opposite distinction between the Rambam and the Rashba in this *halacha*:
The Rashba wrote "that if the servant or maidservant were performing the work of their master without our knowledge, and it is evident that they are not doing it for his knowledge, it is permitted, and one is not commanded to stop them."

But from the plain language of the Rambam, it is implied that he is forbidden from performing work for the sake of his Jewish master, whether with his knowledge or without his knowledge (as is explicit in *Kiryas Sefer* here), and only his own work is permitted.

### 122

**5.** It can be said that they disagree on the definition of the *mitzvah* of "that the son of your maidservant and the stranger may be refreshed," which can be interpreted in two ways:

1) It is a detail of the Jew’s rest, which does not belong to the son of the maidservant and the *ger* themselves. That is to say, the rest of his servant and *ger toshav* is included in the rest of their master; when they perform work for a Jew, there is a deficiency in the Jew’s rest.

2) The rest belongs (also) to the son of the maidservant and the *ger toshav* themselves. A Jew’s servant and the *ger toshav* who belongs to him have a requirement upon their bodies to be in a state of rest ("be refreshed") on the day of Shabbos. However, the warning regarding this is not upon the servant and the *ger toshav* themselves, but upon the Jew, who is warned that the son of his maidservant and the stranger shall not perform work for him. That is to say: as a master, the responsibility and obligation are placed upon him to ensure that his servant and the *ger toshav* who belong to him rest.

[And even though they are forbidden only from the work of their masters but are permitted to perform work for themselves, this is because the obligation of "being refreshed" (and the prohibition of work) is not due to themselves [since they are not of the Jews upon whom the obligation of rest applies] but only because of their connection to the Jew, by virtue of being a Jew’s servant and a *ger toshav* belonging to a Jew. Therefore, they are forbidden only from work for their master, i.e., in the portion belonging to the Jew; whereas when they perform work for themselves—in which there is no connection to the Jew and it does not enter into the category of their servitude—the obligation of rest does not apply.]

This is the root of the difference between the opinion of the Rashba and the Rambam:
According to the Rashba, the Torah warned (in his words) "not to perform our work through our servants or through a *ger toshav*." That is, the rest of a servant and *ger toshav* is a detail of "our work," of the rest of a Jew (and "similar to an ox and a donkey, which are derived from one verse, as it is written: 'That your ox and your donkey may rest, and the son of your maidservant and the stranger may be refreshed.' Just as we are commanded regarding an ox and a donkey, so too we are commanded regarding our servant"). Therefore, regarding a servant who is conquered under our hand, by performing work for his master, he nullifies his master’s rest on Shabbos. For this reason, it is forbidden for an uncircumcised servant (and likewise a *ger toshav* who is not conquered under our hand) to perform work even for another Jew who is not his master when the Jew commands him to perform work for him, as this is as if the Jew is performing work through the servant and the *ger toshav*. Only when a Jew does not command him to perform the work is he permitted (by Torah law) to perform work even for a Jew.

Unlike the Rambam, for whom the rest of the servant and *ger toshav* belongs to the servant and *ger* themselves. Therefore, the prohibition is only for a *ger toshav* who is a hired worker or laborer of a Jew, who is conquered under our hand and is "like a *eved olam*" (as mentioned above from the Alter Rebbe), because since the Jew "rules over his body to enslave him just like the son of his maidservant," the law of Shabbos rest is extended to him regarding the performance of work for his master.

Therefore, the Rambam holds that even if the *ger toshav* performs work for his Jewish master without his master’s knowledge, it is forbidden—because the prohibition is not due to the Jew’s rest, but due to the rest of the *ger toshav* himself.

On the other hand, an ordinary *ger toshav* (who is not a hired worker or laborer of a Jew) who performs work for a Jew, even at the command of the Jew, does not violate a Torah prohibition, because such a *ger toshav* cannot have the law of Shabbos extended to him (since he is not the Jew’s "*eved olam*").

### 124

**6.** In this, we can add an explanation regarding another change between the language of the Rambam and the language of the Gemara.

In the *braisa* there, in the words of the *Tanna Kamma*, the language is: "A *ger toshav* is permitted to perform work on Shabbos for himself like a Jew on *Chol HaMoed*; Rabbi Akiva says: like a Jew on *Yom Tov*." This means that the "permitted" in the words of the *Tanna Kamma* also applies to the words of Rabbi Akiva. However, Rabbi Yose does not say concisely, like the language of Rabbi Akiva, "like a Jew on a weekday," but elaborates: "A *ger toshav* performs work on Shabbos for himself like a Jew on a weekday." Similarly, in the words of Rabbi Shimon: "Both a *ger toshav* and a [non-Jew] manservant and maidservant who are *toshavim* perform work on Shabbos for themselves like a Jew on a weekday."

The question is: Why did the Rambam change from the language of Rabbi Yose and Rabbi Shimon (whose opinion he rules as the *halacha*)—"they perform work on Shabbos for themselves"—and write "and they are permitted to perform work on Shabbos for themselves" (like the language of the *braisa* in the opinion of the *Tanna Kamma* and Rabbi Akiva)?

Based on the above, it can be said: The reason Rabbi Yose and Rabbi Shimon changed from the language of the *braisa* before them, it seems to me, is because the difference between their opinion and the opinion of the *Tanna Kamma* and Rabbi Akiva is not only a difference in quantity—how many works are permitted to them (for according to the *Tanna Kamma* and Rabbi Akiva, many works are forbidden and only these works (for the *Tanna Kamma*, a *davar ha'aved*; for Rabbi Akiva, *ochel nefesh*) are permitted to them; unlike Rabbi Yose and Rabbi Shimon, for whom all works are permitted)—but rather this is a different manner of permission, which depends on the person (as the plainness of their language implies). For the *Tanna Kamma* and Rabbi Akiva, the *ger toshav* and the servant themselves are forbidden in work, even in their own work, but they were permitted to perform certain works for themselves. Unlike Rabbi Yose and Rabbi Shimon, for whom from the outset there is no prohibition of work upon them, and the prohibition is only not to perform work for his master. Thus, the fact that "they perform work... for themselves" is not merely a permission, that they are permitted to perform work for themselves, but that "they perform work" for themselves, because from the outset they are not in the category of Shabbos rest.

With this, the reason for the Rambam is resolved: he was precise in his language and wrote "and they are permitted to perform work on Shabbos for themselves"—because according to the Rambam, the "refreshing of the son of your maidservant and the stranger" belongs to the son of the maidservant and the *ger* themselves (as mentioned above, and not (only) due to the nullification of the rest of the Jew, the master), and the performance of work for themselves is in the category of a permission—"they are permitted to perform work on Shabbos for themselves."

### 125

**7.** Based on all the above, we can explain the precision of the Rambam’s language, which necessitated adding (to the language of the *Shas*) "and they are permitted to perform work on Shabbos for themselves in public"—because according to what we have explained, according to the Rambam, this servant (whose law is that of a *ger toshav*) is not removed from the essence of the obligation of rest. On the contrary, the fact that he performs work on Shabbos is a form of "permission"—"and they are permitted to perform work, etc."—and therefore one might have thought that even when he is permitted to perform work, it is only in private but not in public, so that it does not appear that he has no concept of Shabbos at all. Therefore, the Rambam needed to add that this is not so, but that it is permitted to perform work even in public.

And to explain the reason that they are truly not forbidden from performing work in public, the Rambam wrote "like a Jew on a weekday." It can be said that his intention is according to the plain meaning of his words: he did not come to exclude (as in the *Shas*) "like a Jew on *Chol HaMoed*... on *Yom Tov*" (which is not mentioned in the Rambam here at all), but rather—"like a Jew on a weekday" and not "like a non-Jew." That is, do not say that the fact that he is permitted to perform work for himself is because he is a non-Jew who is not in the law of Shabbos at all (and only that he is forbidden from performing work for his Jewish master—due to his master’s rest)—but rather, the reason for the permission for his own work is similar to a "Jew on a weekday." Just as the performance of work by a Jew on a weekday is not because the person is not in the law of Shabbos, but because the time is "weekday" [for even then there is a prohibition of work on Shabbos, but now it is a "weekday" time], so too is it with this servant whose law is that of a *ger toshav*: the person is in the law of Shabbos, but the concept of Shabbos for him is only in connection to his master’s work, whereas regarding work for himself, he is "like a Jew on a weekday."

And since regarding work for himself he is "like a Jew on a weekday," it is understood that there is no room to forbid him from performing work in public [and on the other hand, this does not detract at all from the fact that for him, too, there is the law of Shabbos (regarding his master’s work)].

### 126

**8.** It was brought above (Section 4) that according to the *Maggid Mishneh*, the Rambam also holds like the Rashba, that even if he is not a hired worker, it is forbidden by Torah law to perform work for the sake of a Jew, and we find no permission except when he performs his own work.

Furthermore, the *Maggid Mishneh*’s position differs from the Alter Rebbe’s—for according to his position, the Rambam holds that a non-Jew who has not accepted upon himself the seven mitzvos of the Bnei Noach is not included in the prohibition, as only one who has accepted the seven mitzvos of the Bnei Noach did the Torah warn regarding his rest on Shabbos for the sake of a Jew—unlike the Rashba’s position, who holds that even a servant who has not accepted the seven mitzvos of the Bnei Noach (and even an idolater) we are commanded regarding him [unlike the Alter Rebbe, who wrote that the Rambam holds "that the fulfillment of the seven mitzvos is not a matter regarding the prohibition of work on Shabbos," and he holds that even for the Rambam, the fact that a hired worker or laborer is forbidden from his master’s work applies even when he has not accepted the seven mitzvos].

The reasoning for the dispute between the Alter Rebbe and the *Maggid Mishneh* can be explained as depending on the cause and reason for the application of the law of Shabbos rest upon the servant and *ger toshav* themselves (as explained at length above, that this is the Rambam’s position), which can be explained in two ways:

1) Because of their connection to the Jew. Since the servant is acquired by his master and the *ger toshav* is conquered under our hand (as mentioned by the Alter Rebbe), the law of Shabbos rest is extended to him (regarding his master’s work).

2) Because of their connection to the laws of Israel and the Torah of Israel. For even the acceptance of the seven mitzvos of the Bnei Noach by a *ger toshav* is connected to the Torah of Israel, as the Rambam writes: "Moshe our teacher commanded from the mouth of the Almighty to compel all who come into the world to accept the mitzvos that the Bnei Noach were commanded... and whoever accepts them is called a *ger toshav* everywhere, and he must accept them before three sages... and this is that he accepts them and performs them because the Holy One, Blessed be He, commanded them in the Torah and informed us through Moshe our teacher... but if he performed them because of his own reasoning, this is not a *ger toshav*." That is, he must accept upon himself the seven mitzvos (a) before three sages (from the Jews), and (b) "because the Holy One, Blessed be He, commanded them in the Torah and informed us through Moshe our teacher." Since he has a relationship and connection to the Torah of Israel (for he "accepted upon himself" the instructions of the Torah of Israel that apply to him), there is room to say that the day of Shabbos for him is not just any day that has a command, but it has the application and name of the "Shabbos" of Israel.

This is what the *Maggid Mishneh* holds: that according to the Rambam, the rest of the servant and *ger toshav* is not (only) because of their connection to property, the wealth of a Jew, but also because of their connection to the laws and Torah of Israel (as the Rambam himself writes regarding the definition of the acceptance of the seven mitzvos of a *ger toshav*). Therefore, in matters connected to Israel (i.e., when he performs work for a Jew), the concept of Shabbos rest is extended to him as well.

Therefore, the *Maggid Mishneh* interprets that according to the Rambam, "hired worker and laborer" is not specific, but rather anyone who performs work for a Jew is forbidden—because the reason for the prohibition is because of the very name "*ger toshav*" (or "servants... (who) accepted upon themselves the seven mitzvos that the Bnei Noach were commanded... (who) are like a *ger toshav*"). On the other hand, a non-Jew who has not accepted the seven mitzvos of the Bnei Noach is not forbidden in his work for a Jew, because it is impossible for the law of rest to apply to him (unlike the Rashba, for whom there is no need for this law to apply to him, because according to his position, the entire prohibition is because of the Jew’s rest, as explained at length above).

(From the *Sichos* of *Shabbos Parshas Metzora* and *Shabbos Parshas Acharei*, 5746)

## Sicha 3

## 128

A. "There shall be no one miscarrying or barren in your land...". Rashi copies the words "There shall be no one miscarrying" and explains: "If you do My will."

Commentators have noted that Rashi’s innovation here is that "even though the condition is not explicitly written here, it must be that it is stated conditionally; for if not, then it would be possible for there to be a miscarriage." In other words, although the preceding verses explicitly state the condition: "If you listen to His voice and do all that I speak... and you shall serve Hashem your G-d," nevertheless, since the verse here begins "There shall be no one miscarrying" (without the conjunctive *vav*), it might appear that "this is a separate passage and is an unconditional promise." This is what Rashi refutes in his commentary, "If you do My will," indicating that this promise, too, is conditional upon Bnei Yisrael doing the will of Hashem. Rashi’s necessity for asserting that this promise is also conditional is that "otherwise, there could be a miscarriage."

On the face of it, this interpretation is forced. Even if it is stated "There shall be no one..." without the conjunctive *vav*, how could it enter one's mind that these blessings of "There shall be no one miscarrying..." are different from all other blessings in the Torah, and were stated without conditions, and are not dependent on the state of the person (to the extent that Rashi felt compelled to refute this initial assumption)?

Furthermore: following "There shall be no one miscarrying... I will fulfill the number of your days," it is said: "I will send My terror before you, and I will throw into confusion all the people... and I will send the hornet before you... and it shall drive out the Hivite..." (and several other details regarding the driving out of the nations from the land and the inheritance of the land). It is certainly impossible to say that all these promises are "unconditional," for they are the very same promises stated earlier, conditional upon: "If you listen to His voice... I will be an enemy to your enemies... My angel will go before you and bring you to the Emorite... and I will destroy him." This passage ("There shall be no one miscarrying...") is a continuation of the preceding passage.

Some commentators suggest that the initial assumption applies only to this specific blessing of "There shall be no one miscarrying... in your land." Because the verse specifies "in your land," there is an implication that this blessing is not dependent on the fulfillment of Torah and mitzvos (for if it were, why would it be only "in your land" and not wherever one fulfills His will?), but rather it is a quality of the land itself. Rashi comes to teach us that even this blessing ("in your land") is conditional upon "If you do My will."

But this interpretation also seems forced, for if so, Rashi should have also copied the word "in your land," since it is the foundation of his explanation, "If you do My will."

## 129

Furthermore: according to this, it is difficult to understand Rashi’s conclusion that even this blessing is dependent on doing the will of the Ribono shel Olam—why was this blessing given only "in your land" and not wherever a Yid who does His will might be (like the other blessings) ?

B. The Re’em writes that Rashi’s intent in saying "If you do My will" is: "As if it said: 'Serve Hashem your G-d, and He will bless your bread and your water, and there shall be no one miscarrying or barren, etc.'—meaning, serve Hashem, and through this your bread and water will be blessed, and there shall be no one miscarrying, etc." This means that "There shall be no one miscarrying..." is a continuation of the end of the previous verse: "(And you shall serve Hashem your G-d) and He will bless your bread and your water, and I will remove sickness from your midst" (and several literalists, such as the Ibn Ezra, Ramban, and others, explained it this way).

It appears from his words that Rashi’s intent here (not to refute the initial assumption that this blessing was given without the condition of listening to the voice of Hashem, but rather) is to resolve why it says "There shall be no one miscarrying" without the conjunctive *vav*. Rashi comes to teach us that this is the way of the verses, as if it were written "And there shall be no one miscarrying," with the addition of a *vav*.

However, it is difficult to interpret Rashi’s words "If you do My will" in this way, for according to this, the main point is missing from the text. Rashi should have clearly written "The verse is elliptical," or "It is connected to the preceding verse" (or similar), or "If you serve Me," as the language of the verse here is "And you shall serve."

Therefore, it seems to me the opposite: that by precisely using the language "If you do My will," Rashi’s intent is to refute this interpretation (that "There shall be no one miscarrying..." is a continuation of "And you shall serve..."):

From the plain meaning of the verse, it appears that the intent of "And you shall serve Hashem your G-d" is not the general fulfillment of the will of the Ribono shel Olam (by keeping Torah and mitzvos), but rather the rejection of idol worship. In the preceding verse, it says: "You shall not bow down to their gods, nor serve them... for you shall surely destroy them... (and following this it says) And you shall serve Hashem your G-d" (the rejection of idol worship).

Rashi is precise in his commentary on "There shall be no one miscarrying," and he changes the language from the verse's "And you shall serve" and writes "If you do My will." This means that the condition for this blessing is not the "And you shall serve" of the previous verse, but rather doing His will (i.e., performing the mitzvos). This verse refers back to the very beginning of the topic, where it is stated : "Be careful before Him and listen to His voice... If you listen to His voice and do all that I speak" —that is, the performance of all the mitzvos. When Yisrael does His will, then, in addition to all the promises written above ("I will be an enemy... and I will bring you..."), there is also this: "There shall be no one miscarrying..."

[However, the text interrupts between them (as a "parenthetical remark") with the warning against idol worship. Following what was mentioned in the verse regarding "the Emorite and the Hittite, etc.", it warns not to stumble into the idol worship of the inhabitants of the land: "You shall not bow down... nor serve them... (but rather) you shall serve Hashem your G-d" (and likewise

## 130

the reward that comes for this—"And He will bless your bread... and I will remove... from your midst").]

Based on this, the language of the verse "There shall be no one miscarrying..." (without the conjunctive *vav*) is resolved: it is to separate it from the preceding verse and to emphasize that it is not a continuation of "And you shall serve Hashem your G-d," but rather refers back to the very beginning.

C. The content of the matter requires explanation: What is different about this blessing of "There shall be no one miscarrying," such that it comes specifically because of doing His will ("If you do My will"), and not (only) because of "And you shall serve Hashem your G-d" (like the blessings of "He will bless your bread... your water, and I will remove...")?

One can say regarding this, by first noting the reason the verse is precise in saying "There shall be no one miscarrying... in your land." For on the face of it, it is difficult (as noted in section A): since this blessing is also conditional upon "If you do My will," why was the blessing given only "in your land," and not wherever Yisrael does the will of the Ribono shel Olam?

The explanation—simply (to the point where Rashi does not need to explain it)—is that the meaning of "There shall be no one miscarrying... in your land" is exactly as it sounds: this blessing is not (only) for a private individual, for one who does the will of the Ribono shel Olam, but a blessing for the land. "There shall be no one miscarrying... in your land" means that the blessing is for the land itself, that "if you do My will," its nature changes to become a land in which there is no "miscarrying or barrenness."

Thus, the addition of the word "in your land" does not come to limit this blessing (that it is only in Eretz Yisrael), but on the contrary, to magnify the blessing that comes through doing the will of the Ribono shel Olam: that the blessing is drawn down not only to the person who does His will, but brings a blessing to all of Eretz Yisrael as a whole.

According to this, it is simply understood why this blessing is (a) a reward for "doing My will" (fulfilling "all that I speak"), and not only for "serving Hashem your G-d" (which is only the rejection of idol worship, as mentioned)—because such a great blessing requires the fulfillment of all the mitzvos; (b) only "in your land" and not in the diaspora—because this blessing is connected with doing His will in Eretz Yisrael. Since this is the special and exalted place that the Ribono shel Olam prepared for Bnei Yisrael (as is explicit in the preceding verses: "to bring you to the place which I have prepared"), therefore, doing His will in this land effects a special blessing for the entire land (unlike in the diaspora, where the blessing ("there shall be no one miscarrying or barren") is drawn down only to that specific person who does the will of Hashem).

[And with this, the distinction between the two parts of the verse is also well-explained—(a) "There shall be no one miscarrying... in your land," (b) "I will fulfill the number of your days" (without emphasizing that this is only in your land) —because the first part speaks of a blessing effected in the entire land, and in the second part, the verse specifies a special blessing for the person who does His will: "I will fulfill the number of your days," meaning in every place of residence of each and every Yid, even in the diaspora.]

D. And one should add further (according to the inner dimensions of these matters):

## 131

As a reward for "And you shall serve..." it is said "He will bless your bread..."—the language of a blessing. Whereas here, the verse says "There shall be no one miscarrying...", which implies a promise.

The difference between them: a blessing is merely the granting of power from above for this abundance, but it is possible that there may be obstacles and delays, etc., in the drawing down of the abundance below, such that the blessing is not realized in actual fact, in simple physical terms. A promise of the Ribono shel Olam for good, however, cannot fail to be fulfilled (as explained in the Rambam).

This is the reason for the distinction: for "And you shall serve Hashem your G-d," whose content is only the rejection of idol worship (as mentioned), the reward comes only by way of a "blessing" ("He will bless..."), whereas when "you do My will"—then the reward comes as a promise, "There shall be no one miscarrying... in your land, I will fulfill the number of your days," with long life and good years in the simple sense.

And they merit the inheritance of the land, up to "I will set your border from the Red Sea to the Sea of the Philistines, and from the desert to the River," for this verse speaks of the time to come, in the fulfillment of the prophecy "When Hashem your G-d expands your border," and it is said "He shall have dominion also from sea to sea, and from the river unto the ends of the earth."


## פ' שקלים

## 132

### Parshas Shekalim

1. In the mitzvah of giving the *machatzis hashekel* (half-shekel), we find two unique details:

a) The *cheftza* (object) with which the mitzvah is performed must be a "half-shekel," not a "full shekel." The verse emphasizes this detail to such an extent that it does not say "ten gerah" (even though the verse continues, "the shekel is twenty gerah"), but rather "half-shekel." This is also the obligation for all generations, as ruled by the Rambam: "The mitzvah of the half-shekel is to give half of the currency of that time, even if that currency is larger than the *shekel hakodesh*."

This emphasis on "half" specifically (rather than "ten gerah," which is the full number) contains a novelty regarding all matters of the Beis Hamikdash (and holiness in general), which are required to be "complete" and "unblemished." For example: the Temple vessels only sanctify when they are complete and full; *korbanos* must be unblemished (and not have a blemish); the Kohen who performs the service must be unblemished, and so on. Yet here, the precision is that it must be a "half" specifically, and not a complete object.

b) Regarding the act of giving, the Rambam rules that one does not give the half-shekel "in many installments, a little today and a little tomorrow, but rather gives it all at once, in a single act." This is also a novelty regarding other monetary obligations, where we do not find a requirement to give the entire sum one is obligated to pay "all at once, in a single act." Here, however, one is obligated to give "all of it at once, in a single act."

Seemingly, these two details are contradictory: on the one hand, this mitzvah emphasizes a lack of completeness—specifically a "half-shekel"—while on the other hand, there is an emphasis that the act of giving must be a "complete" act, "all of it at once, in a single act," rather than giving in halves.

2. This can be explained according to *mussar* and Chassidus:

It is a well-known allusion regarding the *machatzis hashekel* that the existence of the giver is only a "half," and in order to be a complete entity ("*shekel hakodesh*"), he requires a second "half." One of the explanations for this is that the two "halves" (of the *shekel hakodesh*) allude to Hashem and *Bnei Yisrael*, for Hashem and *Knesses Yisrael* are, so to speak, compared to one another, where each is a "half-body" (*palag gufa*). As the Maggid taught regarding the verse, "Make for yourself two trumpets," that "trumpets" (*chatzotzros*) means "half-forms" (*chatzi tzuros*), and the "two trumpets" allude to Hashem and *Knesses Yisrael*, for "together they are a complete form."

The meaning of this is understood based on what is explained

## 133

in the Tanya, that the soul of every Jewish person is "a part of Elokus from above, literally." This means that the soul is not, in and of itself, a separate entity from Him, may He be blessed, that is added to His essence; rather, from the outset, it is "entirely one" with Hashem, "a part of Elokus from above, literally."

This is the concept of the "half-shekel"—the revelation of the *nefesh elokis*, which awakens in a person the feeling that he is only a "half," and he yearns and desires to unite with the second "half-form," with Hashem.

3. This recognition and feeling—that a person has no completeness in himself because his entire existence is only a "half" and a "half-form"—finds expression (primarily) in the matter of *mesiras nefesh*.

It is explained in the Tanya that the matter of *mesiras nefesh* to Hashem does not come from knowledge and contemplation of Hashem at all, but rather without any contemplation, as if it were impossible to deny the One Hashem, without any reason or argument whatsoever. This is because "Hashem is One" illuminates and enlivens the entire soul, etc.

In other words: the fact that all of Israel are prepared to surrender their souls to Hashem is in a manner of "it is impossible to deny the One Hashem"—meaning, they feel they cannot be separated from Him, may He be blessed. This feeling comes about by awakening within themselves their connection to Hashem, that Israel and Hashem are like two half-forms. When one feels that "Hashem is One"—that He is his vitality and the truth of his existence ("because the One Hashem illuminates and enlivens the entire soul")—then it is impossible for there to be a separation between the soul and Hashem, for they are nothing but "two half-forms."

This is the allusion in the two opposites mentioned above regarding the mitzvah of *machatzis hashekel*—(a) specifically a half, (b) "all of it at once, in a single act"—and on the contrary, the two are dependent upon each other:

When a person feels himself to be a "half"—which is from the perspective of the revelation of the connection to Hashem, that he is not a separate entity from Him, may He be blessed, but rather like a "half-form"—then his surrender and giving to Hashem is in a manner of "all of it at once," without division.

This is like the matter of *mesiras nefesh* in its simple sense, which is a single point that is above division. It is not applicable to say that a person has "a little" *mesiras nefesh* or "a lot" of *mesiras nefesh*, because the definition of *mesiras nefesh* is that he surrenders his soul—the point of his vitality—to Hashem, and division is not possible in this. By way of this, he surrenders everything he has to Hashem.

It can be said that this is also the allusion in the statement of our Sages that Hashem showed Moshe a "coin of fire" and said to him, "Give like this." The content of this mitzvah is not merely the giving of money, but the giving of a "coin of fire"—a giving permeated with the fire of the soul, until it is entirely fire. This is the concept of surrendering his soul to Hashem, the fire of *mesiras nefesh*.

## 134

4. The *terumas machatzis hashekel* is (primarily) for the communal *korbanos* that were offered throughout the year. "Korbanos" are called "avodah" (service) simply, because the service of the *korbanos* alludes to the general concept of "drawing near" to Hashem (*korban* comes from the word *kiruv*—drawing near). This is the content of all matters of *avodas Hashem*, through which a person draws near to his Creator. From this, it is understood that the mitzvah of *machatzis hashekel* is the foundation of the entire concept of *avodas Hashem*.

The explanation for this is sweetened by the fact that *avodas Hashem* is complete only when a person feels in all his affairs that he is nothing but a "half." For in everything he does to serve Hashem with all his powers, it is only a "half," because were it not for Hashem helping him, he would not be able to do it. Hashem gives the person the powers necessary to be able to serve Hashem, and even more so according to our Sages regarding the verse, "Who has preceded Me, that I should pay him?"

This feeling comes about through the "half-shekel," whose essence (spiritually) is the matter of *mesiras nefesh* (as mentioned above). When the foundation of his service is the awakening of "surrendering his soul to Hashem for His Unity, that it should be fixed in his heart constantly, day and night, and never depart from his memory," then he feels in all his affairs that he is nothing but a "half."

Through engaging in the Torah of the *machatzis hashekel*—which was given specifically "all of it at once, in a single act," above division—we will merit soon the true and complete Redemption through Mashiach Tzidkeinu. This Redemption will also not come in a sequence of stages, but (as the clear ruling of the Rambam states), "The Torah has already promised that in the end, Israel will repent... and immediately they will be redeemed," immediately and truly, in our days.


## תרומה

## Sicha 1

### 135

A. One of the items that the Jewish people were commanded to bring for the contribution to the Mishkan ("And this is the contribution that you shall take from them") was "tachash skins". Rashi explains that *tachash* is "a type of wild animal."

We find a dispute among the Sages regarding this. In the Bavli, the words of Reish Lakish in the name of Rabbi Meir are cited: "The *tachash* that existed in the days of Moshe was a unique creature, and the Sages could not determine whether it was a type of wild animal or a type of domesticated animal, etc." However, in the Yerushalmi, it states: "The Rabbis say it is a type of kosher wild animal" (and this is also found in the Tanchuma: "Rabbi Yehuda says it was a large kosher wild animal that existed in the desert, etc., and its skin had six colors, and they took it and made curtains from it").

Based on this (as is also explained by the commentators), Rashi rules according to the opinion of the Yerushalmi (and the Tanchuma) that it is "a type of wild animal."

One must ask: What is the source for Rashi, in the simple meaning of the verse, to decide between these opinions and conclude that it is "a type of wild animal"?

B. Rashi subsequently continues, "It existed only for a time, and it had many colors; therefore, the Targum translates it as *sasgona*, because it rejoiced and took pride in its colors."

The reason Rashi needed to state that "it existed only for a time" can be explained simply: Since a student of Scripture (a five-year-old child) does not know of any wild animal named *tachash*, Rashi explains that it is indeed not a known animal because "it existed only for a time".

## 136

[Based on this, it is also understood why Rashi chose the phrasing "it existed only for a time," which follows the language of the Bavli: "It was provided to Moshe for a time, and he made the Mishkan from it, and it was then hidden away"—and not like the Yerushalmi: "The Holy One, Blessed be He, created a type of kosher wild animal for Moshe in the desert; once he performed the work of the Mishkan, it was hidden away," or the language of Rabbi Nechemiah in the Tanchuma: "It was a miraculous creation, and at the time it was created, it was hidden away"—because in the simple meaning of the verse, there is no necessity to assume a special creation or a miracle (or a special act of hiding the *tachash*), only that "it existed only for a time," and therefore its memory was forgotten in Tanach].

Similarly, it is understood why Rashi continues, "and it had many colors." Since it is not a known animal (because "it existed only for a time"), Rashi needs to explain what was special about the *tachash* skins that caused Hashem to command that they be brought for the contribution to the Mishkan (especially since the verse does not state that the skins needed to be dyed, as he explains regarding "ram skins—dyed red"). Therefore, Rashi explains that "it had many colors" (and there was no need for dyeing).

However, one must ask: Why does Rashi use the lengthy phrasing, "therefore the Targum translates it as *sasgona*, because it rejoiced and took pride in its colors"? Seemingly, it would have sufficed to write briefly, "as it is translated (*sasgona*)," which proves that the *tachash* had many colors. What is the emphasis in Rashi's (a) "therefore the Targum translates, etc.," and (b) his concluding with the explanation of the Targum's words, "because it rejoiced and took pride in its colors"?

Seemingly, Rashi's words (in explaining the Targum) originate in the Gemara, where the Gemara explains the words of Rabbi Nechemiah that "there was one covering [of ram skins dyed red and *tachash* skins] and it resembled a *tala ilan*," meaning: "It resembles a *tala ilan* in that it has many colors, but it is not a *tala ilan*, for that is impure and this is pure. Rabbi Yosef said: If so, this is why we translate it as *sasgona*, because it rejoices in many colors"—and this is the same as Rashi's language, "therefore the Targum translates it as *sasgona*, because it rejoiced and took pride in its colors."

But it has already been explained many times that it is not Rashi's way in his commentary on the Torah to copy the language of Chazal unless it pertains to the simple meaning of the verse. How does the lengthiness of the language (of the Gemara and) of Rashi pertain to the meaning of the word "tachash"?

[Especially in this case, where Rabbi Yosef's intent in saying "If so" (it is a support, Rashi) is not only to prove that the *tachash* has many colors, but primarily to explain that it is pure (unlike the "tala ilan" which is impure)—for since "it is pure, it makes sense that we translate it as *sasgona*, because it does not resemble the *tala ilan* except in that it has many colors"].

Some commentators suggest that Rashi comes to resolve the words of the Targum: Since Rashi wrote "it existed only for a time," how can the Targum (in Bavel) name this animal, which was not known either before or after the time of the Mishkan? Therefore, Rashi wrote "therefore the Targum translates it as *sasgona*, because it rejoiced and took pride in its colors," meaning that the Targum does not intend this to be

## 137

the name "*tachash*" in Aramaic, but rather a description of the appearance of the *tachash*, "because it rejoiced... in its colors."

But this explanation is also forced, for (as mentioned many times) it is not the purpose of Rashi's Torah commentary to copy and explain the words of the Targum.

C. The explanation for this can be stated simply:

In his commentary "a type of wild animal," Rashi does not come to negate the interpretation that the *tachash* is "a type of domesticated animal", but rather to negate the assumption that "*tachash*" is the name of a color, and that the phrase "tachash skins" is like "ram skins dyed red"—meaning that just as "ram skins dyed red" means ram skins that "were dyed red after they were processed", there is room to interpret "tachash skins" as skins that were dyed with a color called "*tachash*" [similar to the opinions found in the Yerushalmi: "Rabbi Yehuda says it is named for the one who dyes it" (ram skins were dyed, and they are called *tachash* skins after the color that resembles the *tachash*—Korban HaEdah), and Rabbi Nechemiah says *galaktinin* (a name of a color—Korban HaEdah)].

Therefore, Rashi wrote that "*tachash*—a type of wild animal," and his intention is simply that they took skins of a type of wild animal named *tachash*, and that they did not take (ram) skins and dye them with the color *tachash*.

[Rashi's necessity for this simple meaning is that if the intent were that they dyed the (ram) skins with the (color of) *tachash*, the verse should have said "ram skins dyed red and *tachash*" (or similar), and not "and *tachash* skins." From the fact that the verse adds "and *tachash* skins," it is understood that these are not the skins mentioned previously ("ram skins"), but rather that they are skins of a *tachash* (and *tachash* is "a type of wild animal")].

This is also the continuation of Rashi's words, "therefore the Targum translates it as *sasgona*, because it rejoiced and took pride in its colors":

With this, Rashi comes to bring proof from the Targum that it is not merely the name of a color with which the skins were dyed, but that it is a type of wild animal, for *tachash* is translated as *sasgona*, because the animal "rejoiced and took pride in its colors".

Therefore, Rashi did not write briefly "as it is translated (*sasgona*)," because then there would be room to interpret that the Targum's intent is that "*tachash*" is a color composed of a combination of many colors, or that "*sasgona*" means six colors (similar to the opinion in the Tanchuma that the *tachash* skin had six

## 138

colors)—rather, he emphasizes "rejoiced and took pride in its colors," through which it is understood that the Targum's intent is "a type of wild animal."

D. However, one must still ask about the precision of Rashi's language, "rejoiced and took pride in its colors," for seemingly "rejoiced" (as in the Gemara) would have sufficed. What is the addition of (not only "rejoiced" but also) "took pride in its colors"?

It can be said that by this, Rashi also hints at the special quality of the "tachash skins," which is not only due to the beauty of the colors in these skins, but because it emphasizes the nature of the *tachash*: that these multicolored skins cause the *tachash* to take pride in its colors (and this is a primary aspect of it, to the point that it is part of its name in the language of the Targum).

This is also the quality and importance of this contribution of "tachash skins": It is understood and simple that a contribution from the animal kingdom is superior in importance to a contribution from the plant kingdom (similar to what we find regarding sacrifices on the Altar). In this lies the special importance of "tachash skins" even relative to "ram skins dyed red"—not only because the *tachash* skins had "many colors" (unlike the other colors, where each was brought separately: *techeiles*, *argaman*, and *tola'as shani*, etc.), but primarily because the colors are part of the *tachash* skin itself. In the donation of "ram skins dyed red" (which were dyed after they already existed), the donation from the animal kingdom is only regarding the body of the skin itself, but not regarding the color of the skins. This is not the case in the donation of *tachash* skin, where the color is also part of the animal donation, since the colors are part of the very essence of the animal's skin, to the point that it relates to the nature of the animal—"rejoiced and took pride in its colors" (as above).

It can be said that this is an additional reason why these curtains were the topmost covering of the Mishkan—because in addition to the fact that these curtains surpassed the others in their beauty, and furthermore, that the beauty of a dyed skin does not compare to the beauty and splendor of a skin that has a multitude of colors in its nature and birth—in *tachash* skins, the colors are part of the "animal" nature, as above.

E. One can add an explanation as to why the *tachash* skin covering was the topmost covering of the Mishkan: because this quality of the *tachash* skins—that even the color within them is part of the animal—contains a special expression of the inner content of the Mishkan as a whole:

"Animal" (*chai*) is a hint to Hashem, who is called "the Living One of the worlds" (*Chai HaOlamim*), because He, may He be blessed, is the source of life for all worlds, and in the language of the Rambam: "

## 139

All beings, from the heavens to the earth and what is between them, were found only from the truth of His existence." It is reasonable that the Rambam's intent in saying that "all beings... were found only from the truth of His existence" is not only regarding all beings in their totality, but also regarding the details of existence, that all details of existence "were found only from the truth of His existence."

That is to say:
Everything in the world is divided (in general) into three parts: 1) The essence of the thing's existence, which is a matter common to everything. 2) The specific nature of each thing, in which each thing is distinct from another—like fire and water, which are distinct in their nature and properties, to the point that the nature of fire is the opposite of the nature of water. 3) The other details of each thing, which are not part of its essential nature and content.

And all aspects of the aforementioned created being "were found only from the truth of His existence"—meaning that in addition to the essence of the creature's existence, and similarly the content and specific definition of each creature—even the secondary details were found from "the truth of His existence."

This is the matter of the curtains of "tachash skins" in the Mishkan:
Since the purpose of the Mishkan is—"And I will dwell among them", the dwelling of the Shechinah in a revealed manner, it is understood that it contained, in a revealed manner, the fact that all beings in all their details were found from the "truth of His existence." [As is explained in the sefarim, that the "physical forms" in the Mishkan and its vessels—one can "contemplate from them the supernal forms of which they are a likeness," which is because they are drawn down from them, as the Rema elaborated in his book *Toras HaOlah*].

Therefore, the Mishkan had to contain a matter indicating the connection between the external things of the creatures and the "truth of His existence"—and this is hinted at in "tachash skins," where even the color of their skin (which is only an external thing) is from "the animal kingdom"—to teach that even the most external things of the creatures, down to their color, "were found only from the truth of His existence," the "Living One of the worlds".

F. One can add to this, based on the explanation of our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe (in *Torah Or*) regarding the difference between the matter of a creature and its form, that the matter of the creatures was created by the word of Hashem and the Ten Utterances, whereas "the form of the firmament (for example)... was made by the will of Hashem, which did not reach the level of revelation in speech or utterance"—the reason for this is because "speech and utterance... is what activates the 'being'" in all creatures, for if it were created only "by the will and desire of Hashem alone... the world would be nullified in existence entirely," whereas in the form of the creatures (whose source is "the will of Hashem... which did not reach the level of revelation in speech or utterance"), the matter of *bitul* (nullification) is drawn into the creatures. See there at length.

[It can be said that this is expressed in the fact that the form of the creatures is an external thing that has no "content"—the inner reason for this is because in the form of the creature, the matter of *bitul* is drawn down, and therefore it comes in the form of a creature that has no "existence" and content of its own (like the "existence" in the essence of the creature's existence, and likewise in the content of the creature and its definitions)].

Based on this, one can understand more deeply the (inner) reason why the topmost covering was specifically of *tachash* skins, because the matter of *bitul* in the creatures (which is the primary content of the Mishkan—the dwelling of the Shechinah which effects a revealed *bitul* to Hashem) relates to the "form" of the creatures more than to the other parts of the creature (as above). And this *bitul* is hinted at specifically in "tachash skins," where even the color of the skin (which is only an external thing) is from "the animal kingdom," and on the contrary, this external thing relates to the very essence of the animal, more than the specific properties within it, which is why it "rejoiced and took pride" specifically in its colors.

G. Based on the above, one can also explain the reason why this matter was expressed specifically in the curtains of the Mishkan, for the distinctions in the parts of the Mishkan—the vessels, the boards, and the curtains of the Mishkan—correspond to the three aforementioned matters:

The vessels of the Sanctuary, each of which is distinct from the other and in each of which there was a different service according to the specific nature of the vessel, reveal how the content and specific definition of everything in the world is the word of Hashem and His specific life-force that constitutes and enlivens it, and the truth of its existence is as it is above;

The boards, which surround everything within them equally, point to an *ohr makif* (encompassing light), and through them it is effected and revealed that all beings were found only from the truth of His existence, a matter which is equal in all beings, for "all beings... from the highest form to a small gnat that might be in the center of the earth, all are found from the power of His truth";

And the curtains, which encompass everything in the Mishkan and the boards from above, also point to an *ohr makif*, but to a higher *makif*, which is why they needed to be dyed, and especially the upper curtains of the *tachash* skin covering—to teach that even the external form of things in the world is nullified to the "Living One of the worlds," and on the contrary, this nullification of the external form comes from a higher level, an *ohr makif* of holiness (will and desire) that is higher still.

H. The three aforementioned matters also exist in the service of man:

In every *mitzvah*—there are three matters:
1) The performance of the *mitzvah*, in which every single Jew is equal, from the greatest of the great to the smallest of the small (for example: the act of putting on *tefillin* by Moshe Rabbeinu is equal to the act of putting on *tefillin* by a simple person at all times), and in the language of the *Tanya*: "One Torah and one law for all of us in the fulfillment of all the Torah and *mitzvos* in the aspect of action."

2) The intent of the *mitzvah*, in which there are differences from one to another, and it depends on the person's level in his inner service in his mind and heart—the measure of his understanding of the Creator's greatness, etc., which leads to the love of Hashem and the fear of Hashem (and in the language of the Rambam's ruling: "And what is the way to His love and His fear? At the time that one contemplates, etc."), and these are "the hidden things to Hashem our G-d", regarding which it states in the *Zohar* that "each and every one according to his measure."

3) *Hiddur mitzvah* (beautification of the *mitzvah*, which is not indispensable to the fulfillment of the *mitzvah*), even to the point of beautification in details additional to the body of the *mitzvah*, and especially—matters that depend on custom.

Now, there is a place where a person might err and think that the main precision in fulfilling the *mitzvos* is only in the first two aforementioned matters, because:
The need for precision in the performance of the *mitzvos* is understood and simple, for the action is the main thing, as the Alter Rebbe elaborated in the *Tanya*, that "if one recited the *Shema* in his thought and heart alone with all the strength of his intent, he has not fulfilled his obligation and must go back and recite it... and if he uttered it with his lips but did not intend with his heart, he has fulfilled his obligation *bedieved* (after the fact)."

Likewise, the need for the intent of the *mitzvos* is understood, for a *mitzvah* without intent is "like a body without a soul," as the Alter Rebbe elaborated in the *Tanya* regarding the great necessity of fulfilling the *mitzvos* out of the love of Hashem and the fear of Hashem.

But regarding the additional matters, and especially the customs, what is the precision in them? And especially in customs pertaining to the education of young children, who do not understand and are not capable of setting their hearts on what they see and hear, and all the more so for infants at a very young age.

And to this comes the instruction that even color, the external hue, is part of the "animal kingdom," that things like external form must be done out of vitality, and vitality that comes from the essence of the soul. And many of the customs of Israel have their root higher than many matters of *dinim* (laws), and "the custom of Israel is Torah", "a custom overrides a law", as in the well-known response of the Rashba regarding this.

And through this, that each and every one adds in all matters of Torah and *mitzvos*—both the fulfillment of Torah and *mitzvos* in practice and *hiddur mitzvah*, "the revealed things to us and our children", both in "the hidden things to Hashem our G-d" in the service of Hashem in the love of Hashem and the fear of Hashem, and in precision in the customs of Israel—through this, "And I will dwell among them" is effected, within each and every one of the Jewish people.

And this brings closer and hastens even more the building of the Third Holy Temple, "the Sanctuary, O L-rd, which Your hands have established", for the Temple below is directed toward the Holy Temple above, speedily in our days, truly.

(From the Sicha of Shabbos Parshas Terumah, 5734)


## תצוה

## Sicha 2

A. "And I will meet there with the Children of Israel", and Rashi explains: "I will meet there — I will appoint a meeting with them for speech, like a king who designates a meeting place to speak with his servants there."

One must understand: The content of this matter was already stated in the preceding verse, "where I will meet with you to speak to you there," and Rashi explains: "When I appoint a time to speak to you, there I will appoint it to come." Similarly, it was stated earlier in Parshas Terumah, "And I will meet with you there and I will speak with you, etc.," and Rashi explains: "When I appoint a time for you to speak with you, that place I will appoint as the meeting time that I will come there to speak to you." What is Rashi’s intent in this additional parable of "a king who designates a meeting place to speak with his servants there," and why does he bring it only here, regarding the verse "And I will meet there with the Children of Israel"?

The Re’em explains that Rashi is troubled by why the matter of "speech" is not mentioned here (after "And I will meet there")—unlike in Parshas Terumah and the preceding verse here, where it explicitly says "and I will speak with you" or "to speak to you." Therefore, he "needed to explain 'I will meet with them for speech,' which means as if it said 'And I will meet there and I will speak.' And because it is difficult... to say that it is a 'shortened verse' missing the speech, he was forced to explain it as a king who designates a place, etc., which means that because it is the custom of a king to designate a place for speech, he said 'I will meet' instead of 'I will speak'."

Seemingly, this interpretation is strained, because (in addition to the fact that we find many times in Rashi that a verse is a "shortened verse," and in this case) since the previous verse explicitly states, "where I will meet... to speak to you," there is no need to repeat it again in the adjacent verse.

Furthermore, the content of the interpretation is strained, because it is difficult to say that the verse does not need to specify that Hashem’s "meeting" is for the purpose of "speech" because that is the custom of a flesh-and-blood king who "designates a meeting place to speak with his servants there"—when, on the contrary, it is common for Chazal to explain matters regarding Hashem by saying "like a king of flesh and blood" or similar expressions.

The Gur Aryeh writes that the reason Rashi brings the parable is "because it is difficult why it says simply 'And I will meet there with the Children of Israel' and does not write 'And I will meet to speak there to you,' and furthermore, it is already written 'where I will meet with you there.'" To this, he "answered with 'like a king who designates a meeting place, etc.,' meaning that the verse did not come to say anything other than that this place will be a fixed place to speak with them, like a king who designates a place for servants to speak with them. Therefore, he wrote additionally 'And I will meet with the Children of Israel,' meaning that this place is the place designated to speak with them, and he did not need to explain also *why* He meets with them, as he already mentioned that... and this verse only came to say that this place was designated with permanence, a matter which the verse had not yet mentioned—that the speech would be there with permanence."

His words require study, for the fact that "this place will be a fixed place to speak with them" is already stated in the verse (and Rashi) preceding it ("there I will appoint it to come") and also earlier in Parshas Terumah ("that place I will appoint... to speak to you"), as mentioned above.

Perhaps his intent (as is implied from the end of his words) is that the novelty in this verse is (not the permanence of the place of speech, but) "that the speech would be there with permanence" (and not just temporarily).

However, it is difficult to interpret Rashi’s language, "like a king who designates a meeting place to speak with his servants," in this way; according to his interpretation, the emphasis is not on the designation of the place, but on the designation of the time—that the king speaks with his servants in a permanent manner.

B. Seemingly, one can interpret Rashi’s intent simply:
The primary difficulty in the verse that Rashi comes to resolve is the phrase "And I will meet there with the Children of Israel", from which it is proven that the intent is a matter pertaining (not only to Moshe, but) to all of Israel.

Based on this, one must say, seemingly, that the intent of "I will meet" here is not "I will meet with them for speech" (which applies only to Moshe), but rather it is in the content of "And they gathered to you", to meet and assemble together, which refers to the resting of the Shechinah in the Mishkan, which is for all of Israel (as it says later, "And I will dwell among the Children of Israel... to dwell in their midst").

[Unlike in Parshas Terumah, where it explicitly says "And I will meet with you," where one must say that this is a matter pertaining only to Moshe, and therefore Rashi explains "When I appoint a time for you to speak with you." Also, in our Parshah, where it says "where I will meet with you there (to speak to you there)," and yet Rashi (as above) explains "When I appoint a time to speak to you there, etc." (and does not explain that "I will meet with you" is a separate matter referring to the resting of the Shechinah in the Mishkan for the Children of Israel)—the reason is understood, because it says "to speak to you" (and not "and I will speak to you"), which implies that this is the purpose of the "I will meet with you"].

This is what Rashi negates in his commentary, writing "I will meet with them for speech," meaning that here too the intent is the designation of a place for the purpose of speech (and not the resting of the Shechinah in the Mishkan, which was (not at a designated time, but) constantly).

To resolve how it is possible to say that "And I will meet there with the Children of Israel" means "I will meet with them for speech," when we never find that Hashem spoke to the Children of Israel from this place—for this, Rashi brings the parable of "a king who designates a meeting place to speak with his servants there":
Just as a king who wishes to convey something to his servants does not necessarily have to speak to them himself every time; rather, the king speaks to his ministers and servants, and they convey the king’s words—so too in the analogy: the meaning of "And I will meet there with the Children of Israel" is that Hashem designates a place to speak with the Children of Israel like a king who speaks with his servants, where the speech reaches them (at times) through a minister, etc., and likewise, Hashem’s speech reaches the Children of Israel through Moshe Rabbeinu.

It can be said that this was so in the simple sense: when Hashem wished to convey a commandment to the Children of Israel, the Children of Israel gathered at the entrance of the Ohel Moed, and Moshe heard Hashem’s words inside the tent and conveyed them to the Children of Israel.

[Based on this, one can also understand the continuation in the following verses—"And I will sanctify the Ohel Moed, etc., and Aaron, etc., I will sanctify to serve Me. And I will dwell in the midst of the Children of Israel and I will be to them for a G-d": At first, the verse explains regarding Moshe, that even though Hashem’s words are only to Moshe, nevertheless, it is like a king speaking with his servants, where the speeches are directed to all of Israel, and consequently, the statement "And I will meet there with the Children of Israel" applies to it. Following this, it says that this is the case regarding the Mishkan as a whole, that even though the service in the Mishkan belongs to the sons of Aaron the Kohen who serve in the Mishkan, nevertheless, the resting of the Shechinah in the Mishkan is (not only for Aaron and his sons, but) "in the midst of the Children of Israel"].

C. However, this interpretation does not fit Rashi’s language, because:
1) If Rashi’s intent is to resolve the phrase "(And I will meet there) with the Children of Israel," he should have copied these words in the heading.
2) If Rashi’s main novelty is that the intent of "I will meet" is for the purpose of speech, even though it says "with the Children of Israel"—Rashi should have been brief at the beginning of his commentary and written "I will meet there — for speech." Why does he preface it with "I will meet with them," which seemingly is nothing more than a repetition of the verse’s own language?
3) Why does Rashi write "for speech" and not "to speak" (like the language of the verse "to speak to you")?
4) And most importantly: This question already arises in the previous verse, "where I will meet with you... to speak to you," from which it is proven that the speech to Moshe (to you) is called "I will meet with you" (with the Children of Israel), and one must say that this is because the speeches to Moshe are for the sake of Israel. If so, Rashi should have brought the parable there.

D. Therefore, it seems appropriate to interpret Rashi’s words in a different way, with the following preface:
The verses "where I will meet, etc." and "And I will meet, etc." come in continuation of what is written, "a continual burnt offering for your generations at the entrance of the Ohel Moed before Hashem—where I will meet, etc.," and simply put, this is the explanation of "Ohel Moed", which is so called because there "I will meet with you, etc., to speak to you, etc., and I will meet there with the Children of Israel."

Based on this, one can say that the two verses—(a) "where I will meet with you, etc., to speak to you," (b) "And I will meet there with the Children of Israel"—are two different matters regarding the "Ohel Moed."

The explanation of this:
Later in Parshas Tisa it is explained that after the sin of the Golden Calf, Moshe took his tent outside the camp "and called it Ohel Moed, and it was that everyone who sought Hashem would go out to the Ohel Moed, etc.," and Rashi there says, "And he called it—and he would call it Ohel Moed, it is a house of meeting for those who seek Torah." Afterward it says, "And it was when Moshe would come to the tent, the pillar of cloud would descend and stand at the entrance of the tent and He would speak with Moshe," meaning that Moshe’s tent (which he called "Ohel Moed") was the place where Hashem would speak with him. It turns out that in Moshe’s tent there were two matters: (a) "a house of meeting for those who seek Torah," (b) the place where Hashem would speak to Moshe.

Now, this order in Moshe’s tent did not prevail until the Mishkan was erected, as Rashi says there, "And this matter Moshe practiced from Yom Kippur until the Mishkan was erected and no more... once it was erected, He did not speak with him except from the Ohel Moed," meaning that after the erection of the Mishkan, these two matters that were in Moshe’s tent moved to the Mishkan.

As we find, in addition to the fact that the Mishkan was the place where Hashem spoke to Moshe, the Mishkan was a "house of meeting" for Israel, a place for the gathering of Israel, as is explicitly stated in the verse, "for the calling of the congregation, etc., and all the congregation gathered to you at the entrance of the Ohel Moed." We also find this regarding the daughters of Zelophehad, who stood "before Moshe, etc., and all the congregation at the entrance of the Ohel Moed" (and Rashi there says, "They were in the Beis Medrash, etc.").

E. It can be said that this is Rashi’s intent here in what he writes, "And I will meet there — I will meet with them":
The meaning of the word "I will meet" in the previous verse, "where I will meet with you, etc., to speak to you," is the designation of a place, as in Rashi’s language, "When I appoint a time, etc., there I will appoint it to come" (and likewise "And I will meet" earlier in Parshas Terumah, where Rashi says, "When I appoint a time, etc., that place I will appoint for the meeting"), and as in the Targum Onkelos, which is a term of invitation, meaning that it is designated and set apart for a specific matter.

But in the verse at hand, the intent is a second matter: "And I will meet" is a term meaning "I will meet with them," from the root of "house of meeting" (and in the language of the aforementioned verse, "and all the congregation gathered to you"). The reason it says "with the Children of Israel" is understood simply, because the intent is a place for the gathering of the congregation.

[It turns out that in the "Ohel Moed" there are two explanations: (a) "where I will meet, etc., to speak to you"—a place fixed and prepared for Hashem’s speech to Moshe. (b) "And I will meet there with the Children of Israel"—a "house of meeting" for the Children of Israel].

The reason Rashi adds "(I will meet with them) for speech" is because the verse says "And I will meet there," meaning that this is not just a "house of meeting" of Israel among themselves, but the place where Hashem "meets" with Israel. Therefore, Rashi needs to explain what the "connection" is between Hashem and Israel in this place, and so he says "for speech."

Based on this, the precision of Rashi’s language "for speech" (and he did not use the language of the previous verse "to speak") is also resolved: This "meeting" between Hashem and Israel for speech is not only in that in this place Hashem speaks to Israel (through Moshe, as mentioned above), but also the opposite: when the Children of Israel need to request something from Hashem, they gather to this "house of meeting" to pray to Him, may He be blessed.

[This is precise in Rashi’s language in the parable of "a king who designates a meeting place to speak with his servants there"—for seemingly he should have said "to his servants" (similar to the language of the previous verse "to speak to you" (not "with you" or "to you"), even though there it refers to Moshe)—but Rashi’s intent in this is to be precise that the emphasis is not that this is the place where the king speaks to his servants, but that this is the fixed place where the king "converses" with his servants (including also that the servants speak there with the king)].

One should not ask, where do we find (in the simple sense of the verse) that the Children of Israel prayed at the entrance of the Ohel Moed—because this is something self-evident (and Rashi does not need to explain it), that when there is a need to pray, one does so in a place of gathering (like a synagogue), and in the desert, this was at the entrance of the Ohel Moed, the place of "the calling of the congregation."

Especially since we find this explicitly regarding the Beis Hamikdash, which is called "My house is a house of prayer," that in addition to the fact that the Beis Hamikdash is the place of the resting of the Shechinah and where sacrifices were offered, it is also the place where Israel gathered to pray to Hashem, "And they will pray and supplicate to You in this house" [It can be said that this is also understood from Rashi earlier on the verse "And this is the gate of heaven"—"a place of prayer for their prayers to ascend to heaven"].

And since the Mishkan is also called "a sanctuary," "And they shall make Me a sanctuary and I will dwell in their midst", it is understood that before the building of the Beis Hamikdash, the Mishkan was a "place of prayer".

May it be the will that this shall be for us, that through our engagement in "the form of the House... and its exits and its entrances," we will merit the building of the Beis Hamikdash (the Third), at which time the Mishkan (and all its vessels) that Moshe made will also be revealed, "And I will meet there with the Children of Israel," speedily in our days, truly.

(From the Sicha of Shabbos Parshas Tetzaveh, 5740)


## פורים

## Sicha 2

### 177

A. The Gemara states: "Rava said: A person is obligated to become intoxicated (*lebasumei*—to become drunk on wine) on Purim until he does not know the difference between 'Cursed is Haman' and 'Blessed is Mordechai'." And it continues: "Rabbah and Rabbi Zeira held their Purim meal together. They became intoxicated (*istabsum*—they became drunk), and Rabbah arose and slaughtered Rabbi Zeira. The next day, he prayed for mercy and revived him. The following year, he said to him, 'Let the Master come and we shall hold the Purim meal together.' He replied, 'A miracle does not occur at every hour and every moment.'"

On the face of it, this story, according to its simple meaning, requires explanation and clarification. How is it possible that Rabbah could fail in a matter involving (even the accessories of) bloodshed, Heaven forbid?

In *Chiddushei Aggados*, the Maharsha explains that there was no actual death here (that he "slaughtered" him in the literal sense), but rather "something akin to slaughter, for he pressed him to drink more than enough until he fell ill and was near death." A similar explanation is given in the *Hagahos Yaavetz*: "He did not slaughter him in reality... (but) through an optical illusion... (only) that the onlookers thought he had truly slaughtered him... even Rabbi Zeira was affected by them, he became terrified and fainted from the intensity of the worry, etc."

However, in addition to the fact that the simple reading of the story—and especially its conclusion, "the next day he prayed for mercy and revived him"—is not understood according to this, it still does not resolve Rabbah's conduct. After all, the act ultimately harmed Rabbi Zeira's health (to the point that he "was near death"), which is a Torah prohibition against injuring one's fellow in any manner, and especially—as mentioned above.

In *Teshuvos Chasam Sofer*, he links the fact that Rabbah "slaughtered" Rabbi Zeira with the Gemara's statement: "He who is born under Mars will be a man who sheds blood... Rabbah said, 'I am under Mars, etc.'"

But see the Yaavetz, who writes: "However, Rabbah certainly did not shed blood, even if he was under Mars." Seemingly, one cannot say that there was any matter of bloodshed with Rabbah, Heaven forbid. As is explained in the Rambam and elsewhere, the fact that a person is born with a certain nature (including the constellation of Mars) does not contradict free will. On the contrary, he is certainly given the strength to overcome his natural inclinations, and this was certainly the case with Rabbah.

## 178

Even more puzzling:
The continuation of the story explicitly states (as mentioned above): "The following year, he said to him, 'Let the Master come and we shall hold the Purim meal together.'" That is to say: not only do we not find in the Gemara that Rabbah did *teshuvah* for this act, but furthermore, the following year he again invited Rabbi Zeira to celebrate the Purim meal with him (despite knowing that what happened the previous year might happen again)!

Furthermore: The reason Rabbi Zeira did not accept Rabbah's invitation was only because "a miracle does not occur at every hour and every moment." This implies that, in principle, he was prepared to celebrate the Purim meal together with Rabbah again this year, even though it was possible that Rabbah would do what he did the previous year. The only reason he did not agree was because he was not certain that the miracle would happen again this time—that Rabbah would revive him after Purim!

B. We find explanations based on *derush* and *sod* (Kabbalah) that this story—that "they became intoxicated and Rabbah arose and slaughtered Rabbi Zeira"—alludes to spiritual matters. According to this, it is possible that it is merely a parable, similar to the matter of "becoming intoxicated" in the story of Rabbah bar bar Chana, which is merely a parable (and similar to many of the stories in the *sugya* of Rabbah bar bar Chana, which are parables and allusions to spiritual matters, and did not actually occur in physical reality).

However, we cannot interpret it this way in our case (that the story of Rabbah and Rabbi Zeira is only a parable and allusion to a spiritual matter), for this story is brought in the continuation (as a support, reinforcement, and example) of the halachic ruling in the Gemara, "A person is obligated to become intoxicated on Purim until he does not know," that Rabbah and Rabbi Zeira fulfilled the obligation "to become intoxicated... until he does not know" in actual reality. Since it is a matter of *halacha*, it must be interpreted that this story occurred as it is literally written, and not (only) in spiritual matters [unlike the aforementioned stories of Rabbah bar bar Chana, which were brought in the *Aggadah* section of the Gemara].

This is also proven by the words of the Ran and the *Baal HaMaor*, who cite that "Rabbi Ephraim, of blessed memory, wrote regarding that incident where Rabbah arose and slaughtered Rabbi Zeira... the statement of Rava is rejected, and the *halacha* is not in accordance with it, and it is not proper to act in this way." This proves that their opinion is, simply put, that the story of Rabbah and Rabbi Zeira is literal—and that damage (death) was caused to Rabbi Zeira, for otherwise there would be no basis to say that because of this, the obligation to become intoxicated on Purim should be nullified.

C. Therefore, it seems correct to say that this story occurred literally, that as a result of the state of "intoxication," a literal death was caused (by Rabbah) to Rabbi Zeira—and yet, this death (and Rabbah's act) was in such a manner that it contains no trace of even the accessories of bloodshed, or any undesirable matter at all, to the extent that even for the following year, Rabbah wanted to repeat the same conduct.

This can be explained by prefacing it with what is explained regarding the death of Nadav and Avihu—that as the Sages say,

## 179

they died because "they entered the Sanctuary intoxicated with wine," and this death was connected to (a result of) drinking too much wine.

The well-known difficulty is: It is explicitly written in the Torah that Moshe said to Aharon, "It is what Hashem spoke, saying: 'Through those near to Me I will be sanctified,'" and Rashi explains, "Now I see that they are greater than I and than you." How is it possible that there could be an undesirable matter with them, especially to such an extent that they would enter the Sanctuary "intoxicated with wine"?

This is explained based on what is written in the holy *Ohr HaChayim*, that Nadav and Avihu died because of the greatness of their desire to draw close to Hashem, that "they did not refrain from drawing close to the pleasant, sweet, friendly, beloved, and sweet kisses of attachment, until their souls expired from them." In the words of the verse, "When they drew near before Hashem and they died," meaning, the cause of their death was "their drawing near before Hashem."

This is also the meaning of "they entered the Sanctuary intoxicated with wine": The intention of "wine" is the secrets and the inner dimensions of the Torah, as the saying goes, "Wine enters, secrets emerge." Just as physical wine reveals what is hidden in a person's heart, so too is it in spirituality: the "wine of Torah" is the revelation of the secrets of the Torah. And "intoxicated with wine" indicates the greatness and intensity of the revelation of the inner Torah, to the point of a state of "expiration of the soul" [for just as with "intoxicated with wine" in the literal sense, the wine causes a nullification of the senses, so too the greatness of this revelation and this longing caused them a nullification of the senses, to the point of expiration of the soul].

Therefore, after this event, the Torah warned, "Do not drink wine or strong drink," that it is forbidden to drink "wine in a way of intoxication," which hints that when one "drinks" the wine of Torah—the secrets and mysteries of the Torah—one must be careful not to reach a state of "intoxication" of "expiration of the soul." It must be in a manner of "entering in peace and departing in peace," as is the well-known interpretation of this: that the entry into the "Pardes" must be "in peace," in such a way that the departure will certainly be "in peace"—souls within bodies.

## 180

D. It is simple that this explanation of "they entered the Sanctuary intoxicated with wine" is not meant to remove the matter and the verse from its literal meaning—for it is explicitly written in the verse (as mentioned above) that after this, the Torah warned, "Do not drink wine or strong drink," the prohibition to drink "wine in a way of intoxication" literally.
