Therefore, it must be that even with Nadav and Avihu, there was a matter of drinking wine (also) in the physical sense.

The meaning of this can be said to be that with Nadav and Avihu, the drinking of wine in the literal sense was connected with the revelation of the "wine" of Torah (the secrets and mysteries of the Torah). As is explained in the *Shelah*, that we find with "the holy ones who are in the land... who sometimes drank much more than enough at great feasts... their intention in this was for the sake of Heaven, because through the drinking of wine they would become very joyous, and through this they would say many, many words of Torah at the table, twofold for wisdom... for through joy the wise man reveals the secrets of the Torah... for with the increase of joy, the intellectual power that is in the soul is strengthened, and then he is more prepared to reveal the hidden things of wisdom... 'Wine enters, secrets emerge'... that through the drinking of wine, words of Torah, which are called secrets, emerged, etc." And so it was with Nadav and Avihu, who were extremely holy—"Through those near to Me I will be sanctified," as mentioned above—that through "drinking... much more than enough," the secrets of the Torah were revealed to them until they reached expiration of the soul.

Based on this, it can be said that this was also the reason for Rabbi Zeira's death—that it was similar to the death of Nadav and Avihu. That which it says "he slaughtered him" means (as the Maharsha says) "he pressed him to drink more than enough," and "he fell ill and was near death" (not through the physical wine, but) means expiration of the soul through the abundance of "drinking" (revelation) of the "wine" of Torah (the secrets of the Torah).

Nevertheless, this does not remove the matter from its literal sense—that there was a drinking of wine in the literal sense (similar to the above regarding Nadav and Avihu); and Rabbi Zeira's death was also literal, a separation of the soul from the body (not just fainting)—and therefore Rabbi Ephraim holds that because of the incident of Rabbah and Rabbi Zeira, the obligation to become intoxicated is nullified.

According to this, it is well understood that there is no accessory of bloodshed here at all, Heaven forbid, for the death was the expiration of the soul through Rabbi Zeira's study of the secrets of the Torah.

In this, the Gemara's emphasis on "they became intoxicated" (*istabsum*—plural) is also explained: that Rabbah and Rabbi Zeira both became intoxicated, and it implies that the intoxication of both of them caused "Rabbah to arise and slaughter Rabbi Zeira"—because (not only Rabbah's act, but) Rabbi Zeira's death was also because he reached a state of intoxication that leads to the "expiration of the soul" of a person, as mentioned above, except that this was done to him by Rabbah, "Rabbah arose and slaughtered Rabbi Zeira," as will be explained in section VI.

E. This is the content of the story that "Rabbah and Rabbi Zeira held their Purim meal together and they became intoxicated":
Their drinking of wine (literally) was for the intention mentioned above in the *Shelah*, that through the drinking of wine, the wine of Torah, the secrets of the Torah, would be revealed to them. And so it was, that through their drinking at this meal, secrets and mysteries of the Torah were revealed to them, which were extremely lofty, to the ultimate level of "until he does not know."

## 181

However, even though both of them became intoxicated, nevertheless, only with Rabbi Zeira did this lead to an actual expiration of the soul. The reason is that Rabbah had a greater intellect ("*mochin d'gadlus*"), and therefore he was able to receive the intensity of the revelation of the secrets of the Torah, whereas Rabbi Zeira could not contain this revelation [as is also alluded to in their names: "Rabbah" indicates greatness (*gadlus*), and "Zeira" indicates smallness (*katan*), from the word *ze'eira*].

Therefore, with Rabbi Zeira, this led to an actual expiration of the soul, to the point that his soul separated from his body literally; whereas Rabbah (in himself) did not reach expiration of the soul.

F. Based on this, we can explain the meaning of "Rabbah arose and slaughtered Rabbi Zeira" [for on the face of it, it should have said "he killed him" or something similar]:
The Gemara states, "Slaughtering is nothing other than drawing," meaning that the content of the matter of slaughter is—(drawing and) elevation, for through the slaughter, one prepares the animal for human consumption, and thereby elevates the animal from the category of the "living" (*chai*) to the category of the "speaking" (*medaber*—human) (for it becomes blood and flesh like his own flesh—that of the speaker), and as is explained in many places, for this reason slaughter was permitted despite the matter of cruelty to animals—because through this, one elevates the slaughtered one to a higher level.

In our case: The meaning of "Rabbah slaughtered Rabbi Zeira" ("he pressed him to drink more than enough") is—that Rabbah drew (slaughtered) and elevated Rabbi Zeira to an incomparably higher level (by revealing to him extremely deep secrets and mysteries of the Torah ("more than enough") relative to his regular study according to his level), and this matter brought him to an actual expiration of the soul, just like with Nadav and Avihu.

Both matters exist—that the intention of "slaughtered" is the expiration and separation of the soul from the body literally (not just fainting), and together with this—it is not a matter of bloodshed, Heaven forbid, but on the contrary, an extremely lofty matter—expiration of the soul (similar to Nadav and Avihu).

[And this is also the precision of the language "Rabbah arose and slaughtered Rabbi Zeira"—for on the face of it, it should have said "Rabbah slaughtered Rabbi Zeira," and what is "Rabbah arose"?
The explanation for this can be said to be: "Arising" (*kum*) indicates Rabbah's ascent to an extremely lofty level ("And he arose" (*vayakam*)—he had a rising), "to become intoxicated until he does not know." However, regarding Rabbah himself, since he had the vessels of "Rabbah" (greatness), even when he was in a state of "arising" (an extremely great ascent even relative to Rabbah's level), it did not necessitate expiration of the soul. Whereas regarding Rabbi Zeira, when Rabbah revealed to him the matters he had attained then (in the state of "arising"), through this—his soul expired, "he slaughtered Rabbi Zeira," literally.]

And on the face of it, how could Rabbah bring Rabbi Zeira to expiration of the soul, which is the opposite of the intention that it must be "for it to be inhabited," specifically souls within bodies—for Rabbah said and assessed that Rabbi Zeira was on a level similar to Rabbah and would not reach expiration of the soul.

In a deeper sense, it can be said: Since Rabbah had the power to act "and he revived him"—to return Rabbi Zeira's soul to his body, therefore it was not in contradiction to the intention of specifically souls within bodies, for the expiration

## 182

of the soul with Rabbi Zeira was only a temporary matter—during the day of Purim (when there is the obligation "to become intoxicated... until he does not know")—whereas "the next day" (after Purim, when there is no obligation "to become intoxicated... until he does not know," and on the contrary)—"he prayed for mercy and revived him," in accordance with the ultimate intention—specifically souls within bodies, similar to "entering in peace (for a time, and afterwards) departing in peace."

G. According to the above, the continuation of the Gemara's story also fits well—"The following year, he said to him, 'Let the Master come and we shall hold the Purim meal together.' He replied, 'A miracle does not occur at every hour and every moment'":
Not only did Rabbah not regret what happened at the previous Purim meal, but on the contrary—he invited Rabbi Zeira to hold the Purim meal together again this year, with the intention that this year too, he would effect in Rabbi Zeira such a great elevation to the point of expiration of the soul. And since the ultimate intention is souls within bodies—after Purim he would return and revive him (as was the act of the Master the previous year), and Rabbi Zeira would have two merits: the elevation of the expiration of the soul and the elevation of fulfilling the intention of souls within bodies.
[Or in a slightly different way: Rabbah thought that during the year, Rabbi Zeira had ascended to a higher level, and it was already within his ability to attain extremely deep secrets and mysteries of the Torah in such a way that he would not reach actual expiration of the soul, the separation of the soul from the body.]

To this, Rabbi Zeira answered him—"A miracle does not occur at every hour and every moment": He desired and longed for the elevation of the expiration of the soul [similar to "When will it come to my hand and I will fulfill it"], and for this reason he wanted to hold the Purim meal together with Rabbah again this year; but nevertheless, since the ultimate intention is souls within bodies, he feared that after he reached expiration of the soul, his soul would no longer want to descend and be clothed in his body, and who knows if this time too Rabbah would succeed in effecting such a "miracle" that Rabbi Zeira's soul would want to descend and be clothed in his body again—"A miracle does not occur at every hour and every moment."

H. Now, according to *halacha*, it is ruled in the *Shulchan Aruch* that there is an obligation to become intoxicated on Purim until he does not know, and we do not fear that any undesirable matter will branch out from this, etc.

From this, it is also understood regarding the spiritual content of drinking wine "until he does not know"—that is, to study in a manner of "you shall be ravished with her love," the secrets ("wine") of the Torah, until one reaches a state of "intoxication" and nullification of his existence,

## 183

that on Purim, the power is given to every single Jew, each and every one with all your might, according to his own measure, to fulfill this "drinking" and to reach such an attachment to the secrets of the Torah that nullifies his existence, without fear that from this *avodah* he will come to a conduct of isolation and withdrawal from the world, the opposite of the intention of "for it to be inhabited." But on the contrary, through fulfilling this obligation on the day of Purim, vitality will be added to him in the *avodah* of Hashem throughout the entire year, in making a dwelling place for Him, may He be blessed, in the lower worlds—"entering in peace and departing in peace."

(From the *sichos* of Shabbos Parshas Shemini and Shabbos Parshas Tazria, 5744)


## תשא

## Sicha 1

## 184

### Sicha 1

A. The Rambam writes in *Hilchos Tefillah* : "Five things impede prayer... the purification of the hands, etc." Further on, he explains: "What does this refer to? That one does not purify himself for prayer except for his hands alone in other prayers, besides the morning prayer. But for the morning prayer, he washes his face, hands, and feet, and afterwards he shall pray." In his *Hassagos* there, the Raavad writes: "I do not know why the feet."

In the *Migdal Oz*, it is written regarding the Raavad’s critique: "I, his student, found it taught in a *Baraisa* in the final *shema'ata* of the chapter *Bamah Tomnin*, where we conclude that they hold like that which is taught: 'A person washes his face, hands, and feet every day for the sake of his Creator, as it is said : "Hashem has made everything for His own purpose."'"

The *Kesef Mishneh* writes regarding this: "It seems to me that this *Baraisa* did not escape the Raavad... for this *Baraisa* was not taught regarding prayer, but rather regarding whether it is permitted to wash one’s face and feet in order to beautify oneself, as Rashi explains."

According to this, the dispute between the Rambam and the Raavad is in the interpretation of the aforementioned *Baraisa*. According to the Rambam, this *Baraisa* was taught regarding prayer, while according to the Raavad, it was not taught regarding prayer, but rather regarding whether it is permitted to wash one’s face and feet to beautify oneself.

The *Acharonim* added an explanation of the Rambam’s position based on a manuscript version of the *Shas* in *Shabbos* there: "He washes... his face, hands, and feet, because it is said : 'Prepare to meet your G-d, O Israel... Hashem has made everything, etc.'" The Rambam had this version in the *Shas*, that this washing is "because it is said: 'Prepare to meet your G-d, O Israel,'" and therefore he explains that the *Baraisa* is taught regarding prayer. Whereas for the Raavad, the version is as we have it, where only the verse "Hashem has made everything for His own purpose" is cited, and therefore he explains that the intent of the *Baraisa* is not regarding an obligation before prayer, but rather a permission to beautify oneself.

This requires clarification:
1) Why did the Rambam not cite the verse which is the source for the innovation of this law — "because it is said: 'Prepare to meet your G-d, O Israel'" (as it is cited in the commentary of the *Geonim* and in the books of the students of Rashi), in the same way that they cited it later regarding the prayer of a *baal keri*?

## 185

2) According to this version of the *Baraisa*, there is seemingly no difference between the morning prayer and the *Mincha* prayer, etc., for "Prepare to meet your G-d, O Israel" applies to other prayers as well. If so, the reasoning of the Rambam, who wrote that this law applies only to the morning prayer, is not understood.

B. In the *Shev HaKohen*, it is explained that the Rambam’s reason is like the words of the Rashba, who wrote regarding the distinction between the morning prayer and the *Mincha* and *Ma'ariv* prayers: "Because in the morning we become a new creation, as it is written : 'They are new every morning; great is Your faithfulness... We must thank Him, may He be blessed, for creating us to honor Him, to serve Him, and to bless in His name. And for this matter, they instituted in the morning all those blessings that we recite every single morning. Therefore, we must sanctify ourselves with His holiness and wash our hands from a vessel, like a *kohen* who sanctifies his hands from the *kiyor* before his service.'"

Based on this, he explains : "Since every *kohen* had to sanctify his hands and feet before the service in the morning, therefore one must wash his hands and feet for the morning prayer. And since a *kohen* who performs the service, once he washes his hands in the morning, does not need to sanctify them again the entire day, therefore they did not institute [washing] for other prayers, only the washing of hands because the hands are 'busy' [i.e., prone to touching things]." (Based on this, he wrote there that the version in the Rambam is "he washes his hands and feet," omitting the word "and his face," since a *kohen* only sanctifies his hands and feet, and that this appears to be the case from the Raavad’s critique, as he critiqued only "his feet" and not also what the Rambam said, "and his face").

Now, the law of washing hands before prayer according to the reasoning of the Rashba was brought by our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe in the *Shulchan Aruch* in the laws of *netilas yadayim*. He writes: "Every

## 186

person who rises from his bed in the morning, whether he relieved himself or did not relieve himself, must wash his hands... from a vessel, and even if he does not want to pray until several hours later. For when Hashem returns a person’s soul to him, he becomes a new creation, as it is written: 'They are new every morning,' etc. For man deposits his soul weary, and Hashem returns it to him new and refreshed in order to serve Hashem with all his ability and to serve Him all this day, for this is the whole of man. Therefore, we must sanctify ourselves with His holiness and wash our hands from a vessel in order to perform His service and serve Him, like a *kohen* who would sanctify his hands from the *kiyor* every day before his service."

From the words of the Alter Rebbe, that the obligation of this washing exists "even if he does not want to pray until several hours later," it is proven that this washing is not related to prayer, but is a preparation for the totality of the service of Hashem "all this day" (and this is also understood from the fact that the Alter Rebbe brought this law in the laws of *netilas yadayim*, unlike the Rambam, who brought it in *Hilchos Tefillah*).

We find another difference in the words of the Alter Rebbe there compared to the Rambam — the Rambam also brought the obligation of washing the feet, which according to the aforementioned explanation (from *Shev HaKohen*) has its source in the sanctification of the feet of the *kohen*; whereas the Alter Rebbe (like the Rashba) brought only the obligation of washing the hands — even though he also cited the reason that this obligation relates to the sanctification (of hands and feet) of the *kohen*.

It can be said that these two differences depend on one another, as follows.

C. The obligation of "And Aharon and his sons shall wash, etc., and they shall wash, etc." contains two aspects:
1) According to its literal meaning, the reason for this washing is (as the Ramban writes) "a way of honor from above, for anyone who approaches the table of kings to serve them, etc., washes his hands because the hands are 'busy,' etc." It can be said that this includes not only cleanliness in the literal sense, but also the category of *taharah*, like the *taharah* from *tumah* which is achieved through immersion. According to this, the sanctification of the hands and feet is akin to the matter of immersion that must take place before entering the *Azarah* for service, even though one is already *tahor*.
2) From the language of the *Targum* "and they shall sanctify, etc." and from the language of *Chazal* "sanctification of hands and feet," it is implied that this is not a matter of *taharah* alone, but that there is an addition of *kedushah* through this washing.

The practical difference between them can be said to be: regarding the law of

## 187

*taharah* in the sanctification of hands and feet, there is one law for the hands and feet, for just as in immersion one must immerse his entire body, likewise it is regarding the sanctification of hands and feet, where the hands and feet for this purpose are like one entity, and there is no *taharah* in halves. Whereas the law of *kedushah* in the sanctification of hands and feet is primarily in the hands, in which the service of the *Mikdash* is actually performed (and the feet are only like an aid — it should be standing on the floor, etc.).

[It can be said that both aspects are hinted at in the two verses in the *Parsha* : "And Aharon and his sons shall wash from it their hands and their feet," and "And they shall wash their hands and their feet so that they do not die." The second verse refers to the law of *taharah* in the sanctification of hands and feet, and therefore it says "their hands and their feet" together without a break, because regarding *taharah* they are like one body and one entity. Not so the first verse, which refers to the matter of *kedushah*, regarding which it says "And Aharon and his sons shall wash from it their hands and their feet", for the hands and feet are not in the same category and entity, and the sanctification of the feet comes as an addition to the sanctification of the hands].

D. It can be said that this is the difference between the *halacha* in the Rambam (according to the explanation of *Shev HaKohen*) and the law in the *Shulchan Aruch* of the Alter Rebbe:
According to the Rambam, the washing of hands, etc., in the present time is akin to the law of *taharah* in the sanctification of hands and feet, as is explicit in his language in "Five things that impede prayer" — "purification of the hands, etc.," and in this he adds that for the morning, the purification of the hands is not enough, but there must also be the washing of (his face and) his feet, like the sanctification of hands and feet of the *kohen* before his service in the *Mikdash*.

However, the Alter Rebbe holds, in his interpretation of the words of the Rashba, that this enactment is not from the aspect of the matter of

## 188

*taharah*, but from the aspect of the law of *kedushah*, as is emphasized in the language of the Rashba: "Therefore we must sanctify ourselves with His holiness and wash our hands from a vessel like a *kohen* who sanctifies, etc.," and he did not mention the language of *taharah*. And therefore he holds that this is fulfilled even with the washing of hands alone, since even in the sanctification of hands and feet in the *Mikdash*, the essence of the sanctification was in the hands.

The basis of the difference between them can be said to be:
According to the Rambam, the fact that they enacted the washing of hands and feet akin to the sanctification of hands and feet of a *kohen* is because the prayers were enacted corresponding to the sacrifices, and since the prayer is like the service of the *kohen* in the *Mikdash*, therefore they must sanctify the hands and feet before the morning prayer.

Now, the basis of the difference between the law of *taharah* in the sanctification of hands and feet and the law of *kedushah* in it can be said to be: the law of *taharah* is primarily from the aspect of the *gavra* (the person), that one who is not *tahor* is not fit for service. Not so the law of *kedushah*, which is from the aspect of the *kedushah* of the *Mikdash*, that before one enters or performs service in the *Mikdash*, he must be in a state of extra *kedushah*.

And this is the reason that the Rambam holds that the washing of hands, etc., before prayer is only akin to the category of *taharah* in the sanctification of hands and feet of a *kohen*, because the fact that prayer is in place of sacrifices does not imbue it with *kedushah* as in the *Mikdash*, and the comparison is only from the aspect of the *gavra*, that the service of the one praying is like the service of the one offering.

Not so the Alter Rebbe, who holds that the similarity to the service of the *kohen* is not because prayer is in place of a sacrifice, but because "man deposits his soul weary, and Hashem returns it to him new and refreshed in order to serve Hashem with all his ability and to serve Him all this day, for this is the whole of man. Therefore, we must sanctify ourselves with His holiness and wash our hands from a vessel in order to perform His service and serve Him."

For this reason, he holds that this washing is not related to the category of *taharah*, for the category of *taharah* is possible only as a preparation for a specific service (prayer, Torah study, performing a *mitzvah*, etc.). Not so the fact that a person serves Hashem "all the day... for this is the whole of man," in which is included all his actions throughout the entire day (including also "all your deeds for the sake of Heaven," until (in the language of the Rambam) that he "serves Hashem always") — and therefore he holds that this is akin to the law of *kedushah* in the sanctification of hands and feet, for this *kedushah* does not have the intent of purifying oneself to be distinct from his previous state in order to be able to work and serve, but is like a preparation for service and ministry.

E. Now, it was brought above that according to the common version of the Rambam, the washing of the face is also mentioned, which was not part of the sanctification of hands and feet of the *kohanim* in the *Mikdash*.

It can be explained that the difference (according to this version)

## 189

between the washing before the morning prayer (which also includes the washing of the face) and the washing of the hands and feet of the *kohanim* is based on the inner dimensions of the matters:
The difference between the face and the hands and feet is that in the hands and feet dwells the power of action of the person, whereas the face is the place of the dwelling of the higher powers within him: the power of intellect, the sense of sight, hearing, speech, etc.

Their meaning in the service of man is that the hands and feet point to the external powers of the *nefesh*, which are clothed in the matters of this world, whereas the face is the internal powers, which are not (so much) occupied with the matters of the world. For the matters of the world must be done without a "heart and heart" [i.e., insincerely], without putting one’s head and heart into it, only "the labor of your hands you shall eat" — the labor of your hands specifically, and not the labor of the mind and heart.

It can be said that this is the difference according to the Rambam (for this version) between the sanctification of hands and feet in the time of the *Beis Hamikdash* before the service of the *kohanim*, and the washing which is a preparation and *taharah* before the morning prayer, which includes also the washing of the face:
In the time of the *Beis Hamikdash*, when the service of Israel in general was as it should be, meaning that the face — the internal powers of intellect and *midos* — were always separated from the mundane and the matters of the world, and especially in a *kohen* who is separated to stand to serve before Hashem, there was no need for the *taharah* of the face, only the sanctification and *taharah* of the hands and feet, which are the external powers of the person with which he eats and drinks — things that belong to "your deeds."

But in the time of exile, and in every one of Israel who is obligated in prayers, one must rectify the matter of *taharah* also for people whose involvement in the matters of the world is in a way that they also put their intellect and heart, their internality, into it. Therefore, there must also be the *taharah* of the face, the *taharah* of the internal powers, every day before the morning prayer.

F. But all this is according to the Rambam, that this washing is akin to the category of *taharah* in the sanctification of hands and feet.
But according to what the Alter Rebbe wrote, that "we must sanctify ourselves with His holiness... in order to perform His service and serve Him," the category of *kedushah* for the totality of the day’s service, then even in the present time there is no need for the washing of the face.

The explanation for this:
This *netilah* is immediately in the morning when he rises from his bed, as the Alter Rebbe’s language at the beginning of the section, and it comes in continuation of the saying of "I offer thanks before You, living and eternal King, for You have returned my soul to me in compassion; great is Your faithfulness". Even though the reason in simple terms that this saying can be done with unpurified hands is "because there is no *kedushah* in the words of this formula, since it contains no Name", it is explained elsewhere that the inner reason for this is because all the *tumah* in the world cannot make the "I offer thanks" of a Jew *tamei*. Even if he is lacking in this matter or another, his "I offer thanks" always remains in its completeness.

## 190

And this is that the gratitude in the saying of "I offer thanks" has its root in the simple will of every one of Israel, which is always present in every *nefesh* of Israel, whoever he may be and in whatever state he may be, like the well-known ruling of the Rambam that every person of Israel always wants to fulfill the will of his Creator.

And this matter is emphasized immediately upon his rising from his sleep, because "when Hashem returns a person’s soul to him, he becomes a new creation, as it is written: 'They are new every morning,' etc. For man deposits his soul weary, and Hashem returns it to him new and refreshed in order to serve Hashem with all his ability and to serve Him all this day, for this is the whole of man." This means that every person of Israel, immediately when he rises from his sleep, is ready to serve Hashem with all his ability and to serve Him all the day.

[And this is the content of this saying of "I offer thanks": "to contemplate immediately before whom he is lying, before the King of kings of kings, the Holy One, blessed be He, as it is written : 'Do I not fill the heavens and the earth?'" — "so he should think in all his business and matters", "and this is a great principle in the Torah and in the levels of the *tzaddikim* who walk before G-d, as it is written : 'I have set Hashem before me always'", and this is that likewise it should be the entire day.]

Consequently, there is no need here for the matter of *taharah* in the sanctification of hands and feet (since it is akin to something that cannot receive *tumah* (for example — everything in the sea)), but only the category of *kedushah*. This means that this invitation and preparation of the person to serve Him and to minister to Him must be brought into action, "to sanctify ourselves with His holiness" in practice — and therefore there is no need and no place for the washing of the face.

(From the *sichos* of 18 Elul 5745, *Shabbos Parshas Devarim* 5746)

## Tissa

### 2

a. The Gemara states: "An additional soul (*neshamah yeseirah*) is given to a person on Erev Shabbos; at the conclusion of Shabbos, it is taken from him, as it is written [in our Parsha: 'And on the seventh day'] He rested and was refreshed (*vayinafash*); once He rested, woe (*vi*), the soul (*nefesh*) is lost" (interpreting *vayinafash* via *notrikon* as *vi-nefesh*). The Rishonim (and likewise in the Zohar) write that this is the reason for the Sages’ enactment to smell spices at the conclusion of every Shabbos — to restore the soul, which grieves over the loss of the *neshamah yeseirah* at the conclusion of Shabbos. [Admur HaZaken rules similarly in his Shulchan Aruch: "The Sages instituted smelling spices at the conclusion of every Shabbos to restore the soul, which is pained by the departure of Shabbos due to the *neshamah yeseirah* that has departed; therefore, they settle and gladden it with a good scent."]

However, at the conclusion of a Shabbos that coincides with a Yom Tov, one does not recite a blessing over spices. The Rishonim differ regarding the reason for this:

The Rashbam writes, "For even on Yom Tov we have a *neshamah yeseirah* like on Shabbos."

The Tosafot challenge this: "If so, why did they not institute spices at the conclusion of Yom Tov?" (and they continue) "Therefore, it appears that on Yom Tov there is no *neshamah yeseirah*." The reason one does not recite a blessing over spices when Yom Tov falls on Motzaei Shabbos is "because the joy of Yom Tov and eating and drinking are as effective as spices" (and in another place, the Tosafot write, "Since one has important and good foods, his mind is settled of its own accord without the scent of spices").

[Admur HaZaken rules similarly in his Shulchan Aruch, that when Yom Tov falls on Motzaei Shabbos, "one does not need spices, for the delight of Yom Tov and its joy restore the soul."]

It requires understanding what they are arguing about, as it is forced to say they disagree on the reality of whether a *neshamah yeseirah* exists on Yom Tov. Especially since the Zohar explicitly states that there is a *neshamah yeseirah* on Yom Tov as well.

b. Regarding the resolution of the Rashbam’s words—which seemingly are puzzling, as per the challenge of the Tosafot, that if so one should have been required to recite a blessing over spices at the conclusion of Yom Tov as well—it can be said that his intent is along the lines of what the Ran writes regarding why one does not recite a blessing over spices from Shabbos to Yom Tov. He writes: "From Shabbos to a weekday, when one exits complete rest for great labor, the soul requires the settling provided by spices; but from Shabbos to Yom Tov, there is also some rest on Yom Tov, and from Yom Tov to a weekday one is also not exiting complete rest, for one has labored in the preparation of food (*ochel nefesh*), and therefore one does not recite a blessing over spices in either case."

Similarly, one can say regarding the Rashbam: when he says that there is also a *neshamah yeseirah* on Yom Tov, he does not mean it is on the same level as the *neshamah yeseirah* of Shabbos, but rather on a lower level [as is explicit in the Zohar that there is a *neshamah yeseirah* not only on Yom Tov but even on Rosh Chodesh (where there is not even a Havdalah blessing), so it must be that there are several levels of *neshamah yeseirah*]. Therefore, there is no blessing over spices at the conclusion of Yom Tov either, because such grief of the soul that requires a good scent comes only from the departure of the *neshamah yeseirah* that comes on Shabbos (which is a high level, etc.), whereas from the departure of the *neshamah yeseirah* of Yom Tov, the soul is not pained to the extent that it requires spices.

Nevertheless, on a Yom Tov that falls on Motzaei Shabbos, one does not recite a blessing over spices (even though the *neshamah yeseirah* of Shabbos has departed) — because since there is the "neshamah yeseirah" of Yom Tov, the soul is not so pained as to require spices for the "settling of the soul" (along the lines of the aforementioned words of the Ran).

c. Based on this, one can also explain the dispute between the Rashbam and the Tosafot:

According to the Rashbam, the difference between the *neshamah yeseirah* of Shabbos and the *neshamah yeseirah* of Yom Tov is not great enough to require the "settling of the soul with spices." Conversely, according to the Tosafot, even the departure from Shabbos to Yom Tov causes grief of the soul to such a degree that one requires the settling and restoration of the soul, except that the joy and delight of Yom Tov take the place of the action of the spices.

This can be understood by first explaining the concept of *neshamah yeseirah* according to the *nigleh* (the revealed Torah). In the Zohar, it is explicit that the matter of *neshamah yeseirah* is the drawing down of an "additional soul" from above on the day of Shabbos (as the simple meaning of "additional soul"). However, in the *nigleh* of Torah, the concept of *neshamah yeseirah* is explained in a different way:

Rashi in his commentary to the Shas (in Maseches Beitzah) explains *neshamah yeseirah* as: "A breadth of heart for rest and joy, and to be open to relief, and he will eat and drink, and his soul will not be weary of him." And in another place (in Rashi to Taanis), he explains *neshamah yeseirah* as "that they expand his mind for eating and drinking". And in the Tashbatz it is stated: "And the reason for spices at the conclusion of Shabbos is because of the *neshamah yeseirah* that he had from the rest of Shabbos, and he is now pained when that rest departs from him, and he smells spices to settle his soul." And in the responsa of the Rashba: "Some explain *neshamah yeseirah* as the rest and delight that the soul finds on Shabbos, as if it were an additional soul, and when he passes from it and enters the days of toil and affliction, it is as if an additional soul has been lost from him and he is weakened."

We learn from this that the interpretation of *neshamah yeseirah* is not in the simple sense—an additional soul—but a state of soul that comes as a result of the rest of Shabbos and the delight of Shabbos.

Based on this, it can be said that the dispute between the Rashbam and the Tosafot is regarding the definition of rest and the prohibition of labor on Yom Tov: whether it is in the same category of rest and prohibition of labor as Shabbos, and the difference is only in quantity—that certain labors (labor of *ochel nefesh*) are permitted; or whether it is a change in the quality of the rest, and the prohibition of labor on Yom Tov is an object (*cheftza*) of a different rest than the rest of Shabbos:

According to the Rashbam (and likewise the Ran), the rest of Yom Tov is of the same type and category as the rest of Shabbos, only it is less than it—a change in quantity. Therefore, when Yom Tov comes immediately after Shabbos, it is possible for the rest of Yom Tov to be considered a kind of continuation of the rest of Shabbos, and the soul is not pained by the departure from Shabbos to Yom Tov.

Conversely, according to the Tosafot, the rest of Shabbos is an object of a different rest; the difference between the rest of Shabbos and the rest of Yom Tov is not only a change in quantity but also a change in quality.

[It can be said that something similar can be learned from the precision of the Rambam’s language at the beginning of *Hilchos Shabbos* and at the beginning of *Hilchos Shevisas Yom Tov*: In *Hilchos Shabbos*, he opens with the obligation: "Resting on the seventh day from labor is a positive commandment, as it is written: 'And on the seventh day you shall rest'." Whereas in *Hilchos Shevisas Yom Tov*, he opens with the prohibition: "These six days that the Torah prohibited from doing labor, etc., are what are called Yomim Tovim, and the rest of all of them is equal, in that they are prohibited from all labor of work, etc." — because on Shabbos the essence is the positive matter, the rest of Shabbos, and on Yom Tov the essence is the prohibition and the negation.]

And for this reason, upon exiting from Shabbos to Yom Tov, by law they would have required spices, except that the joy of Yom Tov and eating and drinking are as effective as spices.

d. However, the matter is still not settled according to the golden language of our Rebbe, Admur HaZaken, who wrote in his Shulchan Aruch regarding the reason one does not recite a blessing over spices at the conclusion of Yom Tov — because "on Yom Tov a person does not have a *neshamah yeseirah* at all." That is, he adds the word "at all" to the language of the Tosafot (mentioned above).

Seemingly, according to the above that everyone agrees there is some level of "neshamah yeseirah" even on Yom Tov — why does Admur HaZaken add and emphasize that "on Yom Tov there is... not at all"?

The explanation for this is that according to Admur HaZaken, it can be said that the dispute between the Rashbam and the Tosafot is regarding the very definition of *neshamah yeseirah* (and therefore, according to the opinion of the Tosafot, even though they do not disagree with the Rashbam on the reality, nevertheless, there is no *neshamah yeseirah* on Yom Tov at all).

Explanation of the matter:
The reason the state of the rest of Shabbos is called by the name "neshamah yeseirah" — it can be said, is because the rest and delight of Shabbos are not a side or temporary matter, but rather they effect a change in the very person (*gavra*), to the point that it is as if an additional soul has entered him.

It can be said that this is also understood from the source of the teaching for the matter of *neshamah yeseirah*, which is learned (as mentioned above) from what is written in our Parsha: "On the seventh day He rested and was refreshed (*vayinafash*)."

The innovation of the Parsha of Shabbos in our Parsha regarding the Parsha of Shabbos in the Ten Commandments is that in the Ten Commandments, the definition of the essence of the day of Shabbos is its being a day set apart from other days: "For in six days Hashem made the heavens and the earth, etc., and He rested on the seventh day; therefore Hashem blessed the day of Shabbos and sanctified it". Whereas in our Parsha, the definition of the essence of the day of Shabbos is — "It is a sign between Me and you for your generations to know that I am Hashem who sanctifies you", "Between Me and the Children of Israel it is a sign forever".

That is to say: In the Ten Commandments, it speaks about the object (*cheftza*) of the day of Shabbos in itself; whereas in our Parsha, it explains about the effect of Shabbos upon the Jewish people, that on the day of Shabbos a change is made in the person, in the Children of Israel, from the other days of the week.

And especially according to what Rashi explains on the verse "For it is a sign between Me and you" — "It is a great sign between us that I have chosen you by bequeathing to you the day of My rest for rest," which implies that the rest of the person on the day of Shabbos is not just rest from the toil of labor, but that (this is the day of Hashem’s rest, and consequently) the rest of the person on this day effects in him a change and an elevation in his state.

And this is also emphasized in the language of Rashi "Shabbos Shabbaton" — "A rest of tranquility and not a temporary rest." The rest of the day of Shabbos is in that the person changes completely in the state of his soul to a state of "tranquility," and not just that he rests from the toil of labor.

For example: When a person rests from labor on another day (even resting from all labor), it will not be a rest that effects in him a change of person, a rest of tranquility, but only a "temporary rest" (like when a Noahide rests completely on the day of Shabbos, he does not come through this to complete rest), and only on the day of Shabbos was given to the Children of Israel the day of Hashem’s rest, and through His rest he is sanctified and a change is made in the person from the six days of the week.

And this is what is learned from the verse "On the seventh day He rested and was refreshed" which is in this Parsha specifically, that there is a *neshamah yeseirah* — for the meaning of *neshamah yeseirah* is the change of the person that comes through this that "He rested and was refreshed," the rest of Hashem that was given to the person and in the person.

e. Based on this, the dispute between the Rashbam and the Tosafot regarding Yom Tov is understood:
According to the Rashbam, the rest of Shabbos and the rest of Yom Tov are one category, and the change is only in quantity (as mentioned in section g), and therefore the rest of Yom Tov is also in the category of "neshamah yeseirah";
[And perhaps it can be said that according to the Rashbam, the matter of "neshamah yeseirah" is connected primarily (not with the rest of Shabbos, but) with the delight and joy of Shabbos, and as the simple language of Rashi mentioned above that "neshamah yeseirah" is "a breadth of heart for rest and joy, and to be open to relief, and he will eat and drink, etc."; and this matter applies also on Yom Tov, which has the mitzvah of joy and eating and drinking, which is a positive commandment on the festivals, and "they were accustomed to increase types of foods on Yom Tov more than on Shabbos" (even though it is understood that it is not similar to the *neshamah yeseirah* of Shabbos, since on Shabbos there is complete rest, which is necessary for the perfection of the delight and joy)];
Conversely, according to the Tosafot, that there is rest on Yom Tov is not the same quality of rest as the day of Shabbos (as mentioned in section c), and this rest does not effect a change of person to be called by the name "soul (additional)" of the person, because a *neshamah yeseirah* exists only in that category of complete rest, "rest of tranquility" from all labor, which is on Shabbos.

[And as is explicit in the Rashba there: "Some explain *neshamah yeseirah* as the rest and delight that the soul finds on Shabbos, as if it were an additional soul, and when he passes from it and enters the days of toil and affliction, it is as if an additional soul has been lost from him and he is weakened. And according to this interpretation, on Shabbos it is that a soul is added to him because it is a day of rest, and even from the labor of *ochel nefesh*, which is not the case on Yom Tov"].

It can be said that this is also the intent of Admur HaZaken in his writing that on Yom Tov "a person does not have a *neshamah yeseirah* at all" — even though on Yom Tov there is also rest, and there is also an obligation of delight and joy — because this innovation of Shabbos, the change of person that comes through "I am Hashem who sanctifies you," was not explained (as much) on Yom Tov.

And the reason he is precise to add this emphasis — is because through this his words are explained regarding the reason one does not recite a blessing over spices at the conclusion of Shabbos for Yom Tov — "for... the delight of Yom Tov and its joy restore the soul", from which it is understood that essentially they should have required spices, except that the delight of Yom Tov and its joy are in place of spices (which restore the soul), and that is because since there is no *neshamah yeseirah* on Yom Tov at all, therefore the soul is pained (also) from the departure of Shabbos to Yom Tov, except that they restore it, meaning they do not allow it to depart through the delight of Yom Tov and its joy before that, along the lines of the action of spices after it has departed at the conclusion of Shabbos.

[Conversely, if there were some matter of "neshamah yeseirah" on Yom Tov, even if it were not in the same manner as on Shabbos, then there would be room to say that they do not need spices because the soul is not pained (along the lines of the words of the Ran mentioned above)].

f. And now, even though several Rishonim brought the matter of *neshamah yeseirah* as the reason for the blessing over spices at the conclusion of Shabbos — the Rambam did not mention it. His words are (in *Hilchos Shabbos*): "And why do we recite a blessing over spices at the conclusion of Shabbos? Because the soul is pained by the departure of Shabbos; we gladden it and settle it with a good scent."

And although the Hagahos Maimoniyos points to the Gemara's statement "neshamah yeseirah, etc.," and it is implied that his intent is to say and explain that what the Rambam wrote "the soul is pained by the departure of Shabbos" is what is said in the Gemara that at the conclusion of Shabbos they take from the person the *neshamah yeseirah* that was given to him — nevertheless, from the simple language of the Rambam "because the soul is pained by the departure of Shabbos" and he did not mention about the *neshamah yeseirah* that is in the Gemara, seemingly it is implied that his intent is not to the *neshamah yeseirah*, but that the soul (in general) is pained by the departure of Shabbos (in general).

[And this is an additional fundamental change in content — whether the grief of the soul is from the departure of Shabbos itself, or (only) from what is taken from him (*neshamah yeseirah*) by the departure of Shabbos].

It should also be noted that in the vast majority of cases, it is not the way of the Rambam to write in the Yad the reasons for the mitzvos and customs, and when he writes a reason, it is because it touches upon the body of the law, that there is a practical difference (*nafka mina*) in this, and the like. And it requires understanding in this case — why did the Rambam write the reason for the blessing over spices at the conclusion of Shabbos (and especially that he did not write the reason that we recite a blessing over the candle at the conclusion of Shabbos in a separate chapter, but rather — in a place where it touches upon the law).

And one cannot say that his intent in this is to explain what he wrote in the preceding law that at the conclusion of Yom Tov (and Yom Kippur) one does not need to recite a blessing over spices, and he continues in this law "And why do we recite a blessing over spices at the conclusion of Shabbos (specifically)? Because the soul is pained by the departure of Shabbos, etc." — because according to this, the essence is missing from the book: why the soul is pained by the departure of Shabbos and not by the departure of Yom Tov, and the like:

According to the opinion that the grief of the soul is because of the *neshamah yeseirah* that departed — the reason for the difference between Shabbos and Yom Tov is understood, because on Yom Tov there is no *neshamah yeseirah* as explained at length above (and the matter of "neshamah yeseirah" and its departure at the conclusion of Shabbos is learned from what is written "And on the seventh day He rested and was refreshed" (as above), and in the verse it speaks of Shabbos and not of Yom Tov and Yom Kippur); but in the language of the Rambam "because the soul is pained by the departure of Shabbos," there is seemingly no reason and explanation as to how Yom Tov and Yom Kippur have changed that we do not recite a blessing over spices on them, and why do we not say regarding them that the soul is pained by the departure of Yom Tov and the like.

[In the Maggid Mishneh it is explained "that the soul is pained only at the departure of Shabbos, which was a rest from all labor" (and like the words of the Ran mentioned above). But this distinction is not hinted at in the language of the Rambam "because the soul is pained by the departure of Shabbos". And especially that above in this chapter, the Rambam wrote explicitly "Just as we sanctify on the nights of Shabbos and make Havdalah at the conclusion of Shabbos, so we sanctify on the nights of Yomim Tovim and make Havdalah, etc., for all of them are Shabbosos of Hashem."]

And it must be that the Rambam did not descend here to explain the distinction between Shabbos and Yom Tov, but to explain the very connection of the blessing over spices to the conclusion of Shabbos.

g. It can be said that his intent in this is to define the connection of the blessing over spices to Havdalah, that it is not a side matter, but that it is of the very content of the matter of Havdalah.

That is to say: That we recite a blessing over spices at the conclusion of Shabbos at the time of Havdalah is not only to gladden and settle the soul from its grief, but it is connected to the very obligation of Havdalah.

Explanation of the matter:
At the beginning of the chapter, the Rambam wrote that the mitzvah of Kiddush of the day of Shabbos is: "Remember it, a remembrance of praise and sanctification, and one must remember it at its entry and at its exit, etc., and at its exit with Havdalah," which means that Havdalah also, its matter is the fulfillment of the mitzvah "Remember the day of Shabbos to sanctify it", whose content is — "remembrance of praise and sanctification"; and the goal of this remembrance at the exit of Shabbos is (as explained in another place) to draw down the remembrance of Shabbos also into the days of the week that follow it, as is understood also in the simple sense that through remembering the holiness of Shabbos at the time that one exits from Shabbos to the days of the week, one remembers the day of Shabbos during the days of the week.

And this is also the connection of the blessing over spices to Havdalah, because in order to remember it with a "remembrance of praise and sanctification" at the conclusion of Shabbos ("at its exit"), one requires a matter of joy, etc., and it is understood that there is no place for "remembrance of praise and sanctification" in a state of a "pained soul". And seemingly this is the intent in the Sefer HaPardes of Rashi (after he explains that through the spices "he dissipates his pain and will not be sad in his heart when he remembers the six days of work") — "And all this they enacted so that the remembrance of Shabbos would be in his heart and he would be joyful even at its exit."

Based on this, the reason is understood that the Rambam did not bring the matter of the departure of "neshamah yeseirah," but wrote "because the soul is pained by the departure of Shabbos" — because this emphasizes (more) the connection of the blessing over spices to the obligation of Havdalah, which is connected with the departure of Shabbos (in general) — and not only with the departure of the *neshamah yeseirah* from the person. And that is that the blessing over spices is not only to fix a matter that is at the conclusion of Shabbos (the grief of the soul) but its matter is (also) the continuation of the action and remembrance of Shabbos into the time after Shabbos, and therefore it is a part and completion of the remembrance of Shabbos (in Havdalah).

h. Based on this, one can explain several more differences between the language of the Rambam and the language of Admur HaZaken in his Shulchan Aruch:

In the Rambam it is written: "(because the soul is pained, etc.) we gladden it and settle it with a good scent"; whereas Admur HaZaken wrote: "to restore the soul that is pained, etc., therefore we settle and gladden it with a good scent," meaning that the Rambam preceded "gladden" to "settle," and Admur HaZaken precedes "settle" to "gladden":

According to the reason that the blessing over spices is because of the departure of the *neshamah yeseirah*, the essence of the action of the spices is the restoration of the soul — because the taking of the *neshamah yeseirah* causes a weakening of the vitality, etc., and therefore they need to restore the soul; except that afterwards Admur HaZaken adds also "we gladden it" — to explain the essence of the restoration of the soul through the spices, for after all there is no restoration of the soul here in reality (for the action of the spices is not to restore the soul as it was on Shabbos before the *neshamah yeseirah* was taken) but only "we gladden" the soul (in general);

Conversely, according to the Rambam, the essence of the matter of the blessing over spices is to gladden the soul, the opposite of the grief of the soul (its pain, etc.) over the "departure of Shabbos," so that the departure of Shabbos will be from within joy, and therefore he begins with the essence — "we gladden it, etc."

i. According to this explanation in the opinion of the Rambam — that the blessing over spices is connected to the very matter of Havdalah — one can explain in a simple way the reason for the law that on the night of Yom Tov that falls at the conclusion of Shabbos, one does not recite a blessing over spices:

Regarding the mitzvah of Kiddush and Havdalah of Shabbos, the Rambam wrote (as mentioned above) "Just as we sanctify on the nights of Shabbos and make Havdalah at the conclusion of Shabbos, so we sanctify on the nights of Yomim Tovim and make Havdalah at their conclusion, and at the conclusion of Yom Kippur, for all of them are Shabbosos of Hashem."

And therefore, when Yom Tov falls at the conclusion of Shabbos, it is drawn from the rest and holiness of Shabbos into the days of Yom Tov that come immediately after it [and especially that according to the law we hold (and likewise the Rambam ruled) that the order of the blessings on Yom Tov that falls at the conclusion of Shabbos is "he sanctifies the Kiddush of Yom Tov, etc., and afterwards he makes Havdalah"] in a way that the rest and joy of Shabbos are continued into Yom Tov, and consequently there is no place for spices, for there is no grief of the soul here, but the joy of the soul.

(From the Sichos of Shabbos Parshas Behar-Bechukosai and Shabbos Parshas Bamidbar 5745; 13 Nissan 5746)


## ויקהל

## Sicha 1

### 1

At the beginning of the "command regarding the work of the Mishkan" in our Parsha, Rashi writes, "I have already explained the donation for the Mishkan and its work in the place where they were commanded" (in Parshas Terumah and Tetzaveh, and at the beginning of Parshas Tisa).

Nevertheless, we find several details regarding the work of the Mishkan that Rashi does see fit to explain in our Parsha (such as the verse, "the Mishkan, its tent, and its covering," where Rashi explains what the "Mishkan," "its tent," and "its covering" are, and more). The reason for this is understood: it is impossible to know the meaning of these items from "the place where they were commanded," and therefore Rashi found it necessary to explain them here, as is explained by the commentators regarding most of Rashi’s explanations here (the reason why Rashi did not rely on his explanation "in the place where they were commanded").

Based on this, there is a difficulty regarding Rashi’s commentary on the verse, "the pegs of the Mishkan, the pegs of the courtyard, and their cords," where he explains: "pegs, to drive into the ground and tie the ends of the curtains so they will not move in the wind," and "their cords, ropes for tying." For this matter was already explained by Rashi "in the place where they were commanded," at the end of Parshas Terumah, on the verse, "for all the vessels of the Mishkan... and all its pegs, and all the pegs of the courtyard of copper," where he wrote there: "pegs, like copper rods made for the curtains of the tent and the hangings of the courtyard, tied with cords all around their lower edges so that the wind would not lift them." Why, then, was Rashi compelled to return and explain the meaning of the "pegs of the Mishkan" here once again?

[And also regarding "their cords," which were not explicitly defined in the verse "in the place where they were commanded"—seemingly, Rashi should not have needed to explain them here, since he had already explained there that the pegs were "tied with cords all around their lower edges."]

### 2

One must also note the precision in Rashi’s language that the "pegs" were "to drive into the ground and tie the ends of the curtains, etc.":

Above, at the end of Parshas Terumah, Rashi wrote regarding the pegs of the curtains: "And I do not know whether they were driven into the ground or tied and hung, with their weight weighing down the lower edges of the curtains so they would not move in the wind (but he later concludes), and I say that their name proves that they were driven into the ground, for they are called 'pegs' (yeteidoseha), and this verse supports me: 'Do not lengthen your cords, and strengthen your pegs' (Yeshayahu 54:2)."

Based on this, it must be said (and so it is stated by the commentators) that what Rashi writes here, "to drive into," refers to his conclusion above that they were "driven into the ground."

But this is difficult according to the version of Rashi above, where the conclusion "and I say" is not from Rashi himself but from his student; why would Rashi be silent there, yet here conclude that they were driven into the ground?

Furthermore, even according to the common version where the conclusion there is from Rashi’s own words, it requires explanation. Based on what is explained elsewhere, the very fact that Rashi begins there with language of doubt, "and I do not know whether they were driven in, etc." (and did not write immediately according to the conclusion that they were driven into the ground), proves that the proofs Rashi brought that they were driven into the ground (from the fact that "their name proves it" and the "verse... that supports me") are not absolute proofs. We must understand what compelled Rashi here in our Parsha to determine that they were "to drive into," and not use language that avoids a definitive stance on the aforementioned doubt, such as (similar to the beginning of Rashi’s language in Parshas Terumah there): "made for the curtains of the tent, tied around their lower edges so they would not move in the wind," or something similar.

### 3

The explanation for this can be stated simply:

On the verse, "and the oil for the light," Rashi explains: "it too requires 'wise-hearted' people, for it is different from other oils, etc." From this, it is understood that all the items listed here required "wise-hearted" people specifically to make them.

[With this, the change in the language of the text is resolved: above in Parshas Terumah it is said, "for all the vessels of the Mishkan, for all its service, and all its pegs, etc.," and similarly later in Parshas Pekudei, when the Children of Israel brought the Mishkan and all its vessels to Moshe, it is said at the conclusion of the matter, "and all the vessels of the service of the Mishkan," whereas here in our Parsha, "all the vessels of the Mishkan" are not mentioned.

And according to the above, it is understood simply: the "vessels of the Mishkan" at the end of Parshas Terumah are (as Rashi explains there) the vessels "that were needed for its assembly and disassembly, such as hammers to drive in the pegs, etc.," and for these, "wise-hearted" people are not needed, because they are ordinary tools found even among those who are not "wise-hearted."]

Based on this, it is puzzling: why were "wise-hearted" people needed for the making of the "pegs" and "cords"?

To resolve this, Rashi explains that the pegs were an inseparable part of the curtains themselves, because without them, they would move in the wind. It is understood that since the "wise-hearted" people were needed to make the curtains, they did not suffice with making only the main parts of the curtains, but finished them in a way that they would be a complete entity in all their details. This means they made not only the body of the curtains, but also all their hooks, and even the pegs and cords that were required to tie them so they would not move in the wind.

Based on this, we can explain the reason Rashi is precise here that the pegs were "to drive into," meaning they were driven into the ground (and not just "tied and hung, with their weight weighing down the lower edges of the curtains"). For in this way, it is emphasized more that they are part of the permanence (the structure) of the curtains (since through them the curtains are connected to the ground), whereas according to the interpretation that they were "tied and hung, and their weight weighs them down," the pegs are an additional detail to the curtains (which are hung upon them [by their weight weighing them down]).

### 4

From here there is a lesson for the "wise-hearted" of every generation in the work of education, upon whom rests the obligation to raise many students, as per the ruling of the Rambam that "it is a mitzvah for every sage of Israel to teach all students, even if they are not his children." The fulfillment of the sage’s obligation does not end with him teaching the student those things that he is able (with his great wisdom) to influence him with.

Rather, one must be a true "wise-hearted" person, ensuring that the fruits he produces are complete in every detail, to the point that even matters that appear secondary and external—like pegs and cords, for which seemingly one does not need a "wise-hearted" person—he strives that they be present in the student in the ultimate state of perfection.

Specifically when he "finishes" the preparation of the student down to the most external details, then there is the certainty and confidence that it is a lasting thing, that they "will not move in the wind." Even when there are winds blowing in the world, the student stands strong at his post in accordance with the instructions of his teacher. This is the sign that the teacher’s instruction has penetrated the student’s interior, to the point that no detail remains that is not permeated by it. This is similar to the saying of our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe on the verse, "Because (eikev) Avraham heard My voice," that Avraham Avinu "heard" the command of Hashem with every aspect of his stature—head, body, and foot—until even his "heels" (eikev) "heard" the command of Hashem.

This is also hinted at in the words of our Sages that a mitzvah is called "only by the name of the one who finishes it." Even when the main part of the matter has already been done, and only a small detail is missing in its completion, since the entire mitzvah has not been finished, it has not been fulfilled. And the one who finishes the mitzvah, even though he only completed a small detail, the entire mitzvah is called by his name specifically. This is as mentioned above: until the whole thing exists, it is not that only one detail is missing, but rather that because of this, something is missing in the whole, since there is no guarantee that the thing will stand firm against all the blowing winds. It must be that the thing permeates his entire stature.

From this there is also great encouragement for our generation, the generation of the "heels" of Mashiach (ikvesa d'meshicha). Even though we are at the level of "heels" relative to the previous generations, and the generation is worthy in its entirety, it is specifically our work in finishing the clarifications—in the "heels"—like the pegs at the edges of the curtains through which they are tied and fixed to the ground—that finishes the work needed to bring the true and complete Redemption through Mashiach Tzidkeinu (even though this is but a detail, and especially relative to the magnitude of the work of the generations that preceded us).

And a mitzvah is called only by the name of the one who finishes it—the generation of the "heels of the heels" of Mashiach, which finishes "our deeds and our service (of) the entire duration of the exile," and brings into actuality the "ultimate perfection... of the days of Mashiach and the resurrection of the dead," speedily in our days, truly.

## Sicha 2

### 203

1. "And every man with whom was found blue, purple, and scarlet wool, and fine linen, and goats’ hair, and rams’ skins dyed red, and tachash skins, brought them". Rashi explains: "And every man with whom was found" — "blue, or purple, or scarlet wool, or rams’ skins, or tachash skins—all of them brought [what they had]."

At first glance, it appears that Rashi’s intent is to explain that the *vav* (in "and purple, and scarlet wool, etc.") is not a *vav* of addition (which would imply that only one who possessed "blue, and purple, and scarlet wool, etc."—all the items listed in the verse—brought them all, whereas one who did not possess all of them brought nothing). Rather, he is saying "or, or"—that every man with whom was found blue, or purple, or scarlet wool, etc., brought whichever of these he possessed.

The necessity for this interpretation arises from the phrasing of the verse itself: "And every man with whom was found." If it were the case that only one who possessed all the items brought them, the verse should have said "with whom were found" (*asher nimtzu*), in the plural, indicating that all the items were found with him. Since it says "with whom was found" (*asher nimtza*), in the singular, it follows that the verse refers to each item individually—blue, or purple, or scarlet wool, etc.

However, we must understand:
a) What was the initial assumption (*kasd*) that led to the interpretation that only one who possessed all the items brought them, and that one who possessed only some of them did not? This is not at all logical, especially according to the plain meaning of the text—and consequently, the plain meaning of the matter—that one who wishes to donate only one of the items should be unable to do so! Why did Rashi need to inform us of something so obvious?
[And one cannot say that for the sake of clarity, "Rashi wished... to explain what did not need explanation"—for if so, "why did he not explain it this way regarding the verse of the brooches and nose-rings, etc., which are the first mentioned in the text?" When it says there, "every generous-hearted person brought brooches, and nose-rings, and rings, and body-ornaments, all gold jewelry," the intent is surely "or, or"—a brooch, or a nose-ring, or a ring, etc.].
b) Since Rashi goes to the length of listing (almost) all the types, why did he skip and omit two types (words) from the verse—"fine linen and goats’ hair"?

2. Some commentators explain that Rashi only comes to resolve the continuity of the verse’s language, which appears contradictory: it begins with "every man with whom was found" (singular) and ends with "brought" (plural). Therefore, Rashi explains that the phrase "every man with whom was found" (singular) is used because it refers to each item individually—every man with whom was found blue, or every man with whom was found purple, or every man with whom was found scarlet wool, etc.—and the end of the verse, "brought" (plural), means "all of them brought," meaning each and every one of those who possessed one of these types; in this way, the plural form is appropriate.

But this interpretation is difficult for several reasons:
a) The fact that it opens in the singular, "every man," and ends in the plural, "brought," is standard and not at all difficult according to the plain meaning of Scripture, as is proven by the preceding verse, "every generous-hearted person brought"—it does not write "every generous-hearted people brought".
b) According to this, the core of Rashi’s explanation is missing from the text—he should have said, "or a man with whom was found purple", and so on.
c) Even if the *vav* is a *vav* of addition, could Rashi not have resolved the aforementioned question by saying: "Every man with whom was found blue, and every man with whom was found purple, etc.—all of them brought"?
d) It is still not resolved why Rashi omitted "fine linen and goats’ hair".

3. The *Shefetei Chachamim* here explains the initial assumption that only one who has all the items should bring, and one who does not have all of them should not bring, and also why he omitted fine linen and goats’ hair: "Because we find that these three types—blue, purple, and scarlet wool—are always used together in all the work of the Mishkan and in the garments of the Kohen Gadol. Likewise, rams’ skins dyed red and tachash skins—from both of them together they made a covering for the tent, as Rashi explains above in Parshas Terumah. Therefore, one might think that only one who possesses all these items should bring them, and one who has only one type should not bring it, since all three are needed for the work, and none of the work can be done without the others. But regarding fine linen, we find it used in the garments of ordinary Kohanim, where they used fine linen alone, and likewise in the four garments of the Kohen Gadol that he wore for the inner service on Yom Kippur and other services. And regarding goats’ hair, they did not make anything from it except the goats’ hair curtains; it is obvious that they would bring it alone. Therefore, Rashi did not mention fine linen and goats’ hair, because it is a self-evident matter that they would bring them alone."

However, his explanation is difficult: What is the logic, according to the plain meaning, that because these items came together in the work of the Mishkan and the priestly garments, their donation must also be together?

Furthermore: According to the plain meaning, we do not find that every one of the Bnei Yisrael knew all the details of the construction of the Mishkan and the priestly garments and from which materials they were made. Simply put, only the "wise-hearted" who performed the work of the Mishkan knew of this (before the construction). Why would it enter the minds of the donors that only one who possesses all three types (blue, purple, and scarlet wool) should bring them, as opposed to one who has only a part of them?

And most importantly: Even according to his explanation, there is no initial assumption that one must bring all the types in the verse together, but rather "these three types, i.e., blue, purple, and scarlet wool," or "rams’ skins dyed red and tachash skins"—and if so, one is forced to divide the verse into two and interpret the *vav* of "and rams’ skins, etc." as "or rams’ skins, etc." (unlike the other *vavs* in the verse). What room is there for such an initial assumption, especially when there is no hint of it in the language of the verse?

4. It can be said that the explanation for this is to be found by noting another detail in this verse: it says "And every man with whom was found, etc.," and not as in the preceding verse, "every generous-hearted person".

Seemingly, one could say: The donation for the Mishkan came after Hashem was appeased with Yisrael regarding the sin of the Golden Calf (to the point that the Mishkan is called the "Mishkan of the Testimony"—"a testimony to Yisrael that the Holy One, blessed be He, had forgiven them for the sin of the Calf, for He caused His Shechinah to dwell among them").

Therefore: Regarding those items from which the Calf was made (namely, gold vessels in general, and specifically the jewelry made from gold), the verse emphasizes "every generous-hearted person brought brooches, and nose-rings, and rings, and body-ornaments, all gold jewelry," for the giving of these items for the work of the Mishkan was an act of generosity of heart, with a special intention and special feeling of the heart, to atone for the giving of these items for the making of the Calf. But regarding the other items for the Mishkan’s donation, which are not related to the making of the Calf, there is no need for emphasis that they came from a generous heart, and therefore it says simply, "every man with whom was found."

But this is not a sufficient explanation, because even though there were items in the Mishkan’s donation for which no similar type was given for the Calf, nevertheless, since the entire donation of the Mishkan came after Hashem was appeased regarding the sin of the Calf, it stands to reason that in the giving of the other items for the Mishkan’s donation, there also had to be the good will and generosity of heart to give them for the Mishkan, the purpose of which is "and I will dwell among them", the opposite of their donation to the Calf. As is indeed stated in Hashem’s command to Moshe at the beginning of Parshas Terumah (regarding all thirteen items): "from every man whose heart moves him to give." If so, why does the verse emphasize "every generous-hearted person" only regarding "brooches, and nose-rings, etc., all gold jewelry"?

Therefore, it must be that in all the donations, there was the generosity of heart of the Bnei Yisrael, as is explicitly stated in the preceding verse: "And they came, every man whose heart stirred him, and everyone whose spirit moved him, they brought the offering of Hashem, etc."

Since this is so, there is room to say that the generosity of heart is indeed emphasized in the text regarding the giving of the other items as well: just as regarding gold it says, "every generous-hearted person brought brooches, and nose-rings, etc., all gold jewelry," because bringing jewelry, etc., and all gold vessels indicates the utmost generosity of heart, so too in the following verse, "and every man with whom was found blue, and purple, and scarlet wool, etc.," we would have interpreted (with the *vav* of addition) that the merit of the generosity was that everyone who possessed all these types brought them all.

That is to say: The initial assumption is not that one who had only one item could not bring it, but rather the initial assumption is that this verse comes to inform us of the greatness of the generosity of heart of Yisrael for the work of the Mishkan—that one who possessed all the types did not settle for one item, but brought of everything.

Therefore, Rashi needed to negate this interpretation, and explains that the intent of the verse is not to emphasize that one who possessed everything brought of everything, but rather that it means "or, or"—every man with whom was found blue, or purple, or scarlet wool, etc., all of them brought.
[And it can be said that the preference for this interpretation (that it means "or, or") is hinted at by Rashi in the precision of his language at the end of his comment—"all of them brought." That is, according to this interpretation, the verse speaks of the donation of all of Yisrael (for simply put—after the plunder of Egypt and the sea, everyone possessed at least one of these types), whereas if you say that the verse speaks only of one who possessed all the types (and brought of everything), it turns out that the verse speaks only of a certain number of donors.]

It can be said that the generosity of heart regarding these types was expressed in the special action and exertion of the Bnei Yisrael, in addition to the actual giving and bringing—for "blue" does not mean they brought wool alone and dye alone, but "wool dyed with the blood of the *chilazon* [snail]"; and likewise for purple and scarlet wool. And since they not only brought these items for the work of the Mishkan, but also exerted themselves to dye the wool in its appropriate color, etc.—this is proof of their generosity of heart and their "good will" in giving them for the construction of the Mishkan.

It can be said that the aforementioned matter (the expression of generosity of heart through action and exertion, etc.) is hinted at in Rashi here, by the fact that Rashi omitted fine linen and goats’ hair from the verse, because only in these types that Rashi lists—blue, purple, scarlet wool, rams’ skins, and tachash skins—is there a necessity for special work and action on the part of the person; whereas with fine linen (which is flax) and goats’ hair (i.e., goats’ hair), there is no necessity (from the perspective of the Mishkan donation itself) for a special action by the person other than the mere bringing of them to the Mishkan (for the harvesting of the flax and the shearing of the hair are merely necessary preparations to enable their being brought to the Mishkan). Therefore, it is understood that in bringing fine linen or goats’ hair, each one individually, the generosity of heart of the donor is not as evident.
[And one cannot interpret that the generosity of heart in fine linen and goats’ hair is only in bringing both together, for in that, the good will, etc., of the donor is evident—and that this is the reason Rashi omitted "fine linen and goats’ hair" from counting among those where it is certain that it means "or, or"—for this is a great novelty and change, and Rashi should have explained it.]

5. We may add to this from the perspective of Halacha:
In the donation of the Mishkan, there is a requirement that it be "for His sake," as Rashi explains: "And they shall take for Me an offering—for Me, for My sake." We must clarify what the law is regarding items that require special work and preparation for the Mishkan (like the blue, purple, and scarlet wool, etc., which require dyeing as mentioned above)—whether the act of preparation must also be "for His sake."

Now, Rashi explained (on the verse "And they shall take for Me an offering") "offering—a separation, they shall separate for Me from their property a donation," meaning that even before giving the donation to the treasurers, there must be a separation of the property (by the owners) for Hashem. Based on this, it stands to reason that the acts of preparation (required so that the items are fit for the Mishkan’s donation) must also be "for My sake".

It can be said that this is the reason for the initial assumption that when it says "and every man with whom was found blue, and purple, and scarlet wool, etc.," it means they brought all the types together (and not a part of them or only one type):
When one brought only one of these types (which require an act of preparation as mentioned), there is room to say that there is no certainty that he dyed the wool "for His sake," for it is possible that he had dyed wool lying around from before, and did not exert himself to dye new wool with the intention "for His sake" [and the act of bringing itself is not such clear proof of the "for His sake"—because since they had great wealth from the plunder of Egypt and the plunder of the sea, this small donation is not proof of a desire to exert oneself for the Mishkan’s donation];
