## בכל דרכיך דעהו

## In All Your Ways Know Him

 The concept of "In all your ways know Him" — that one must serve Hashem not only through the performance of mitzvos, but also through one's mundane affairs — is a fundamental principle in the service of Hashem. This is not merely a secondary aspect of avodah, but rather a core component: through the refinement of the physical world, one draws down the light of Elokus into the lowest levels of reality.

Seemingly, one might ask: if the purpose of a Jew is to be occupied with Torah and mitzvos, why must one involve himself in the mundane at all? Why not remain entirely detached from the material world? 

But in truth, the purpose of the descent of the neshamah into the body and the physical world is specifically to elevate the material. By engaging in "all your ways"—the necessary activities of daily life—with the intention of serving Hashem, one performs an act of iskafya and eventually is'hapcha. This transforms the physical act itself into a vessel for Elokus, revealing that even within the mundane, there is no existence apart from Hashem. 

And this is the deeper meaning of "know Him": it is not merely an intellectual awareness, but an experiential knowledge—a dveikus—that permeates every aspect of one's life. When a Chassid conducts his business or his personal affairs with this kavanah, he is not stepping away from his avodah; he is fulfilling it in the most complete sense, bringing the light of the Torah into the very fabric of the world.


## בכל מאדך

## בכל מאדך



The Gemara states: "‘With all your *me'odecha’ (might/wealth) — with all your wealth; and another opinion says: ‘with all your *me'odecha’ — with every measure that He measures out for you, whether it be a measure of good or a measure of [what appears to be] suffering."

On the face of it, the two interpretations of the word *me'odecha*—wealth, and the measure of one's circumstances—seem to be two distinct, unrelated matters. One refers to the *avodah* of utilizing one's material possessions for the sake of Hashem, while the other refers to the *avodah* of *bitul* and acceptance of Hashem’s will regarding the events that befall a person.

However, in truth, these two interpretations are intrinsically connected. The *avodah* of "with all your wealth" is the *preparation* and the *vessel* for the *avodah* of "with every measure that He measures out for you."

When a person realizes that his wealth is not his own, but rather a deposit entrusted to him by the Ribono shel Olam to be used for His purposes, he attains a state of *bitul*. He recognizes that he is merely a conduit. Once he has internalized this level of *bitul*—where he does not view his material possessions as "his own"—it becomes possible for him to reach the higher level of *avodah*: accepting "every measure" that Hashem decrees for him with the same equanimity. Because he has already nullified his sense of self-ownership regarding his wealth, he can now nullify his sense of self regarding his personal circumstances, recognizing that both the "good" and the "suffering" are simply different ways in which the Ribono shel Olam conducts His world.

Thus, the two interpretations are one: the *avodah* of *me'odecha* is the total surrender of one's entire existence—both that which is external to him (his wealth) and that which is internal to his experience (the measures of his life)—to the will of Hashem.

## גוף ונפש

## דון מינה ומינה, מינה ואוקי באתרה

## Don Minei U’minei, Minei V’oki B’asrah

 The principle of *don minei u’minei*—deriving a law from the subject itself—and *minei v’oki b’asrah*—deriving it from the subject and establishing it in its own place—is a fundamental method of Talmudic reasoning. It is not merely a technical tool for extracting a halachic ruling, but a reflection of the inner logic of the Torah itself. When the Torah presents a specific law within a particular context, that context is not incidental; it is the very source from which the law’s internal structure and application are derived. By examining the law *from* the subject itself, we uncover the essential connection between the *mitzvah* and the specific circumstances in which it is situated. This ensures that our understanding of the *halacha* is not an external imposition, but an organic outgrowth of the Torah’s own framework, firmly established in its proper place.

## הקזת דם

## Bloodletting



The Gemara states that "bloodletting is a sign of healing for some, and a sign of illness for others." This concept is also reflected in the spiritual realm, specifically regarding the *avodah* of *iskafya* (subjugation) and *is'hapcha* (transformation).

On the face of it, *iskafya*—the act of suppressing and breaking one’s base desires—is a form of "bloodletting." It involves removing the "excess blood," the vitality that fuels the *sitra achra*. For some, this is a "sign of healing," as it serves as the necessary *avodah* to purify the soul and bring it to a state of health and *bitul*. For others, however, it may be a "sign of illness," implying that their *avodah* should not be focused on the harshness of *iskafya*, but rather on the more refined *avodah* of *is'hapcha*, where the darkness itself is transformed into light.

Just as in the physical world, where the necessity of bloodletting depends on the specific condition of the patient, so too in the *avodah* of a Chossid: one must discern whether his current state requires the "healing" of *iskafya*—to cut away the *kelipah*—or whether he has reached the level where he can engage in the *avodah* of *is'hapcha*, elevating his *taanug* and *hispaalus* to *Kedushah*.

## ידיעה ובחירה

## Knowledge and Choice



The concept of *yedi'ah* (knowledge) and *bechirah* (choice) is well known: How can man have free choice if Hashem knows in advance what he will choose? For if Hashem knows that he will choose a certain path, then he must choose it, and if he does not choose it, Hashem’s knowledge would be incorrect—which is impossible. And if he must choose it, then he has no free choice.

This is a famous question, and the Rambam writes that the answer is beyond human comprehension: "We do not have the capacity to know how the Holy One, Blessed be He, knows all the creations and their deeds." Just as we cannot comprehend the essence of Hashem, we cannot comprehend His knowledge.

However, in Chassidus, it is explained that this question arises only when we view Hashem’s knowledge as being analogous to human knowledge. Human knowledge is separate from the object being known; the object exists independently, and the person then acquires knowledge of it. Therefore, the knowledge is dependent on the object. But Hashem’s knowledge is not separate from Him—"He, His knowledge, and His life are one." His knowledge is not something that happens to Him as a result of the object; rather, the object comes into existence *because* of His knowledge.

Thus, the fact that Hashem knows how a person will choose does not force the person to choose that way. On the contrary, the person’s choice is the very thing that Hashem knows. Hashem’s knowledge encompasses the future just as it encompasses the present, and He knows that the person will choose freely. The choice remains entirely in the hands of the person, and that choice is what is known to Hashem.

## כיסוי ראש

## Regarding the Custom of Wearing a Kippah Under the Hat

The custom of wearing a *kippah* under the hat is a practice that has been observed by many, and it is rooted in the principle of *yiras shamayim*. 

On the face of it, one might ask: if the head is already covered by a hat, why is there a need for an additional covering? Yet, in truth, this practice reflects a deeper *avodah*. Just as the *neshamah* is clothed in various levels—*nefesh, ru'ach,* and *neshamah*—so too, the external expression of one's *yirah* should be layered. The hat represents the outer covering, while the *kippah* serves as the immediate, constant reminder of the *Ribono shel Olam* who stands above one's head. 

By maintaining this double covering, a *Chassid* internalizes the awareness that even beneath the formal attire required for public conduct, there remains a private, constant state of *bitul* and *yirah* before Hashem. This is not merely a matter of custom, but a tangible expression of the *avodah* to ensure that one's *dveikus* is not only for the public eye, but is deeply rooted in one's personal, private reality.

## כשרות האכו&quot;ש

## The Kashrus of Non-Jewish Food



The Gemara states that the prohibition against eating food cooked by a non-Jew (*bishul akum*) was enacted for a specific reason: "lest he come to marry her." This is a decree of the Sages, intended to prevent social closeness that could lead to intermarriage.

However, we must understand why this specific prohibition—the cooking of food—is singled out as a cause for concern regarding intermarriage. After all, there are many other forms of social interaction that could potentially lead to such a result. Why is it specifically the act of cooking that the Sages identified as a catalyst for this danger?

The answer lies in the nature of the act of cooking. Cooking is not merely a technical preparation of food; it is a process that creates a bond between the one who prepares the food and the one who consumes it. When a person prepares a meal for another, they are investing their own effort and care into the sustenance of the other. This creates a sense of intimacy and familiarity. 

In the context of *avodah*, we see that the physical act of eating is a means of elevating the sparks of *Elokus* contained within the food. When a Jew eats, they are performing a *mitzvah*—provided the food is kosher and prepared in a manner consistent with the holiness of a Jewish home. When the cooking is done by a non-Jew, this process of elevation is compromised. The *bitul* and *kavanah* that should accompany the preparation of food are absent, and instead, the food becomes a vehicle for a connection that is outside the framework of *kedushah*.

Thus, the Sages decreed that the act of cooking by a non-Jew is forbidden, because it fosters a level of social closeness that bypasses the boundaries established by the Torah to maintain the distinct identity of the Jewish people. By restricting this, the Sages ensured that the home remains a place of *kedushah*, where the bonds formed are rooted in shared *avodah* and commitment to the *mitzvos*.

## עץ

## Tree

(For man is the tree of the field). Its purpose is to produce fruit. In the time to come, even barren trees will produce fruit, and that which the earth did wrong by producing barren trees will be rectified.


## עקב

## Eikev

(Eikev—"because"—as in, "Because [Eikev] Avraham listened to My voice." The Alter Rebbe’s adage is that this implies that even the "heels" [the lowest levels of the soul, or the final generations] also listened, etc.)

## פסח

## Pesach

 The law regarding the Chagigah offering brought with the Pesach sacrifice. Parameters of the mitzvah of reclining (heseibah). The recounting of the Exodus from Egypt as a fundamental principle and sturdy pillar, etc. Two aspects: Rosh Hashanah for the festivals, and the fact that the Festival of Pesach is always ongoing. Regarding the son’s question: "When your son asks you tomorrow, etc." In the Haggadah: "The Torah speaks regarding four sons, etc." On the night of the Seder: Performing various actions so that the children do not fall asleep, a lesson in chinuch.

See also: Matzah, Korban Pesach.

## פרנסה

## Parnassah

The blessing of Hashem is what brings wealth, yet one must still create a vessel. Parnassah in our current time is like the influence of the manna. One must exert the labor of one's hands, but not the labor of the mind and heart.

## צורה וחומר

## Form and Matter

Everything in the world is divided into three parts: the essence of the thing; the specific quality of the thing by which it is distinguished from others; and the remaining details of the thing that are not part of its essential quality. Form and matter were created through Will and Speech.


## קבלת עול

## Kabbalas Ol

 The virtue of avodah through Kabbalas Ol. Two levels: that which is below the kochos hanefesh, and that which is above the kochos hanefesh.


## קטן

## Katan

[mafteach_gopage file="ls31" gopage="121"] Certain matters are forbidden to a katan even before he reaches the age of chinuch, lest he come to be accustomed to them when he grows up. Even before reaching the age of chinuch, he is within the category of one who performs Torah and mitzvos. [mafteach_gopage file="ls31" gopage="43"]-[mafteach_gopage file="ls31" gopage="242"] To make their room a house of Torah, tefillah, and gemilas chasadim. Every person must have a Siddur, a Chumash, and a tzedakah box. [mafteach_gopage file="ls31" gopage="281"] They recognized Him first.

## רפואה

## Refuah

[ftnref_ls31_246] "For I am Hashem your healer" (*Ki Ani Hashem Rofecha*)—the initials spell *Arich*; for all healing comes from above. "I will not place upon you from the beginning, etc."

[ftnref_ls31_262] The use of medicines that prevent illness. Injections, even those derived from non-kosher substances. Scrupulousness regarding blood transfusions: who is the donor of the blood?

[ftnref_ls31_263] Regarding bloodletting. The negation of worry and melancholy (*marah shechorah*) in matters of health.

[ftnref_ls31_264] Permission has been granted to the physician to heal. One must strengthen hope and *bitachon*. One should keep a kosher mezuzah in one’s hospital room and undertake a commitment to *tzedakah*. Regarding a tonsillectomy.

[ftnref_ls31_265] Reciting the entire Tehillim on Shabbos before a surgery. The patient should not interfere in the physicians' decisions.

[ftnref_ls31_266] Strengthening *bitachon*.

[ftnref_ls31_267] Sometimes it is through a miracle. Permission has been granted to the physician to heal. "He who increases knowledge increases pain."

[ftnref_ls31_268] A story about an individual whom the Alter Rebbe healed with a piece of *matzah shemurah* and half a cup of water—clothed in the garments of nature. The incantation for one who has a bone stuck in their throat, etc., which they would teach even to the children of the *beis harav*.

[ftnref_ls31_70]-[ftnref_ls31_268] A *segulah* and remedy for eyesight: the study of *Pnimiyus HaTorah*, being careful with *Kiddush* and *Havdalah*, and participating in the synagogue candles.

## שבת

## Shabbos

[ftnref_ls31_87] The parsha of Shabbos was given to the Jewish people at Marah. [ftnref_ls31_91] On the reason it was given specifically to the Jewish people. [ftnref_ls31_99] On the opinion that Havdalah is a Torah obligation. Zachor and Shamor. [ftnref_ls31_100] The parameters of the positive mitzvah to "Remember the Shabbos day to sanctify it." [ftnref_ls31_101] Whether women are obligated in Havdalah. [ftnref_ls31_103] Performing Havdalah while it is still day. [ftnref_ls31_104] The purpose of Havdalah is to draw the holiness of Shabbos into the subsequent weekdays. Tosefes Shabbos. [ftnref_ls31_107] The laws of Shabbos are like "mountains hanging by a hair," etc. Rabbinic versus Torah prohibitions. [ftnref_ls31_108] The law regarding one who becomes ben hashmashos outside the techum, and the law regarding tying a knot of a hair, etc. [ftnref_ls31_109] Rabbinic versus Torah prohibitions. The laws of Shabbos are like mountains, etc. [ftnref_ls31_110] The prohibition of techumin has a basis in the Torah. [ftnref_ls31_111] Its purpose is the absence of transferring from one domain to another, and to reveal that the entire world is in the domain of the Ribono shel Olam. [ftnref_ls31_119] On the law of "that your manservant and the stranger may rest." [ftnref_ls31_123] On the law of the rest of an animal. [ftnref_ls31_191] Motzaei Shabbos: the departure of the neshamah yeseirah, and the scent of the besamim. [ftnref_ls31_192] Eating fish on Shabbos has no rationale in the revealed dimension of Torah. [ftnref_ls31_194] On Shabbos, the essence is rest; on Yom Tov, the essence is the cessation of melachah. The rest and oneg of Shabbos effect a change in the person, to the point that "a person’s facial radiance is not the same," etc., and also a change in the pulse of the hand, etc. [ftnref_ls31_198] When making Havdalah on Sunday, whether one can still recite the blessing over the besamim. When one does not have wine for Havdalah on Motzaei Shabbos, whether one must recite the blessing over the besamim. [ftnref_ls31_247] The order of the meals: the "Field of Holy Apples," the secret of Ze'er Anpin. Regarding the caution not to make Kiddush from the hour [ftnref_ls31_7]-[ftnref_ls31_6] on Erev Shabbos, and whether this is a concern in Eretz Yisrael. Whether eating on Shabbos is physical. [ftnref_ls31_248] The negation of a yahrtzeit fast on Shabbos. They were commanded at Marah. [ftnref_ls31_252] "If the Jewish people would keep [two Shabbosos]," etc., "the son of Dovid will come." [ftnref_ls31_257] An old, empty pot on the fire. [ftnref_ls31_59]-[ftnref_ls31_257] Regarding the melachah of sewing and the use of a safety pin. [ftnref_ls31_260] Regarding a "Shabbos clock." The negation of money associated with the desecration of Shabbos. [ftnref_ls31_261] Giving tzedakah in the amount of "Shabbos." [ftnref_ls31_284] Whether the melachah of kindling (hav'arah) was singled out to be a category or a negative prohibition.

See also: Yom Tov, Man, Shabbos candles.

## שינויים

## Changes

"I, Hashem, have not changed" (Malachi 3:6). The virtue of avodah that is without change—whether in Torah, in tefillah, or in mitzvos—is that the same point of bitul shines within it.

Avodah through kabbalas ol, which is far removed from changes.

## שלמה, רש&quot;י

## Shlomo, Rashi

[ftnref_ls31_28] The language of "his translation" in Rashi’s commentary on the Torah. [ftnref_ls31_34] Sometimes, the words within his commentary are part of the *dibbur hamaschil*. [ftnref_ls31_62] Even when he uses the language of Chazal, his intent is the simple meaning of that expression and phrase. [ftnref_ls31_80] His methodology regarding the repetition of terms in the songs found in Scripture. [ftnref_ls31_128] It requires further study whether the division of *parshiyos* in a Sefer Torah is relevant to the study of *peshuto shel mikra*. [ftnref_ls31_137] His purpose is not to copy and explain the words of the Targum. [ftnref_ls31_143] His method of citing the Midrashim that serve as the source for his words. [ftnref_ls31_165] Rashi writes many times that the verse is a *mikra katzar* (an abbreviated text). [ftnref_ls31_204] Sometimes, the words within his commentary are part of the *dibbur hamaschil*. [ftnref_ls31_215] Rashi’s language: "To fulfill that which is said, etc." Changes in the language of the verses between Hashem’s command to Moshe and Moshe’s words to the Bnei Yisrael.

## תורה

## Torah

[ftnref_ls31_7] The Torah preceded the world by two thousand years. In every word of the Torah, there is also an internal and spiritual interpretation that is entirely good. [ftnref_ls31_115] Every halacha in the Torah also has an interpretation in spiritual avodah. [ftnref_ls31_153] Above, it is one Torah in a state of totality, and afterwards, it divides into details.


## תורה שבכתב

## Regarding the study of Torah Shebichtav by heart

The requirement to study Torah Shebichtav by heart is a matter that requires clarification. Seemingly, the prohibition against reciting Torah Shebichtav by heart—as stated in the Gemara (Gittin 60b), "Words that were written, you are not permitted to say by heart"—would apply even when one is studying or reviewing it. 

Yet, we find that the halacha permits, and even encourages, the memorization of Torah Shebichtav. This is based on the principle that the prohibition is specifically regarding the act of reading from memory for the purpose of teaching or public recitation, whereas the internalizing of Torah through study is a fundamental aspect of *avodah*. 

When a person engages in the study of Torah Shebichtav by heart, they are not merely reciting words; they are internalizing the *Elokus* contained within the text. This process of *hisbonenus* allows the Torah to become part of the *neshamah* itself. Thus, the study of Torah Shebichtav by heart is not a violation of the prohibition, but rather a higher level of *dveikus*, where the *neshamah* and the Torah become one. 

Furthermore, this practice is essential for the *chossid* in their daily *avodah*. By carrying the words of Torah Shebichtav within their heart, the *chossid* ensures that their thoughts, speech, and actions are constantly aligned with the will of Hashem, reflecting the ultimate goal of *teshuvah* and the refinement of the world.

## The Great Orchard (Rashi)
